Agent's Attribution of Contracts: Acting Without Mentioning the Principal
Commentary on the agent's ability to attribute contracts to himself without mentioning the principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which contracts an agent may attribute to himself without mentioning the principal, and explain the legal consequences regarding rights and their transfer upon the agent's death.

Core Idea
An agent may conclude sale, lease, and sulh without naming the principal—rights attach to the agent, not the principal.

In Hanafi wakalah, an agent does not always need to reveal the principal. The law allows the agent to act as if the contract is his own for certain transactions. This lesson explains which contracts qualify, what rights the agent holds, and what happens when the agent dies. Understanding this rule is crucial for structuring agency relationships in commerce and dispute resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contracts the agent can attribute to himself
From the passage, list the specific contracts: sale (bay'), lease (ijarah), and sulh based on an acknowledgment (sulh 'an iqrar). The agent can say 'I sold' or 'I leased' without mentioning the principal.
These are contracts where personal trust or right is involved, unlike contracts requiring capacity on behalf of the principal.
2
Understand the legal effect of attribution
When the agent attributes the contract to himself, all rights and obligations under the contract pertain to the agent while he is alive, not to the principal. This includes delivery of the sold item and receipt of the price.
The principal is not a party to the contract; the agent is financially responsible.
3
Trace the transfer of rights upon the agent's death
If the agent dies, the rights transfer to his executor (wasi). If there is no executor, the judge appoints one to handle the rights. A minority view says they transfer to the principal, but the preferred view is that they go to the executor.
The passage ends mid-sentence cautioning: 'so one must be cautious when...' indicating the need for careful handling.

وحقوق عقد يضيفه الوكيل إلى نفسه كبيع، وإجارة، وصلح عن إقرار يتعلق به فيسلم المبيع ويقبضه [٢] وحقوق عقد يضيفه الوكيل إلى نفسه): أي لا يحتاجُ فيه إلى ذِكْرِ الموكل، فإنَّ في البيع والشراء عن الموكل يكفي أن يقول الوكيل : بعت، أو اشتريت، (كبيع، وإجارة وصلح عن إقرار يتعلق به " فيسلم المبيع) : أي في الوكالة بالبيع، ويقبضه) : أي في الوكالة بالشراء وكلام أبي حنيفة الله فيه أبين؛ لأنَّ التوكيل وقع هاهنا بدفع دعوى القصاص والحد، ودفعهما يثبت مع الشبهات، حتى يثبت العفو عن القصاص بالشهادة على الشهادة، وبشهادة النساء . مع الرجال، غير أنّ إقرار الوكيل لا يقبل عليه؛ لأنَّ فيه شبهةً عدم الأمر به، ولا يصح التوكيل بإثباتِ حد الزنا وشرب الخمر اتفاقاً. كذا في «كمال الدراية»(۱)، وغيره. (٢) قوله : أي لا يحتاج فيه...الخ؛ في هذا التفسير إشارة إلى أنَّ المراد كل عقد يصح إضافته إلى الوكيل نفسه لا يحتاج فيه إلى ذكر الموكل. (۳) قوله : يتعلق به؛ أي يتعلق العقد بنفس الوكيل دون الموكل ما دامَ حياً كما في (۲) نقلاً عن ابن ملك، فلا فرق بين موكله حاضراً أو غائباً، ولما في «البحر» نقلاً عن «الفتاوى الصغرى»: لا تنتقل الحقوق إلى الموكل فيما يضاف إلى الوكيل ما دام الوكيل حياً، وإن كان غائباً. و في «المحيط»: الوكيل بالبيع باع وغاب لا يكون للموكل قبض الثمن، فإذا مات الوكيل عن وصي قال الفَصْلِيُّ : تنتقل الحقوق إلى وصيه، وإن لم يكن وصي يُرْفَعُ إلى الحاكم فينصب وصيَّاً عند القبض، وقيل : تنتقل إلى موكله فيحتاط عند
And rights of a contract that the agent attributes to himself, like sale, lease, and sulh based on an acknowledgment that pertains to him, so the sold item is delivered and he takes possession. [2] 'And rights of a contract that the agent attributes to himself': meaning he does not need to mention the principal in it. For in sale and purchase on behalf of the principal, it is sufficient for the agent to say 'I sold' or 'I bought'. Like sale, lease, and sulh based on an acknowledgment that pertains to him, so the sold item is delivered: meaning in the agency for sale, 'and takes possession': meaning in the agency for purchase. And the statement of Abu Hanifa regarding it is clearer because the agency here is for repelling the claim of qisas and hadd, and repelling them is established despite shubah, such that pardon from qisas is established by testimony upon testimony and by women's testimony with men, except that the agent's confession is not accepted against him because it involves the shubha of lacking command. And it is not valid to appoint an agent for establishing the hadd of zina and drinking by consensus. As in Kamal al-Dirayah and others. [1] His statement 'attributed to himself': what is meant by attribution is the linguistic meaning, which is ascription, such as saying 'I sold', 'I leased', and 'I reconciled'. [2] His statement 'meaning he does not need...': in this interpretation, there is an indication that what is meant is every contract that is validly attributed to the agent himself does not require mention of the principal. [3] His statement 'pertains to him': meaning the contract pertains to the agent himself, not the principal, as long as the agent is alive, as quoted from Ibn Malik, so there is no difference whether the principal is present or absent. As in al-Bahr quoting from al-Fatawa al-Sughra: 'The rights do not transfer to the principal, regarding what is attributed to the agent, as long as the agent is alive, even if he is absent.' And in al-Muhit: 'The agent for sale sold and became absent; the principal does not have the right to take the price. If the agent dies and leaves an executor, al-Fasli said: the rights transfer to his executor. If there is no executor, the matter is referred to the judge who appoints an executor for taking possession. And it is said: they transfer to his principal, so one must be cautious when...'
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage explains a key rule in Hanafi wakalah: certain contracts can be attributed by the agent to himself without mentioning the principal. These contracts include sale (bay'), lease (ijarah), and sulh (reconciliation) based on an acknowledgment that pertains to the agent. The agent can say 'I sold' or 'I bought' and the contract is valid. The text then returns to the earlier discussion of hadd and qisas, noting that Abu Hanifa's view is clearer because the agency here is for repelling claims, which can be established despite shubah. However, the agent's own confession is not accepted against the principal due to the shubha of lack of authorization. The passage also discusses the transfer of rights: according to al-Fatawa al-Sughra, rights remain with the agent while he is alive; they do not transfer to the principal. Upon the agent's death, the rights go to his executor (wasi) if he has one, otherwise the judge appoints one. There is a difference of opinion: some say the rights transfer to the principal. The passage ends mid-sentence with a note to be cautious. This is a detailed legal commentary (expansion probe) on the matn phrase 'Haqooq aqd yudifuhu al-wakeelu ila nafsihi'.

Key Terms
إضافة (iḍāfah): Attribution or ascription of the contract to the agent himself (e.g., saying 'I sold').
صلح عن إقرار (ṣulḥ 'an iqrār): A reconciliation based on an admission (iqrar) by one party, which the agent conducts on behalf of the principal.
وكيل (wakīl): Agent or representative who acts on behalf of another (principal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to the rights of a contract that the agent attributed to himself after the agent dies?
A) They automatically transfer to the principal.
B) They transfer to the agent's executor (wasi).
C) They become null and void.
D) They remain with the agent's heirs directly.

Key Takeaway
An agent can validly conclude sale, lease, and sulh contracts in his own name, and the contractual rights remain with him until his death, after which they pass to his executor, not directly to the principal.

Buyer's Right to Withhold Payment from Principal
Ruling on buyer withholding payment from principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the buyer's payment to the principal, apply the exception when the buyer owes a debt to the principal, and explain the qiyas distinguishing sale from marriage in agency.

Core Idea
The buyer may pay the principal directly, but if the buyer owes a debt to the principal, he must pay the agent.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling in Hanafi sales law: when a seller appoints an agent, the buyer has the right to pay the principal directly. However, an exception arises if the buyer has a pre-existing debt to the principal. The commentary also draws a fascinating comparison between sale and marriage to justify why the agent can be treated as the principal in contractual rights. By the end, you will understand not only the ruling but the legal reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Locate the core statement: the buyer may withhold payment from the principal (the one who appointed the agent) and if he pays him, it is valid and the agent cannot demand payment again.
This is the main hukm: payment to the principal is sahih (valid) and discharges the buyer.
2
Note the Exception
Read the commentary's caveat: if the buyer has a debt on the principal, paying the principal does not discharge the buyer; he must pay the agent instead.
This is a key practical limitation.
3
Examine the Qiyas (Analogy)
The passage compares sale to marriage. In marriage, the effects (like prohibition of intercourse) cannot be transferred once consummated, so the agent cannot be treated as principal. In sale, ownership can be transferred, so the agent can act as principal in rights and then transfer them to the principal.
This is a qiyas khafi (subtle analogy) based on the nature of the subject matter.
4
Understand the Underlying Principle
The key difference is that the subject matter in sale (price and object) is created mubah (permissible) and can be owned by way of asalah (principality), whereas in marriage the rights are non-transferable after consummation.
This justifies why the agent can receive payment and then the right transfers to the principal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمشتري منعُ الثَّمن من موكل بائعه، فإذا دفع إليه صح، ولم يطالبه بائعه ثانياً [1] [2] [3] الوطء؛ ولهذا لا يظهر في حق الفسخ والتمليك من الغير، وفيما وراء الوطء فهو إسقاط جرياً على الأصل، إذ الحرمة تنافي الملك والساقط متلاش ومضمحل فلا يجوز أن يسقط في حق الوكيل، ثم يسقط ثانياً في حق الموكل بالانتقال؛ لأنَّ الساقط لا يعود إلا لسبب جديد، ولم يوجد. فكان حكم النكاح ثابتاً كمن أضيف إليه ابتداءً وهو الموكل بخلاف البيع، فإنّ حكمه يقبلُ الفصل عن السبب، كما أنه في البيع بشرط الخيار، فجاز أن يصدر السببُ عن شخص أصالة، ويقع الحكم لغيره خلافةٌ. فإنَّ المحل فيه خلق مباحاً وقابلاً للتملك بطريق الأصالة، وذلك الحكم مما يقبلُ ملك إلى ملك، فجاز أن يملكه شخص ثم ينتقل عنه إلى شخص، وجاز أن يصدر السبب من شخص ويقع الحكم لغيره. [1] قوله: وللمشتري منع ... الخ؛ يعني إذا وكُل رجلاً بيع شيء فباعه، ثمَّ إِنَّ الموكل طلب من المشتري الثمن له منعه؛ لأنَّ الموكَّل أجنبي عن العقد، والوكيل أصيل في الحقوق؛ ولذا له أن يوكل الآخر بهذه الحقوق، وإن لم يكن له حق التوكيل. [2] قوله: صح؛ لأنَّ الثمن المقبوض حق الموكل، وقد وصل إليه، ولا فائدة في الأخذ منه، ثم الدفع إليه. [3] قوله: ولم يطالبه بائعه ثانياً؛ لعدم الفائدة، نعم لو كان للمشتري على الموكل دين فلا تبرأ ذمته بدفع الثمن إليه، بل يجب عليه الدفع إلى الوكيل.
The buyer has the right to withhold the price from the principal of his seller; if he pays it to him, it is valid, and his seller does not demand it from him a second time. [1] [2] [3] Intercourse; hence it does not appear in the right of rescission or transfer to another, and beyond intercourse it is a dropping (of right) proceeding according to the principle, since prohibition contradicts ownership, and the dropped (right) is non-existent and annihilated, so it is not permissible that it be dropped in the right of the agent, then dropped a second time in the right of the principal by transfer; because the dropped cannot return except by a new cause, which does not exist. Thus the ruling of marriage is established as if it were attributed initially to the principal, unlike sale, for its ruling accepts separation from the cause, just as in sale with the condition of option, so it is possible that the cause issue from a person as principal and the ruling occur for another by deputyship. For the subject-matter therein is created permissible and capable of being owned by way of principality, and that ruling is of the type that is transferred from ownership to ownership, so it is permissible for a person to own it then it transfers from him to another, and it is permissible that the cause issue from a person and the ruling occur for another. [1] His statement: 'The buyer has the right to withhold...' means: If someone appoints an agent to sell something and he sells it, then the principal demands the price from the buyer, the buyer may withhold it because the principal is a stranger to the contract, and the agent is the principal in rights; thus he may delegate another in these rights even if he had no right of delegation. [2] His statement: 'it is valid' because the price received is the right of the principal and it has reached him, and there is no benefit in taking it and then giving it to him. [3] His statement: 'and his seller does not demand it from him a second time' due to lack of benefit; yes, if the buyer had a debt on the principal, his debt is not discharged by paying the price to him; rather he must pay to the agent.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation from a commentary on a matn. The ruling states that the buyer of an item sold by an agent may withhold payment from the principal (the one who appointed the agent) and may pay the principal directly, which is valid and discharges the buyer. The commentator explains that the principal is a stranger to the contract; the agent is the principal in rights. The passage then draws a distinction between marriage (nikah) and sale (bay') in the context of agency. In marriage, the prohibition of intercourse creates a right that cannot be transferred or rescinded after consummation; thus the effects of marriage are attributed to the principal as if he directly contracted. In sale, the ruling (i.e., ownership of the price and the object) can be separated from the cause (the contract), so it is permissible for the agent to act as principal in receiving rights and for those rights to transfer to the principal. The commentary gives practical consequences: if the buyer has a debt on the principal, paying the principal does not discharge the debt; he must pay the agent. The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah as this is from a standard commentary on Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
الموكل (al-muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent to act on his behalf.
الوكيل (al-wakīl): The agent who acts as principal in the rights arising from the contract.
الأصالة (al-aṣālah): Principality; acting as the primary party in a contract or right.
الخلافة (al-khilāfah): Deputyship; the principal receives the effects of the contract as a deputy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when does the buyer's payment to the principal NOT discharge the buyer?
A) When the buyer has a debt on the agent
B) When the buyer has a debt on the principal
C) When the price is less than the market value
D) When the principal is not present

Key Takeaway
The buyer may pay the principal directly and be discharged, except when the buyer owes a debt to the principal, in which case payment must be made to the agent.

Set-Off in Agency: The Default and Abu Yusuf's Dissent
Intra-Hanafi dispute on muqasah in wakalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on automatic set-off in agency and explain Abu Yusuf's dissenting position based on qiyas.

Core Idea
Set-off in agency occurs automatically, but Abu Yusuf disputes it when the agent's personal debt is involved.

When an agent and principal have mutual debts, does set-off happen automatically? The Hanafi school's default ruling says yes, but Imam Abu Yusuf offers a nuanced dissenting view. This lesson will guide you through the ruling, the dissent, and the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act
The act in question is muqasah (set-off) within a wakalah (agency) relationship. Determine what debts are being set off and between whom.
Focus on the phrase: 'If he has a debt against both of them...'
2
State the default Hanafi ruling
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, set-off occurs automatically by the mere contract of agency, but only for the principal's debt, not the agent's personal debt. If the agent uses the principal's property to pay his own debt, he becomes liable.
Note the distinction: the agent's personal debt is not set off; the agent is liable.
3
Identify Abu Yusuf's dissenting view
Abu Yusuf holds that set-off does not occur at all with the agent's personal debt. He supports this with qiyas to two scenarios: payment to an orphan and receipt in sarf.
His reasoning: in both cases the receiving party lacks capacity or the transaction is invalid.
4
Analyse the qiyas used by Abu Yusuf
First analogy: if someone buys from an orphan and pays the orphan directly, the payment is invalid because the orphan cannot receive; the buyer must pay the guardian. Second: in currency exchange (sarf), if the agent exchanges and the principal receives, the exchange is invalid because immediate possession is required.
Key point: in both, the set-off fails because the recipient is not competent or the contract is void.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The default position (set-off occurs) is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi). The practical difference: under Abu Yusuf, the agent remains liable and must pay separately; under the default, set-off extinguishes the agent's debt but he must compensate the principal.
The thamarah al-khilaf: whether the agent's debt is automatically discharged or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقعُ المقاصة بمجرد العقد بوصول الحقِّ إليه بطريق التقاص، ولو كان له دين عليهما تقعُ بدين الموكل دون دين الوكيل ، ولو كان له دين على الوكيل فقط وقعت المقاصة به، ويضمن الوكيل للموكل ؛ لأنه قضى دينه بمال الموكل.
وقال أبو يوسف : لا تقع المقاصة بدين الوكيل ، بخلاف ما إذا باع مال اليتيم ودفع المشتري الثمن إلى اليتيم حيث لا تبرأ ذمته بل يجب عليه أن يدفع الثمن إلى الوصي ؛ لأنَّ اليتيم ليس له قبض ماله أصلاً ، فلا يكون له الأخذ من المدين، فيكون الدفع إليه تضييعاً، فلا يعتد به، وبخلاف الوكيل في الصرف إذا صارَفَ وقبض الموكَّلُ بدل الصرف حيث يبطل الصرف ولا يعتد بقبضه. ذكره العَيْنِيُّ والزَّيْلَعِيُّ وغيرهما.
Set-off (muqasah) occurs by the mere contract, with the right reaching him through the method of set-off. If he has a debt against both of them, it occurs against the debt of the principal, not the debt of the agent. If he has a debt against the agent only, the set-off occurs with it, and the agent is liable to the principal, because he discharged his debt with the principal's property. Abu Yusuf said: 'Set-off does not occur with the agent's debt.' Contrary to the case of selling the property of an orphan where the purchaser pays the price to the orphan – his liability is not discharged; rather, he must pay the price to the guardian, because the orphan has no right to receive his property at all, so he cannot take from the debtor, so payment to him is wasteful and is not considered. Also contrary to the agent in currency exchange (sarf) if he exchanges and the principal receives the exchange substitute – the exchange is invalidated and his receipt is not considered. This was mentioned by al-'Ayni, al-Zayla'i, and others.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on set-off (muqasah) in the context of agency. The default position is that if the agent and the principal have mutual debts, set-off occurs automatically upon the contract of agency. For example, if the agent owes the principal a debt, and the principal owes the agent a debt under the agency, the debts are set off immediately. However, this set-off only applies to the principal's debt, not the agent's personal debt. If the agent uses the principal's property to pay off his own debt, he becomes liable to the principal. Abu Yusuf disagrees: he holds that set-off does not occur with the agent's personal debt at all. He analogizes this to two cases: (1) If someone buys property from an orphan and pays the orphan directly, the orphan cannot validly receive because he lacks capacity, so the payment is void and the buyer remains liable to the guardian. (2) In currency exchange (sarf), if the agent exchanges money and the principal receives the substitute, the exchange becomes invalid because the principal's receipt is not valid in that context. Thus, Abu Yusuf argues that set-off with the agent's debt is invalid because the agent's debt is not directly connected to the agency. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is the default view that set-off occurs automatically, but this text records the ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
مقاصة (muqāṣa): Set-off of mutual debts, where one debt extinguishes the other automatically upon the contract.
تقاص (taqāṣ): The process of setting off mutual debts; synonym of muqasah.
صرف (ṣarf): Currency exchange, a contract of exchanging gold and silver or currencies, requiring immediate delivery (qabd) on both sides.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf's dissenting view, why does set-off not occur with the agent's personal debt?
A) Because the agent's debt is not due yet.
B) Because the agent lacks capacity to receive.
C) Because it is analogous to paying an orphan directly or invalid receipt in sarf.
D) Because the principal did not authorize the set-off.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi agency, set-off occurs automatically only for the principal's debt; the agent's personal debt is not automatically set off, and Abu Yusuf's dissent reminds us that legal reasoning (qiyas) can lead to differing valid conclusions.

Three Types of Ignorance in Agency for Purchase
Explaining the ruling on ignorance in agency and its three categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given instruction in agency for purchase into gross, minor, or moderate ignorance and determine whether the agency is valid.

Core Idea
Ignorance invalidates agency only if it prevents compliance and cannot be resolved.

When you appoint an agent to buy something, how much detail must you give? Hanafi fiqh classifies ignorance (jihala) about the subject matter into three types. This lesson unpacks that classification, showing when the agency is valid despite uncertainty and when it is void. The key is resolvability: if the agent can figure out what the principal wants by looking at custom or by a minimal specification, the agency is valid.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the general principle
Understand that ignorance invalidates agency only if it prevents the agent from complying and cannot be resolved by referring to the principal's custom or by specifying price/description.
This principle applies to all three types.
2
Define gross ignorance (jihala fahisha)
Gross ignorance is ignorance of the genus (jins), e.g., 'buy a garment' without specifying type. It invalidates agency whether price is mentioned or not.
3
Define minor ignorance (jihala yasira)
Minor ignorance is ignorance within a pure type (naw' mahd), e.g., 'buy a horse'. It does not invalidate agency because the type is sufficient; the agent can follow the principal's custom.
If a commoner's agent buys a royal horse, it does not bind the principal.
4
Define moderate ignorance (jihala mutawassita)
Moderate ignorance lies between genus and type, e.g., 'buy a slave' without qualification. It invalidates agency unless the price or a description (e.g., Turkish) is specified.
Houses are treated similarly due to wide variation in amenities.
5
Apply the classification to examples
Practice by categorizing given instructions (e.g., 'buy a garment' = gross, 'buy a horse' = minor, 'buy a slave' = moderate) and state the ruling.
Remember: specifying price can upgrade moderate ignorance to minor.

باب الوكالة بالبيع والشراء فصل في الشراء (الأمر بشراء الطَّعام" على البر في دراهم كثيرة [١] قوله : باب...الخ ؛ أفرد الوكالة بالبيع والوكالة بالشراء بباب على حدة ؛ لكثرة الاحتياج إليها، وقدم هذا الباب على سائر أبواب الوكالة ؛ لأنه أكثر، وأمس حاجة ، وقدَّم من هذا الباب بحث الشراء ؛ لأنه ينبئ عن إثباتِ الملك، والبيع ينبئ عن إزالته ، والإزالة بعد الإثبات. والاصل في هذا الباب أنَّ الجهالة إذا كانت تمنع الامتثال، ولا يمكن دركها تمنعُ صحة الوكالة وإلا فلا ، والجهالة على ثلاثة أضرب : الأولى: جهالة فاحشة، وهي الجهالة في الجنس، فتمنع صحة الوكالة، سواء بين الثمن أو لا ، كما إذا وكله بشراء ثوب أو دابة أو نحو ذلك. والثانية : جهالة يسيرة، وهي ما كانت في النوع المحض، كما إذا وكله بشراء فرس أو حمار أو ثوب هروي، أو نحو ذلك ، فإنّه يجوز الوكالة به ، وإن لم يبين الثمن ؛ لأنَّ جهالة النوع لا تخل بالمقصود، ويمكن رفعها بصرف التوكيل إلى ما يليق بحال الموكل ، حتى إذا وكل عامي رجلا بشراء فرس فاشترى فرساً يصلح للملوك لا يلزمه. والثالثة : جهالة متوسطة ؛ وهي ما يكون بين الجنس والنوع ، كما إذا وكله بشراء عبد أو جارية إن بيَّن الثمن أو الصفة، بأن قال تركيّاً أو هندياً أو رومياً صحت الوكالة، وإن لم يبين الثمن أو الصفة لا يصح؛ لأنَّ اختلاف العبد والجواري أكثر من اختلاف سائر الأنواع ، وعادة الناس في ذلك مختلفة، فكانت بين الجنس والنوع. وكذا الدار الملحقة بالجنس من كل وجه ؛ لأنها تختلف بقلة المرافق وكثرتها ، فإن بين الثمن ألحقت بجهالة النوع ، وإن لم يبين ألحقت بجهالة الجنس، وصرح به القاضي خان في «شرح الجامع»
Chapter of Agency for Sale and Purchase. Section on Purchase. (The order to purchase food on credit for many dirhams). [1] He said: 'Chapter...etc.' He separated agency for sale and agency for purchase into a separate chapter due to frequent need for them. And he placed this chapter before the other chapters of agency because it is more common and more needed. And within this chapter, he presented the discussion of purchase first because it indicates the establishment of ownership, whereas sale indicates its removal, and removal comes after establishment. The basic principle in this chapter is that ignorance (jihala), if it prevents compliance and cannot be resolved, prevents the validity of the agency; otherwise, it does not. Ignorance is of three types: First: gross ignorance (jihala fahisha), which is ignorance in the genus (jins)—it prevents the validity of the agency whether the price is specified or not, such as if he appoints him to purchase a garment or an animal or the like. Second: minor ignorance (jihala yasira), which is ignorance in the pure type (naw' mahd)—such as if he appoints him to purchase a horse, a donkey, a Herawi garment, or the like—then the agency is valid even if he did not specify the price, because ignorance of type does not undermine the objective, and it can be resolved by directing the agency to what suits the principal's condition. For example, if a commoner appoints a man to purchase a horse and he purchases a horse suitable for kings, it is not binding on him. Third: moderate ignorance (jihala mutawassita), which is between genus and type—such as if he appoints him to purchase a male slave or a female slave: if he specifies the price or the description, like saying 'Turkish,' 'Indian,' or 'Roman,' the agency is valid; if he does not specify the price or description, it is not valid, because the variation in slaves is greater than variation in other types, and people's customs in that regard differ, so it is between genus and type. Similarly, a house is attached to the genus in every respect, because it varies in having few or many amenities; if he specifies the price, it is treated as ignorance of type; if he does not specify, it is treated as ignorance of genus. And Qadi Khan explicitly stated this in Sharh al-Jami'.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe commenting on the chapter heading and basic ruling of agency for purchase. It begins by explaining why the author (al-Hidayah) placed this chapter before others and prioritized purchase over sale: purchase establishes ownership, while sale removes it, and removal follows establishment. The core ruling is stated as a general principle: if ignorance (jihala) about the subject matter or price prevents the agent from being able to comply with the principal's instruction and cannot be resolved (i.e., by referring to the principal), then the agency is invalid; otherwise, it is valid. The passage then divides ignorance into three categories with increasing severity. First, gross ignorance (jihala fahisha) is ignorance of the genus (jins), such as 'buy a garment' without specifying what type—this invalidates agency regardless of whether the price is mentioned. Second, minor ignorance (jihala yasira) is ignorance of the pure type (naw' mahd), like 'buy a horse'—this is acceptable because the type is sufficient; any horse bought can be referred to the principal's customary expectations. However, if a commoner appoints an agent to buy a horse, and the agent buys a horse fit for kings, it does not bind the principal. Third, moderate ignorance (jihala mutawassita) lies between genus and type, exemplified by 'buy a slave' without specifying gender, price, or origin—this is invalid unless the price or one attribute (e.g., Turkish, Indian, Roman) is specified. The reason is that slaves vary greatly in quality and value based on origin and traits, and customs differ. A house is similarly treated: if price is specified, the ignorance is considered minor (type level) because the price brackets the quality; if price is omitted, it is gross ignorance because houses vary enormously in amenities. This classification is confirmed by Qadi Khan in his commentary on al-Jami'.

Key Terms
جهالة فاحشة (jihala fahisha): Gross ignorance: ignorance of the genus (jins) of the object to be purchased; invalidates the agency.
جهالة يسيرة (jihala yasira): Minor ignorance: ignorance within a pure type (naw' mahd); does not invalidate the agency.
جهالة متوسطة (jihala mutawassita): Moderate ignorance: ignorance between genus and type, e.g., 'slave' without specification; requires price or description for validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a principal tells an agent, 'Buy a garment,' without specifying type, color, or price, the agency is:
A) Valid, because the type is sufficient
B) Invalid, due to gross ignorance of genus
C) Valid, only if the price is specified
D) Invalid, only if the agent refuses to act

Key Takeaway
The validity of an agency for purchase depends on the severity of ignorance: gross ignorance invalidates, minor ignorance allows resolution by custom, and moderate ignorance requires specification of price or description.

Agency to Buy Food: Custom vs. Literal Meaning
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agency to buy food – custom vs. literal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on agency to buy food and identify how custom overrides literal meaning in this case, and contrast the qiyas and istihsan positions within the school.

Core Idea
In agency, custom ('urf) can override literal meaning (haqiqa) to resolve ambiguity, making a valid contract where strict analogy would void it.

This passage explores a key intra-Hanafi dispute: whether 'food' in a buying agency means all edibles (literal) or is restricted by local custom (wheat/flour/bread). The commentary walks through the three levels of ignorance (jahala) and then applies them to the specific case, showing how the Hanafi school balances textual analogy with practical custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core jahala framework
The passage first establishes three types of ignorance: gross (genus), slight (pure type), and intermediate (between genus and type). For 'food' to be void under qiyas, it must be gross ignorance.
Gross ignorance (jahala fahisha) invalidates agency because compliance is impossible.
2
Locate the qiyas position
The three disciples (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar) hold that 'food' literally means anything edible, making the agency void due to gross ignorance. This is based on analogy with oaths.
The literal meaning is consistent with how 'food' is interpreted in oath-breaking cases.
3
Understand the istihsan position
Earlier Hanafi mashayikh (e.g., Faqih Abu Ja'far) apply istihsan: custom ('urf) in Kufa restricts 'food' in commercial contexts to wheat, flour, or bread. The amount of money also determines which form (wheat for many, bread for few, flour for medium).
Custom overrides qiyas because it is stronger evidence in transactional contexts.
4
Evaluate the practical difference
Under qiyas, the agency is void and the agent's purchase does not bind the principal. Under istihsan, the agency is valid and the agent must buy according to custom based on the amount. The fatwa is on qiyas, but practice often follows istihsan to avoid hardship.
This is a thamarah al-khilaf: the same facts yield opposite rulings depending on the method.

Section on Buying

Chapter of Agency for Selling and Buying
Section on Buying
(The order to buy food) on wheat with many dirhams
[1] His statement: Chapter... etc.; He dedicated a separate chapter for agency for selling and buying due to the frequent need for it, and he placed this chapter before the other chapters of agency because it is more common and more needed. He presented the discussion of buying from this chapter because it indicates the establishment of ownership, while selling indicates its removal, and removal comes after establishment.
The principle in this chapter is that if the ignorance (jahl) prevents compliance and cannot be rectified, it prevents the validity of the agency; otherwise, it does not. Ignorance is of three types: First: Gross ignorance (jahala fahisha), which is ignorance of the genus, and it prevents the validity of the agency, whether the price is specified or not, such as when he appoints an agent to buy a garment or an animal or the like. Second: Slight ignorance (jahala yasira), which is ignorance of the pure type (al-naw' al-mahd), such as when he appoints an agent to buy a horse or a donkey or a Harawi garment or the like; then the agency is valid even if the price is not specified, because ignorance of the type does not harm the objective, and it can be removed by directing the agency to what is suitable for the principal's condition, so much so that if a common man appoints an agent to buy a horse and he buys a horse suitable for kings, it does not bind him.
Third: Intermediate ignorance (jahala mutawassita), which is between genus and type, such as when he appoints an agent to buy a slave or a slave-girl; if he specifies the price or the attribute, like saying Turkish, Indian, or Roman, the agency is valid; if he does not specify the price or attribute, it is not valid, because the differences among slaves are greater than the differences among other types, and people's customs in that regard vary, so it is between genus and type. Similarly, a house is attached to genus in every respect because houses differ in the number of facilities; if the price is specified, it is attached to ignorance of type, and if not, it is attached to ignorance of genus. Qadi Khan stated this in Sharh al-Jami'.
[2] His statement: The order to buy food... etc.; The form of the issue: If he appoints him to buy food,
(1) See: Fath al-Qadir (8:29).
and on bread with few [dirhams], and on flour with medium [dirhams], and in the case of a wedding feast, on bread in every situation.
and on bread with few [dirhams], and on flour with medium [dirhams], and in the case of a wedding feast, on bread in every situation; this agency ought to be void because the word food applies to everything that is eaten, so it is gross ignorance of genus. However, the customary meaning in his statement "buy me food" is that wheat, or flour, or bread is intended. And he said: Buy me food with these dirhams, and gave him the dirhams; then food here is interpreted as wheat if the dirhams are many, as bread if they are few, and as flour if they are medium. This is the statement of Faqih Abu Ja'far. Know that analogical reasoning (qiyas) would require that food be interpreted as every edible thing, considering the literal meaning, because food is a name for what is eaten, just as it is interpreted in the chapter of oaths. For if a person swears that he will not eat food and then eats fruit, he is judged to have broken his oath. Similarly, it should be interpreted here. The three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar) held this view, and the fatwa is upon it, as stated by al-Sadr al-Shahid, as clarified by al-'Ayni and others.
(1)
However, our mashayikh (senior scholars) considered that custom interprets food as wheat and its flour when food is mentioned in conjunction with buying and selling, and there is no custom regarding oaths of eating. So they ruled here that food should be interpreted as wheat and its flour by way of istihsan. It is said: This is according to the custom of the people of Kufa, for their market for wheat and flour is called the food market. As for the custom of others, it refers to buying every edible thing.
And some scholars of Transoxiana said: In our region's custom, food is what can be eaten without seasoning, like cooked meat, grilled meat, and the like; so the agency refers to that, not to wheat, flour, or bread.
And it was said: If there is a wedding feast, then food is interpreted as bread, whether the dirhams are few or many. Otherwise, it is interpreted as wheat if the dirhams are many, as bread if few, and as flour if medium. This is what the author (al-Musannif) chose, and there is no contradiction with what was mentioned earlier.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqaya, specifically on the chapter of agency for buying and selling, focusing on the section on buying. The commentator begins by explaining why the author dedicated a separate chapter for agency in buying and selling due to frequent need. He then presents the foundational principle (asl) regarding ignorance (jahala) in the object of agency. Ignorance is categorized into three types: gross ignorance (jahala fahisha) regarding the genus—this invalidates the agency; slight ignorance (jahala yasira) regarding the pure type—this does not invalidate the agency because it can be resolved by reference to the principal's status; and intermediate ignorance (jahala mutawassita) between genus and type—this requires specification of price or attribute, especially for slaves and houses. This categorization is from Qadi Khan's Sharh al-Jami'.

The passage then addresses the specific case: when a principal says, 'Buy food' and gives a certain amount of dirhams. The commentator states that according to analogical reasoning (qiyas), the agency would be void because 'food' applies to all edible things, causing gross ignorance of genus. However, customary usage ('urf) in Kufa specifies that 'food' in commercial contexts refers to wheat, flour, or bread. Therefore, the Hanafi mashayikh (senior scholars) applied istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas, ruling that the interpretation should follow custom. Accordingly, the agent must buy wheat if the dirhams are many, bread if few, and flour if medium; in the case of a wedding feast, always bread regardless of the amount. This is the statement of Faqih Abu Ja'far.

Nevertheless, the commentator notes that the three main disciples of Abu Hanifa (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Zufar) held to the qiyas position—that 'food' means all edible things—and the fatwa is upon this view according to al-Sadr al-Shahid and al-'Ayni. This creates an intra-Hanafi dispute: the earlier mashayikh (like Faqih Abu Ja'far) adopted istihsan based on custom, while the later position (the three and the fatwa) leans towards the literal interpretation in line with qiyas. The passage also records regional variations in custom: some scholars of Transoxiana define food as meat and the like, while others maintain the general rule. The commentator concludes by stating that the author of the matn (al-Musannif) chose the view that custom determines the interpretation based on the amount of money, except when a wedding feast is indicated, in which case bread is always intended.

Key Terms
جهالة فاحشة (jahala fāḥisha): Gross ignorance that prevents the validity of the agency; it is ignorance of the genus (jins) of the object, such as simply saying 'buy a garment' without any specification.
جهالة يسيرة (jahala yasīra): Slight ignorance that does not affect the validity of the agency; it is ignorance of the pure type (naw' mahd), such as saying 'buy a horse' without specifying breed, because the type can be resolved by considering the principal's status.
جهالة متوسطة (jahala mutawassiṭa): Intermediate ignorance between genus and type; it requires specification of price or attribute for the agency to be valid. Examples include buying a slave or a house without enough detail.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that deviates from strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of custom or public interest. Here, it is used to interpret 'food' according to custom rather than literal meaning.
عرف (‘urf): Custom or common practice that determines the meaning of ambiguous terms in contracts. In this passage, the Kufan custom specifies that 'food' in commercial contexts means wheat, flour, or bread.
طعام (ṭa‘ām): A word that literally means anything edible. In this passage, it is the subject of dispute: whether to interpret it literally (qiyas) or by custom (istihsan).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Custom ('urf) determines the meaning of ambiguous terms in commercial agency, overriding literal meaning when the custom is well-established, but the formal fatwa may still adhere to literal interpretation.

Agency Invalid Due to Gross Ignorance of Type
Clarifying when agency to buy is invalid due to gross type ignorance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on agency to purchase when there is gross ignorance of the type and distinguish when the agency is valid or invalid.

Core Idea
Gross ignorance of the type (jahālah fāḥishah) invalidates the agency even if the price is specified.

This passage from al-Wiqāyah and its commentary clarifies a key condition for a valid agency contract: the subject matter must be sufficiently specified. If the principal says 'buy a slave' or 'buy an animal' without narrowing it down to a type, the agency is invalid because the agent cannot comply. Even specifying the price does not remedy the gross ignorance. Only specifying a subtype, like 'donkey', makes it valid. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is appointing an agent to purchase a specific item. The ruling is that the agency is invalid (lā yaṣiḥḥ) if there is gross ignorance of the type of item.
The ruling applies even if the price is specified.
2
Understand the criteria for gross ignorance
Gross ignorance occurs when the item mentioned is a genus (jins) that includes multiple subtypes (anwāʿ) differing in purpose or reality, e.g., 'slave' includes male and female, 'garment' includes many kinds, 'animal' includes horses, donkeys, etc.
The text gives examples and explains that two things are of different types if their realities or purposes differ.
3
Trace the legal reasoning
The reason the agency is invalid is that the agent cannot comply with the principal's order because the type ambiguity makes it impossible to know the principal's intent. Therefore, if the agent purchases, it is for himself, not the principal.
This is based on the principle that contracts require certainty (gharar is minimized).
4
Apply the exception
If the principal mentions a specific subtype (naw') of the genus, such as 'donkey' for animals or 'Harawī' for garments, the ignorance becomes tolerable, and the agency is valid.
The commentary notes that the term 'type' here means the lowest genus (al-jins al-asfal), and the ruling relies on custom (ʿurf), which varies.

(۳) قوله : المراد.... الخ ؛ اعلم أنهم قالوا : إنَّ الجنس ما يدخل تحته أنواع شعائرية، والنوع اسم لأحد ما يدخل تحت اسم فوقه ، وقيل : الجنس اسم دال على كثيرين مختلفين بالشخص ، وقيل : كلُّ اسم منتظم لأشياء نوع باعتبار ما فوقه، وجنس باعتبار ما دونه، فاحتيج إلى بيان ما هو المراد هاهنا بالنوع ليتضح المقصود.
And it is not valid to buy something whose type ignorance is gross, like a slave, a garment, and an animal, even if its price is specified. Except if he mentions the type of animal, like a donkey. (And it is not valid to buy something whose type ignorance is gross, like a slave, a garment, and an animal, even if its price is specified. Know that any two things that share the same reality and purposes are of one type, and if the reality or purposes differ, they are of two types. If the ignorance of the type is gross, such as mentioning a type that has subtypes, like a slave, for it is divided into male and female, and they in humans are two types due to differing purposes. Then each of them may be desired for beauty as in the Turk, or for service as in the Indian. Similarly for the garment and animal. So the agency to buy these things is not valid even if the price is specified. Except if he mentions the type of animal, like a donkey. What is meant by 'type' here is the lower genus in the terminology of the jurists. The preferred of the opinions is that all of them rely on custom, and custom varies in every era. [1] His saying: 'And it is not valid... etc.' means that it is not valid to appoint an agent to buy something that includes genera, causing gross ignorance of its type, like a slave, garment, and animal, for in them there is gross ignorance. For the word 'dābbah' linguistically means anything that creeps on the earth, and customarily it refers to horses, mules, and donkeys, thus it combines genera. Similarly, garment includes clothing from atlas to coarse wool. Similarly, slave includes male and female who are different in humans. The reason for the invalidity of appointment is apparent if he does not specify the price, and likewise if he does, because compliance with the principal's order becomes impossible because by that price one can find from each genus, and the intent of the command is not known due to gross ignorance. If the agent purchases in these cases, the purchase is for the agent, not the principal. This is explicitly stated in 'al-Nihāyah' and others. [2] His saying: 'Except if he mentions... etc.' means if he mentions the type of animal like a donkey, then the appointment is valid. Similarly, if he mentions the type of garment like a Harawī, for example. The secret is that such ignorance does not detract from the objective, and it can be resolved by directing the appointment to what suits the principal's condition as previously stated. [3] His saying: 'What is meant... etc.' Know that they said: 'Genus (jins) is that under which there are species (anwā'), and species (naw') is a name for one of the things that fall under a name above it. It was said: Genus is a name that indicates many different individuals. And it was said: Every name that encompasses things is a species with respect to what is above it, and a genus with respect to what is below it. Therefore, it became necessary to clarify what is meant here by 'species' to make the purpose clear.)
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqāyah regarding agency for purchase. The author begins by stating the general rule: an agency to buy an item is invalid if there is gross ignorance (jahālah fāhishah) of its type (jins). Examples include a slave (since it includes males and females of different types), a garment (since it includes many varieties), and an animal (since it includes horses, mules, donkeys, etc.). The ruling remains even if the price is specified because specifying the price does not remove the ambiguity of which specific item is intended. The reason is that with that price, multiple subtypes can be found, and the agent cannot determine the principal's intent. Therefore, if the agent does purchase, it is for himself, not the principal. An exception is made if the principal specifies a particular subtype (naw'), such as 'donkey' for animals or 'Harawī' for garments. This reduces the ignorance to a tolerable level. The passage then explains the terminology: 'type' (naw') in this context means the lowest genus (al-jins al-asfal), and the preferred view is that it relies on custom, which varies. The commentary elaborates each phrase of the matn and clarifies the reasoning.

Key Terms
الجهالة الفاحشة (al-jahālah al-fāḥishah): Ignorance of the type (jins) that prevents the agent from knowing the principal's intended item, making compliance impossible.
الجنس (al-jins): A category that includes multiple subtypes (anwāʿ) that differ in purpose or reality, e.g., slave includes male/female.
النوع (al-nawʿ): A specific subtype within a genus, such as 'donkey' within 'animal'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, when does an agent's purchase become valid for himself rather than for the principal?
A) When the principal specifies the price only.
B) When the agent buys an item of the same general type as requested.
C) When the principal mentions a specific subtype like 'donkey'.
D) When the principal says 'buy an animal' without further specification.

Key Takeaway
The validity of an agency to purchase depends on sufficient specification of the subject matter; gross ignorance of the type invalidates the agency even if the price is known.

Agency to Buy a House: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Specifying Locality
Presenting different Hanafi positions on specifying locality in agency to buy a house

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four Hanafi positions on agency to buy a house and explain why the later scholars' view requiring locality is muftā bihī.

Core Idea
For agency to buy a house, later Hanafi scholars require specifying locality because houses differ by location—a ruling rooted in changing custom.

When you appoint someone to buy a house for you, must you specify the neighborhood? Hanafi scholars differed. This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah presents four positions—from accepting price alone to requiring locality. The later fatwa settled on requiring locality, reflecting how custom shapes fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal issue
Determine what act is being discussed (agency to buy a house) and identify the condition that is disputed: must the locality (maḥallah) be specified for the agency to be valid?
The core question is about the level of specification required.
2
List the four positions from the passage
Extract each position: (1) al-Kanz: price suffices; (2) al-Hidāyah: price + genus description; (3) al-Iṣlāḥ: middle ground; (4) later scholars (al-Nihāyah): locality required.
Note the sources: Kanz, Hidāyah, Iṣlāḥ, Nihāyah.
3
Understand the reasoning on jahālah
Each position categorizes the ignorance (jahālah) about the house as intermediate (mutawassiṭah) or gross (fāḥishah). Price may resolve intermediate ignorance; locality may be needed to overcome gross ignorance.
This is the key legal mechanism: jahālah levels determine validity.
4
Trace the evolution and tarjih
Note that later scholars (mutāʾakhkhirūn) gave muftā bihī status to requiring locality because customs changed—houses now vary greatly by locality.
This shows the dynamic nature of fiqh: rulings can shift with ʿurf.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand the practical consequence: if locality is not specified, the agency is invalid and the agent's purchase is for himself (unless ratified by the principal).
This is the real-world impact of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو ثمن الدار والمحلة)؛ يعني إن وكل بشراء دارٍ بَيَّنَ ثمنها ومحلّتها، فالتوكيل جائز، واختلفوا في هذا المحل:
(1) وقد جعل صاحب «الكنز») الدار كالعبد ، حيث قال : وبشراء عبد أو دار صح إن سمى ثمنا. انتهى. وقال الزَّيْلَعِي في «شرحه» على «الكنز» (۲) : لأنَّ هذه جهالة متوسطة بين الجنس والنوع ، وليست بفاحشة ولا يسيرة، فإذا بَيَّنَ ثمنه عُلِمَ من أي نوع مقصوده. انتهى.
٢. وجعلها صاحب «الهداية» (۳) كالثوب حيث قال : وكذا الدَّارُ تشمل ما هو في معنى الأجناس ؛ لأنها تختلف اختلافاً فاحشاً، باختلاف الأغراض والجيران، والمرافق ، والمحال، والبلدان، فيتعذر الامتثال ، وإن سمّي ثمن الدار ووصف جنس الدار جاز معناه نَوْعُه. انتهى. وقال في «الإصلاح) () : الدارُ ملحقةٌ بالجنس من وجه؛ لأنها مختلفة ؛ لقلة المرافق وكثرتها ، فإن بين الثمن ألحقت بجهالة النوع، وإن لم يبين ألحقت بجهالة الجنس، والمتأخرون قالوا في ديارنا : لا يجوز بدون بيان المحلة ؛ لأنها تختلف باختلافها. انتهى. وقال في «النهاية»: والمتأخّرون من مشايخنا يقولون في ديارنا : لا يجوز إلا ببيان المحلة. انتهى. وقال في «البحر»): وفي «المعراج»: إنَّ ما في «الهداية»(٦) مخالف لما في الرواية
Or the price of the house and its locality. That is, if he appoints an agent to buy a house and specifies its price and its locality, then the appointment is valid. And they differed regarding this: (1) The author of al-Kanz made the house like a slave, saying: 'Appointment to buy a slave or a house is valid if he names a price.' End quote. And al-Zaylaʿī in his commentary on al-Kanz said: 'Because this ignorance is intermediate between genus and species, neither gross nor slight. So if he specifies its price, it becomes known from which type his intent is.' End quote. (2) The author of al-Hidāyah made it like a garment, saying: 'Similarly, the house includes what is in the meaning of genera, because they differ excessively due to differences in purposes, neighbors, facilities, localities, and countries, so compliance becomes impossible. And if the price of the house is named and the genus of the house is described, it is valid – meaning its species.' End quote. And in al-Iṣlāḥ it says: 'The house is attached to genus from one aspect, because it differs due to few or many facilities. If the price is specified, it is attached to ignorance of species; if not, it is attached to ignorance of genus. The later scholars said in our lands: It is not valid without specifying the locality because houses differ with different localities.' End quote. And in al-Nihāyah: 'The later scholars among our mashāyikh say in our lands: it is not valid except with specification of the locality.' End quote. And in al-Baḥr: 'In al-Miʿrāj, it is said that what is in al-Hidāyah contradicts the transmitted report.'
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents a debate among Hanafi scholars regarding the validity of agency to purchase a house without specifying its locality. The author of al-Kanz treats the house like a slave, considering the specifying of price sufficient, as the ignorance is intermediate (mutawassiṭah). The author of al-Hidāyah, however, treats the house like a garment, arguing that houses vary widely in purposes, neighbors, facilities, and locations, making compliance impossible if only price is given. Therefore, he requires describing the 'genus' of the house, i.e., its type. Al-Iṣlāḥ offers a middle position: if price is mentioned, it is attached to ignorance of species (tolerable); if not, it is gross ignorance. The later scholars (mutāʾakhkhirūn) in the author's land ('diyārinā') require specifying the locality because houses differ greatly by location. Al-Nihāyah confirms this fatwa. Finally, al-Baḥr notes a contradiction between al-Hidāyah and the transmitted ruling (riwāyah). This shows the evolution of the fatwa based on custom (ʿurf).

Key Terms
المحلة (al-maḥallah): The neighborhood, district, or locality where the house is situated.
جهالة متوسطة (jahālah mutawassiṭah): Ignorance that is neither gross (fāḥishah) nor slight (yasīrah), but intermediate, which can be remedied by specifying the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later scholars (mutāʾakhkhirūn) cited in al-Nihāyah, when is agency to buy a house valid?
A) Only if the price is specified.
B) Only if the locality is specified.
C) Only if both price and locality are specified.
D) Only if the type of house (e.g., villa) is specified.

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi fatwa requires specifying the locality when appointing an agent to buy a house, reflecting the influence of custom on legal rulings.

Specifying Price and Locality for House Purchase Agency
Intra-Hanafi dispute on house purchase agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on jahalah (ignorance) to determine the required specifications (price, locality, type) when appointing an agent to buy a house, and explain the intra-madhhab dispute between early and later Hanafi scholars.

Core Idea
For houses, the required specifications depend on how much they vary; later scholars require locality, early scholars only price.

The validity of appointing an agent to buy a house depends on what information the principal provides to avoid excessive ignorance (jahalah fahishah). This lesson explores a classic Hanafi dispute: is specifying the price enough, or must the locality also be given? We will trace the reasoning of early scholars (like Al-Kanz) who analogize the house to a slave, versus later scholars who analogize it to a garment, and see how the author of this text reconciles and adopts the later view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What must be specified when appointing an agent to buy a house, and why is this a matter of dispute?
Focus on the concept of jahalah (ignorance) – moderate vs. excessive.
2
Analyze the early scholars’ view (Al-Kanz)
Understand why they considered the house like a slave: specifying the price reveals the type, making ignorance moderate and tolerable.
Note their analogy: slave differs by type (e.g., Turkish), known by price alone.
3
Analyze the later scholars’ view (Al-Hidayah & others)
Understand why they considered the house like a garment: houses vary excessively by amenities, neighbors, location, etc., so without locality, ignorance is excessive and invalidates the agency.
Note the difference: garments require specifications like length, color; houses require locality.
4
Examine the reconciliation offered in Al-Bahr
See how the two views are reconciled based on actual variation: in areas where houses differ greatly, the later view applies; where variation is small, the early view suffices.
The author (Umdat al-Ri'ayah) adopts the later view as the operative position (mufta bihi).
5
Apply the principle to other items (slave, sheep/cow)
Use the same jahalah grading to determine required specifications: for sheep/cow (single genus), price alone suffices; for slave, price and type suffice.
This shows the school’s systematic approach: the needed detail depends on how much the item can vary.

Source Text

(2) وجعلها صاحب «الهداية» كالثوب حيث قال: وكذا الدَّارُ تشمل ما هو في معنى الأجناس؛ لأنها تختلف اختلافاً فاحشاً، باختلاف الأغراض والجيران، والمرافق، والمحال، والبلدان، فيتعذر الامتثال، وإن سمّي ثمن الدار ووصف جنس الدار جاز معناه نَوْعُه. انتهى. وقال في «الإصلاح»: الدارُ ملحقةٌ بالجنس من وجه؛ لأنها مختلفة؛ لقلة المرافق وكثرتها، فإن بين الثمن ألحقت بجهالة النوع، وإن لم يبين ألحقت بجهالة الجنس، والمتأخرون قالوا في ديارنا: لا يجوز بدون بيان المحلة؛ لأنها تختلف باختلافها. انتهى. وقال في «النهاية»: والمتأخّرون من مشايخنا يقولون في ديارنا: لا يجوز إلا ببيان المحلة. انتهى. وقال في «البحر»: وفي «المعراج»: إنَّ ما في «الهداية» مخالف لما في الرواية «المبسوط» قال: والمتأخرون من مشايخنا قالوا في ديارنا لا يجوز إلا ببيان المحلة. انتهى. وبه يحصل التوفيق، فيحملُ ما في «الهداية» على ما إذا كانت تختلف في تلك الديار اختلافاً فاحشاً، وكلام غيره على ما كانت لا تتفاحش. انتهى ما في «البحر»، والمصنف له اختار فعل المتأخرين في الدار، فقال: أو بين ثمن الدار والمحلة، والحاصل أنَّ جهالة الدار جهالة الجنس عند المتأخرين، وجهالة النوع عند المتقدمين، فتحمل عبارة كل من «الكنز» و«الهداية» على كل من المذهبين. [١] قوله: الدار مما ... الخ؛ فإنَّ الدار تختلف بالطول والعرض في السكة والبيوت والمرافق وقرب الماء وبعده، والجيران وغيرها فكانت بمنزلة ما يشمل أجناساً، وتختلف المحلة، فلا بُدَّ من بيان الثمن والمحلة معاً. [٢] قوله: وتصح بشراء عُلِمَ جنسُهُ لا صفتُهُ كالشَّاةِ والبقر؛ يعني إن وكله بشراء شيء علم جنسه ولم يعلم صفته، كالشاة والبقر، فصح التوكيل؛ لأنَّ الشاة والبقر جنس واحد؛ لاتحاد المقصود والشفعة، فلا يحتاج في صحة التوكيل إلى بيان الصفة كالسمن والهزال ونحو ذلك. [٣] قوله: وتصح بشراء شيء جُهِلَ جِنْسُهُ من وجه كالعبد، وذُكِرَ نوعُهُ كالتركي، أو ثَمَنٌ عَيْنَ نوعاً.
Or the price of the house and its locality: meaning if he appoints an agent to buy a house and specifies its price and its locality, then the agency is valid. And they differed regarding this ruling: (1) The author of Al-Kanz made the house like a slave, saying: and for buying a slave or a house, it is valid if he mentions the price. Ended. And Al-Zayla'i said in his commentary on Al-Kanz: because this is a moderate ignorance between genus and species, neither excessive nor slight, so if he specifies its price, it is known which type he intends. Ended. (2) The author of Al-Hidayah made it like a garment, saying: and likewise the house includes what is in the meaning of genera because it differs excessively according to differences in purposes, neighbors, amenities, locations, and cities, so compliance becomes impossible, and if the price of the house is mentioned and the genus of the house is described, it is valid meaning its type. Ended. And it is said in Al-Islah: The house is attached to genus in one respect because it differs due to few or many amenities; if the price is mentioned it is attached to ignorance of species, and if not mentioned it is attached to ignorance of genus. And the later scholars said in our lands: it is not valid without specifying the locality because houses differ by locality. Ended. And it is said in Al-Nihaya: The later scholars among our teachers say in our lands: it is not valid except with specifying the locality. Ended. And it is said in Al-Bahr: And in Al-Mi'raj: What is in Al-Hidayah contradicts what is in the narration of Al-Mabsut: the later scholars among our teachers said in our lands it is not valid except with specifying the locality. Ended. And by this reconciliation is achieved: what is in Al-Hidayah is interpreted as when houses differ greatly in those lands, and the words of others are interpreted as when they do not differ greatly. Ended what is in Al-Bahr. And the author (of this book) chose the action of the later scholars regarding the house, so he said: or specify the price of the house and its locality. And the summary: ignorance of the house is ignorance of genus according to the later scholars, and ignorance of species according to the early scholars, so the expression of both Al-Kanz and Al-Hidayah is interpreted according to each of the two schools. [1] His words: The house is among that which... etc.; indeed the house varies in length and width in streets, rooms, amenities, proximity to water, neighbors, etc., so it is like what includes genera, and the locality differs, so specifying both price and locality is necessary. [2] His words: And it is valid to buy something whose genus is known but not its description, like a sheep or cow; meaning if he appoints him to buy something whose genus is known but description unknown, like sheep and cows, then the agency is valid because sheep and cows are one genus, due to unity of purpose and [benefit], so there is no need in validity of agency to specify the description like fatness or thinness etc. [3] His words: And it is valid to buy something whose genus is unknown in one respect, like a slave, and its type is mentioned like Turkish, or a price that specifies a type.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a ruling from the base text on agency for purchase. It discusses the required specifications when appointing an agent to buy a house. The base text states that the agency is valid if the price and locality (محلة) are specified. The commentator explains that there is a dispute among Hanafi scholars. The early scholars (متقدمون) like the author of Al-Kanz considered the house analogous to a slave, where specifying the price alone suffices because the ignorance is moderate. The later scholars (متأخرون) considered the house analogous to a garment, where the differences are excessive, so specifying the price alone is insufficient; the locality must be mentioned. The commentator reconciles these views by noting that the ruling depends on the extent of variation in houses in a particular area. The author (of this book) adopts the later scholars' view, requiring both price and locality. The passage also explains that for items like sheep and cows (one genus, unified purpose), only the price needs to be specified. For a slave, specifying the type (e.g., Turkish) and price is sufficient. The commentary uses the principle of jahalah (ignorance) graded as moderate or excessive to determine the required specifications.

Key Terms
جهالة (jahālah): Ignorance or uncertainty regarding the subject matter of a contract; graded as moderate (mutawassiṭa) or excessive (fāḥisha) affecting validity.
محلة (maḥallah): Locality or neighborhood; the specific area where the house is located, considered a necessary specification for agency in purchasing a house according to later Hanafi scholars.
نوع (nawʿ): Species; a subdivision of a genus (jins). In this context, it refers to the specific type (e.g., Turkish slave) that must be specified when the genus is partially unknown.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi scholars (متأخرون), what must an agent specify when appointed to buy a house?
A) Only the price
B) Only the locality
C) Both the price and the locality
D) Neither the price nor the locality

Key Takeaway
In areas with significant housing variation, both price and locality must be specified for the agency to be valid according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Agency to Buy Known Genus, Unknown Description
Ruling on validity of agency when genus known but description unknown

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on agency when the genus is known but description unknown, and explain the legal basis for its validity.

Core Idea
If the principal specifies the genus, the agent's purchase is valid even without describing physical attributes.

In wakalah (agency), the principal must specify what the agent can buy to avoid uncertainty. This lesson clarifies that specification of the genus alone suffices if the genus items are uniform in purpose and benefit. The text from the matn and its sharh demonstrate this principle with the example of livestock.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling
Read the matn: 'وتصح بشراء عُلِمَ جنسُهُ لا صفتُهُ'. The agency contract is valid (sahih) when the genus is known but the description is unknown.
2
Examine the Example
The matn gives the example of sheep and cow (الشاةِ والبقر). The sharh explains that these are a single genus because of unified purpose and benefit.
The term 'جنس' refers to a category where items share essential traits.
3
Analyze the Legal Basis
The sharh says: 'لاتحاد المقصودِ والمنفعة' (due to unity of purpose and benefit). Thus, there is no need to state the description like fatness or thinness.
Description (صفة) such as fat or thin is irrelevant within a uniform genus.
4
Derive the Condition for Validity
The condition is that the genus must be known. If the genus is unknown (e.g., 'buy an animal'), the agency would be invalid due to uncertainty.
This ruling is specific to cases where the genus is both known and uniform in purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتصح بشراء عُلِمَ جنسُهُ لا صفتُهُ كالشَّاةِ والبقر. قوله : وتصح بشراء عُلِمَ جنسُهُ لا صفتُهُ كالشَّاةِ والبقر)، فإنهما جنس واحدٌ لاتحاد المقصودِ والمنفعة ، فلا احتياج إلى بيانِ الصَّفَةِ كالسُّمْنِ والهزال
And the agency is valid for buying something whose genus is known but not its description, such as a sheep or cow. He said: 'And it is valid for buying something whose genus is known but not its description, such as a sheep or cow,' for they are a single genus due to unity of purpose and benefit, so there is no need to state the description like fatness or thinness.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This is a ruling from the matn on agency. The ruling: if a principal authorizes an agent to purchase an item whose genus is known (e.g., sheep, cow) but the description (e.g., fat or thin) is unspecified, the agency contract is valid. The reason is that within a single genus like sheep or cattle, the essential purpose and benefit are unified, so specifying further qualities like fatness is unnecessary. The commentator (sharh) confirms this by saying they are a single genus and there is no need to mention the description.

Key Terms
وكالة (wakālah): A contract where one person appoints another to act on their behalf in a legal transaction.
جنس (jins): A category of things sharing a common purpose and benefit, e.g., sheep and cattle as livestock.
صفة (ṣifah): A specific attribute or quality of an item, such as fatness or thinness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A principal authorizes an agent to 'buy a cow.' The agent buys a cow without specifying whether it is fat or thin. Is the agency valid?
A) Yes, because the genus (cow) is known.
B) No, because the description was not specified.
C) Yes, but only if the agent buys a fat cow.
D) No, because cows have different breeds.

Key Takeaway
An agency to buy an item is valid if the genus is known, even if its specific description (like fat or thin) is unspecified, because within a uniform genus the purpose and benefit are consistent.

Agency to Buy a House: Specifying Price and Locality
Ruling on agency to buy a house requiring price and locality with intra-madhhab reconciliation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on agency to buy a house, explain why both price and locality are required, and distinguish between the earlier and later scholarly positions.

Core Idea
A house requires both price and locality in an agency contract because its variation is gross ignorance of genus.

In this lesson, we examine a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the validity of an agency contract to purchase a house. The matn states that because a house's variation is excessive (fāḥish), the principal must specify both the price and the locality. The commentary reconciles earlier and later opinions, and the author adopts the later view. Understanding this shows how Hanafi scholars define excessive ignorance (jahālah fāḥishah) in real estate transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an agency (wakālah) to buy a house. According to the matn, the agency is valid only if both the price and the locality are specified. If either is omitted, the contract is invalid due to gross ignorance (jahālah fāḥishah).
This ruling applies when houses in that locality vary significantly.
2
Understand why the house triggers gross ignorance
The commentary explains that a house differs in length, width, alleys, rooms, facilities, proximity to water, neighbours, etc. Thus, it is like something that includes multiple genera, making ignorance of it gross ignorance of genus, not just type.
Compare with other commodities where only price may suffice.
3
Locate the intra-madhhab dispute
Earlier Hanafi scholars considered ignorance of the house as ignorance of type (jins), requiring only price. Later scholars treat it as ignorance of genus (jins), requiring both price and locality. The author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah opts for the later view.
This dispute is reconciled by differing customs: in lands where houses vary extremely, the later view applies; otherwise, the earlier view may be followed.
4
Determine the muftā bihī position
The muftā bihī (operative for fatwa) is the later view: both price and locality must be specified. This is the relied-upon position in the text.
This reflects the practice of the later scholars (mutā'akhkhirīn).
5
Apply to a concrete scenario
Test: Principal tells agent, 'Buy a house in this city for 1000 dinars' without mentioning the locality. According to the muftā bihī, the agency is void because the locality is unspecified, leading to gross ignorance.
If the principal specifies both price and locality, the agency is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدار ما فحش جهالة جنسه فلا بُدَّ من أن يبيِّن ثمنها ومحلَّتها. قوله : الدار مما ... الخ ; فإنَّ الدار تختلف بالطول والعرض في السكة والبيوت والمرافق وقرب الماء وبعده ، والجيران وغيرها فكانت بمنزلة ما يشمل أجناساً، وتختلف المحلة ، فلا بُدَّ من بيان الثمن والمحلة معاً. انتهى. «المبسوط»(۱) قال : والمتأخرون من مشايخنا قالوا في ديارنا لا يجوز إلا ببيان ) وبه يحصل التوفيق، فيحملُ ما في «الهداية» (۲) على ما إذا كانت تختلف في تلك الديار اختلافاً فاحشاً، وكلام غيره على ما كانت لا تتفاحش. انتهى ما في «البحر»(۳)، والمصنف الله له اختار فعل المتأخرين في الدار ، فقال : أو بين ثمن الدار والمحلة، والحاصل أنَّ جهالة الدار جهالة الجنس عند المتأخرين، وجهالة النوع عند المتقدمين، فتحمل عبارة كل من «الكنز»(4) و«الهداية»(9) على كل من المذهبين.
The house is something whose genus ignorance is gross, so he must specify its price and its locality. He said: 'The house is from...' etc.; for the house differs in length and width, in alleys, rooms, facilities, proximity to water and distance, neighbors, etc., so it is like something that includes genera, and the locality differs, so it is necessary to specify both the price and the locality. End. Al-Mabsut said: The later scholars among our mashayikh said in our lands it is not permissible except with specification, and by this reconciliation is achieved. So what is in Al-Hidayah is interpreted as the case when houses differ extremely in those lands, and the statement of others is for when they do not differ extremely. End of what is in Al-Bahr. And the author opted for the practice of the later scholars regarding the house, so he said: 'or specify the price of the house and the locality.' The conclusion is that ignorance of the house is ignorance of genus according to the later scholars, and ignorance of type according to the earlier scholars, so the expressions of both Al-Kanz and Al-Hidayah are interpreted according to each of the two madhhabs.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage addresses agency to buy a house. The matn states that since a house involves gross ignorance of genus (because houses vary greatly in size, location, facilities, etc.), the principal must specify both the price and the locality. The sharh explains that a house encompasses many aspects (dimensions, alley, rooms, proximity to water, neighbors), making it akin to multiple genera. Therefore, mentioning both price and locality is essential to reduce uncertainty. Later, a discussion from al-Mabsut reconciles early and later Hanafi scholars: early scholars treated house ignorance as unknown type (requiring less specification), while later scholars treat it as unknown genus (requiring price and locality). The author (of this sharh) chooses the later view, so the ruling is that both price and locality must be stated.

Key Terms
جهالة فاحشة (jahālah fāḥishah): Gross ignorance that invalidates a contract due to excessive uncertainty.
محلة (maḥallah): The locality or neighborhood of the house.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A principal tells an agent: 'Buy a house in this city for 1000 dinars' but does not specify the locality. According to the later (mufta bihi) position, is the agency valid?
A) No, because both price and locality must be specified.
B) Yes, because the price suffices.
C) Yes, because the city is specified.
D) No, because the price must be specific to the locality.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an agency to buy a house requires both price and locality; otherwise, the contract is void due to excessive ignorance.
