Agency to Buy a Partially Unknown Genus: The Slave Example
Ruling on agency when genus is partially unknown, like a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on agency when the genus is partially unknown, identifying when specification of type or price renders the agency valid.

Core Idea
Partial ignorance of genus is cured by specifying type or a price that indicates it.

This passage from the Hanafi commentary on al-Hidayah examines a nuanced case: a principal authorizes an agent to buy a slave, but the slave's genus is known only in part. While all slaves share a genus for work, their ethnicities (Turkish, Indian, etc.) are distinct genera for beauty. The ruling clarifies that the agency is valid if the type or an indicative price is mentioned, otherwise it fails due to excessive ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is tawkeel (agency) to buy something with partially unknown genus. The ruling: it is valid if the type or a price indicating the type is specified; otherwise it is invalid.
2
Understand the 'slave' example
A slave is considered one genus for the benefit of work, but for beauty and appearance, different ethnicities (Turkish, Indian, Sindhi, Abyssinian) are distinct genera. Thus, without specification, the agent does not know which type is intended.
Beauty is a key benefit; different ethnicities provide different levels of beauty.
3
Identify the two ways to reduce ignorance
The principal may mention the type (naw‘) explicitly, e.g., 'Turkish slave', or mention a price that is known to correspond to a specific type, e.g., a price typically paid for a Turkish slave.
The price acts as a proxy for the type when it is well-known.
4
Apply the condition
If neither the type nor an indicative price is mentioned, the agency is invalid because the agent cannot determine what to buy, and compliance is impossible (jahl fahish).
Excessive ignorance (jahl fahish) invalidates the contract.
5
Note the rationale
The commentator explains that beauty is a desired benefit, and seeing the face of a human reveals the beauty. Therefore, different ethnicities are considered different categories for this purpose, justifying the need for specification.
This is a practical example of how legal categories can differ based on the benefit sought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتصح بشراء شيءٍ جَهِلَ جِنْسُهُ من وجه كالعبد، وذُكِرَ نوعُهُ كالتركي، أو ثَمَنْ عين نوعاً. قوله : وتصح بشراء شيءٍ جَهِلَ جِنْسُهُ من وجه كالعبد، وذُكِرَ نوعُهُ كالتركي، أو ثَمَنٌ عَيْنَ نوعاً) ; يعني إن وكله لشراء شيء جنسه معلوم من وجه، العبد معلـوم الجنس من وجه لكن من حيث المنفعة والجمال، كأنه أجناس مختلفة، فإن بين نوعَهُ كالتُركي تصحُ الوكالة، وكذا إذا بين ثمناً، ويكونُ الثَّمنُ بحيث يعلم منه النوع. قوله : العبد معلوم الجنس...الخ ; تقريره : إنَّ العبد باعتبار منفعة العمل جنس واحد باعتبار منفعة النظر والجمال أجناس مختلفة، فإنَّ الجمال منفعة مطلوبة من بني آدم ; ولهذا جعل رؤية الوجه من بني آدم كرؤية الكلّ ; لحصول العلم بالمقصود، وهو الجمال ; لكونه مجمع المحاسن. وباعتبار هذه المنفعة يختلفُ التركي والهندي والسندي والحبشي، فإن بين نوعه كالتركي مثلاً تصح الوكالة، وكذا إذا بين ثمناً يعلم منه النوع ، فإن ذكر مثل هذا الثمن كذكر النوع في تقليل الجهالة، وإن لم يبين شيئاً منهما لم يصح التوكيل ويلحق بجهالة الجنس ; لامتناع الامتثال.
And the agency is valid for buying something whose genus is partially unknown, like a slave, and its type is mentioned, such as Turkish, or a price that specifies the type. He said: 'And it is valid for buying something whose genus is partially unknown, like a slave, and its type is mentioned, such as Turkish, or a price that specifies the type'; meaning that if he authorizes him to buy something whose genus is known from one aspect, the slave is known in genus from one aspect but from the perspective of benefit and beauty, it is as if it is different genera. So if he mentions its type, e.g., Turkish, the agency is valid, and likewise if he mentions a price from which the type is known. He said: 'The slave's genus is known...' etc.; its elaboration: The slave, by virtue of the benefit of work, is one genus; by virtue of the benefit of appearance and beauty, it is different genera. Indeed, beauty is a desired benefit from human beings; for this reason, seeing the face of a human is like seeing the whole, because knowledge of the intention is attained, which is beauty, as it is the gathering of virtues. By virtue of this benefit, the Turkish, Indian, Sindhi, and Abyssinian differ. So if he mentions its type, e.g., Turkish, the agency is valid, and likewise if he mentions a price from which the type is known. For mentioning such a price is like mentioning the type in reducing uncertainty. But if he does not mention anything of either, the agency is not valid and is attached to unknown genus, because compliance is impossible.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage discusses agency to buy an item whose genus is known from one aspect but unknown from another, exemplified by a slave. A slave's genus is known as a slave (human), but for purposes of beauty and appearance, different ethnicities (Turkish, Indian, etc.) form distinct categories. Therefore, if the principal specifies the type (e.g., Turkish) or mentions a price that implies the type, the agency is valid because uncertainty is reduced. If neither is specified, the agency is invalid due to excessive ignorance (jahl fahish), making compliance impossible. The commentator elaborates that beauty is a key benefit sought from slaves, so different ethnicities are considered different genera for that purpose.

Key Terms
جنس (jins): A category; here it refers to the broader classification (slave) versus more specific types (Turkish, Indian).
نوع (nawʿ): A subtype within a genus, e.g., Turkish slave within the slave genus.
ثمن (thaman): Price; a specified monetary amount that indicates the type when it is well-known.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Agency to buy an item with partially unknown genus is valid if the type or an indicative price is specified, reducing uncertainty to an acceptable level.

Agency to Buy a Specific Item with a Debt Owed to the Agent
Ruling on agency to buy a specific item using a debt owed to the agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the legal mechanism for a valid agency purchase using a debt, identify who bears the risk of loss, and derive the ruling using the text's reasoning.

Core Idea
When a creditor instructs a debtor to buy a specific item with the debt, the item belongs to the creditor and risk is on him.

Agency contracts can take creative forms. One such form is when a person (creditor) tells his debtor to purchase a particular item using the debt. This lesson unpacks the ruling and reasoning from the Hanafi text, clarifying ownership, risk, and the underlying logic that prevents improper assignment of debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the transaction
Understand that the creditor (principal) tells the debtor (agent) to buy a specific item using the debt owed. The agent buys the item from a third-party seller.
The item must be specified (ʿayn).
2
State the ruling on validity and ownership
According to the text, the agency is valid (ṣaḥīḥ) and the purchased item becomes the property of the principal (creditor) immediately upon purchase.
Ownership transfers directly to the principal, not the agent.
3
Explain the reasoning (proof) given in the text
The text argues that by specifying the item, the seller is effectively determined. This allows the agent to first collect the debt from himself for the principal (by treating the purchase as settlement) and then take possession for the principal. Thus, no transfer of debt to a third party occurs.
The key insight: specifying the item = specifying the seller → agent acts as both debtor (paying off the debt) and buyer for the principal.
4
Understand the risk allocation
If the item perishes in the agent's hand, it is the principal's loss, not the agent's. The agent is merely a trustee (amīn) after purchase.
Contrast with ordinary agency where the agent may bear risk if negligent.
5
Distinguish key terms: ʿayn vs dayn
The text emphasizes that the purchase is of a specific good (ʿayn), not a debt. The context shows that the word ʿayn here means a tangible item, opposite to dayn (debt).
This distinction avoids confusion with prohibited transactions involving debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبشراء عين بدين له على وكيله. قوله : وبشراء عين ...الخ ; أي وتصح الوكالة بشراء شيء معين بدين للموكل على وكيله. وصورته : أنَّ رب الدين قال للمديون: اشترلي هذا العبد بألف لي عليك ، فاشتراه يكون ملكاً للآمـر، ، حتى لو هلك في يد الوكيل يهلك على مال الآمر، لا على الوكيل ; لأنَّ في تعيين المبيع تعيين البائع، وفي تعيين البائع توكيله بقبض دينه من المديون أولاً لأجله، ثم بقبضه لنفسه، فلا يوجد تمليك الدين من غير من عليه الدين. كذا في مجمع الأنهر، وغيره. قوله : المراد...الخ ; لما كان المتبادر من لفظ العين ما يقابل الدين بقرينة أنه ذكره
And (the agency is valid) for buying a specific item with a debt that he (the principal) has on his agent. He said: 'And for buying a specific item...' etc.; meaning the agency is valid to buy a specific item with a debt that the principal has on his agent. Its form: the creditor tells the debtor: 'Buy this slave for me with the thousand (dirhams) that you owe me,' so he buys it; it becomes the property of the commander, even if it perishes in the agent's hand, it perishes on the wealth of the commander, not on the agent, because by specifying the sold item, the seller is specified, and by specifying the seller, the agent is authorized to first collect his debt from the debtor for his sake, and then to take possession for himself, so there is no assignment of debt from one who is free from debt. As in Majma' al-Anhur and others. He said: 'The intended meaning...' etc.; because the immediate understanding from the word 'specific item' (ayn) is that which is opposite to debt due to the context that he mentioned it.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific scenario: if a person (creditor) tells his debtor (agent) to buy a particular item (e.g., a slave) using the debt owed to him, the agency is valid. The item becomes the property of the creditor (the principal), and if it is lost in the agent's hands, it is the creditor's loss, not the agent's. The reasoning: by specifying the item, the seller (the person from whom the item is bought) is effectively determined; thus the agent is authorized to first collect the debt from the debtor for the creditor, then hand over the item. This avoids transferring the debt to someone not liable for it. The commentator also clarifies that 'ayn (specific item) here refers to a tangible good, not a debt, as indicated by context.

Key Terms
عين (ʿayn): A specific, tangible item (as opposed to a debt or obligation).
دين (dayn): A debt owed by the agent to the principal (or vice versa in other contexts).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man owes 500 dirhams to his friend. The friend tells him: 'Buy this piece of cloth for me with the 500 you owe me.' The agent buys the cloth. Who bears the loss if the cloth is destroyed before delivery?
A) The agent, because he was responsible.
B) The principal (friend), because the cloth belonged to him.
C) The seller, because the contract was not completed.
D) Both equally.

Key Takeaway
When a creditor instructs a debtor to buy a specific item with the debt, the item is owned by the creditor from the moment of purchase, and all risk is on him.

Agency to Buy a Specific Item with a Debt Owed by the Agent
Explains validity and conditions of agency when the item is specified using a debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for validity of an agency where the agent owes a debt to the principal and is instructed to buy a specific item, and apply the ruling to determine ownership and liability.

Core Idea
Specifying the item turns a debt transfer into a valid agency, avoiding prohibition.

This passage addresses a practical scenario: a creditor instructs his debtor to buy a specific item using the debt. At first glance, this might seem like an impermissible transfer of debt. However, the Hanafi school validates it through a clever legal construction: specifying the item implicitly appoints the seller as an agent to collect the debt, thus circumventing the prohibition. The key is reducing uncertainty (jahalah) about the item.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what transaction is being ruled upon and its legal status. The passage clarifies that the agency is valid (sahih) when the item is sufficiently specified.
The ruling is not automatically valid; it depends on specification.
2
Identify the condition for validity
Find what must be specified: either the type of the item (e.g., Turkish slave) or a price that reveals the type. The text uses a slave as an example but the principle applies to any commodity.
Mere genus is insufficient; the type must be known from the specification.
3
Trace the legal reasoning (illah)
Understand why specifying the item validates the agency. The commentator explains that specifying the sold item specifies the seller, and specifying the seller means he is authorized to collect the debt for the principal first, then for himself. This avoids the forbidden act of transferring a debt from someone other than the debtor.
The reasoning is rooted in the principle that transferring a debt (tamlik al-dayn) to someone other than the debtor is invalid.
4
Determine the practical consequence
What happens if the item perishes in the agent's hands? Since the agency is valid, ownership passes to the principal immediately upon purchase. Therefore, loss is on the principal, not the agent.
The agent is a trustee (amin), not a guarantor.

[1] قوله : العبد معلوم الجنس...الخ ؛ تقريره : إنَّ العبد باعتبار منفعة العمل جنس واحد باعتبار منفعة النظر والجمال أجناس مختلفة، فإنَّ الجمال منفعة مطلوبة من بني آدم ; ولهذا جعل رؤية الوجه من بني آدم كرؤية الكلّ ; لحصول العلم بالمقصود، وهو الجمال ; لكونه مجمع المحاسن. وباعتبار هذه المنفعة يختلفُ التركي والهندي والسندي والحبشي، فإن بين نوعه كالتركي مثلاً تصح الوكالة، وكذا إذا بين ثمناً يعلم منه النوع ، فإن ذكر مثل هذا الثمن كذكر النوع في تقليل الجهالة، وإن لم يبين شيئاً منهما لم يصح التوكيل ويلحق بجهالة الجنس ; لامتناع الامتثال. The slave is known in genus from one aspect, but from the aspect of benefit and beauty, it is as if different types. So if he specifies its type, e.g., Turkish, the agency is valid. Similarly if he specifies a price by which the type is known. (And buying a specific item with a debt owed to him by his agent) means a specific thing that is unknown in one aspect: like a slave whose type is mentioned, e.g., Turkish, or whose price is specified, e.g., a thousand dirhams, so this agency is permissible. [1] His saying: The slave is known in genus... etc.; its explanation: The slave, in terms of the benefit of work, is one type; in terms of the benefit of appearance and beauty, it is different types. Beauty is a desired benefit from humans; hence seeing the face of a human is like seeing the whole; because knowledge of the purpose (beauty) is attained, as it is the locus of beauty. In terms of this benefit, Turkish, Indian, Sindhi, and Ethiopian differ. If he specifies its type, e.g., Turkish, the agency is valid; similarly if he specifies a price by which the type is known; mentioning such a price is like mentioning the type in reducing ignorance. If he does not specify any of these, the agency is invalid and it is attached to ignorance of the genus, because compliance is impossible. [2] His saying: And buying a specific item... etc.; meaning: The agency is valid for buying a specific thing with a debt owed to the principal by his agent. Its form: The creditor said to the debtor: Buy me this slave with the thousand I have against you, so he buys it; it becomes the property of the principal, even if it is destroyed in the agent's hand, it is destroyed from the principal's wealth, not the agent's; because specifying the sold item specifies the seller, and specifying the seller means authorizing him to collect his debt from the debtor first for his sake, then to collect it for himself; thus there is no transfer of the debt from someone other than the one upon whom it is owed. As in Majma' al-Anhur and others.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil) from the commentary on al-Wiqaya. It addresses the validity of agency (wakalah) when the principal instructs his agent (who also owes him a debt) to buy a specific item (ayn) with that debt. The text first clarifies that a slave, while uniform in genus from one perspective (work), varies in type due to differences in appearance and beauty. Therefore, if the principal specifies the slave's type (e.g., Turkish) or mentions a price that indicates the type, the agency becomes valid because uncertainty (jahalah) is minimized. If no specification is made, the agency is invalid due to excessive ignorance. Then it explains the ruling: If the principal says to his debtor, 'Buy this specific slave for me with the thousand you owe me,' and the agent buys it, the slave becomes the principal's property. If the slave perishes in the agent's hand, the loss is on the principal. The reason (illa) is that specifying the sold item also specifies the seller, making the seller an agent to collect the debt first for the principal and then for himself. This avoids transferring the debt from someone other than the one who owes it (tamlik al-dayn min ghayr man alayhi al-dayn), which is invalid. The Hanafi position is that such agency is valid and effective.

Key Terms
عَيْن (‘ayn): A specific, tangible item (as opposed to debt). In this context, it refers to a specific slave or item that is known by type or price.
دَيْن (dayn): A debt owed by the agent to the principal. The principal instructs the agent to use this debt to buy an item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The debtor (agent) bears the loss.
B) The creditor (principal) bears the loss.
C) The seller bears the loss.
D) The loss is shared equally.

Key Takeaway
An agency to buy a specific item with a debt is valid if the item is specified by type or price, and ownership passes immediately to the principal.

Agency to Buy a Non-Specific Item with a Debt Owed by the Agent
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agency validity when item is unspecified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on agency to buy a non-specific item with a debt, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and apply the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Agency to buy an unspecified item with a debt owed is invalid per Abu Hanifa; the agent bears loss until the principal takes possession.

This passage addresses a specific scenario: a creditor instructs his debtor to buy a non-specific item (e.g., any slave) using the debt owed. The key legal question is whether such an agency (wakalah) is valid. The matn states the ruling according to Abu Hanifa, but the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) dissent, leading to an important intra-Hanafi dispute with practical consequences on liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling in the matn
Locate the statement: 'In the case of a non-specific item, if it perishes in the agent's hand, the loss is on the agent; if the principal takes possession, it is his.' Understand that the agency is purchase of an unspecified item with a specific debt.
The ruling is from the imam (Abu Hanifa).
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's proof (dalil)
He argues that dirhams and dinars become specified in agency contracts, so restricting the purchase to that debt makes it a transfer of debt from someone other than the debtor (tamlik al-dayn min ghayr man 'alayhi al-dayn), which is invalid. Also, the creditor does not own the debt before possession.
This is based on the principle that debts are satisfied by equivalents, not specific items.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's position and proof
The two companions hold that the agency is valid and binding on the principal, just as when the item is specified. The purchase binds the principal regardless of specification.
They draw an analogy (qiyas) to the specified case.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa's rebuttal: the specification of the debt in the agency creates a transfer of debt, which is invalid without proper authorization from the debtor to collect for himself. The analogy fails because in the specified case the seller is identified and becomes an agent to collect.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's: agency is invalid, so if the item perishes in the agent's hands, the agent bears the loss. According to the Sahibayn, the principal bears the loss once the agent takes possession.
The text presents Abu Hanifa's view as the madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And in the case of a non-specific item, if it perishes in the agent's hand, it perishes on him (the agent); but if the principal takes possession, it is his. He ordered him to buy a slave with the thousand he has against the commanded person, and he did not specify the slave, so he bought it and it died in the hand of the commanded person, then its loss is on him, and it does not become the principal's unless he takes possession. This is according to Abu Hanifa based on the fact that the agency was not valid; because dirhams and dinars become specified in agencies, so the purchase is restricted to that debt, resulting in transfer of the debt from someone other than the one upon whom it is owed without authorization from that other, and this is not valid. Unlike when the slave is specified, then the seller becomes an agent to collect the debt, so the transfer of debt is valid. ... If the agent buys and it perishes in his hand, it is upon him; because the purchase is executed on him, not on the principal. If the principal takes possession, it is his. This is the madhhab of Imam (Abu Hanifa). The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Just as it is binding on the principal in the specific case, so it is binding here, whether the principal takes possession or not, and the loss of the sold item is on the principal if the agent took possession. [2] His saying: Unless he takes possession; meaning: unless the principal takes possession and it perishes in his possession, then its loss is on him, not on the commanded agent. (36) His saying: Based on... etc.; its explanation: Dirhams and dinars become specified in agencies. Hence if the agency is restricted to the specific coins or the specific debt, and then the coins are destroyed or the debt is forgiven by the principal releasing the agent, the agency becomes void. So when dirhams and dinars are specified in the agency, this authorization is a transfer of debt from someone other than the one upon whom it is owed. That is not permissible unless he authorizes him to collect it for him to collect for himself, and authorizing an unknown person is not permissible; thus it is void, like if Zayd has a debt on Amr, and Zayd buys something from another person with that debt which he has on Amr, it is not permissible. Or this authorization is an order to spend what he does not own except after possession, and that is void; because the creditor does not truly own his debt before possession, since debts are satisfied by their equivalents, not their specific entities. So what the debtor pays to the seller or to the creditor is the debtor's property, and the creditor does not...
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the validity of agency when the principal instructs his debtor to buy a non-specific item (e.g., any slave) with the debt owed. The text states the ruling from the matn: If the item is not specified and perishes in the agent's hand, the loss is on the agent; if the principal takes possession, it becomes his. This is Abu Hanifa's position (Imam). He argues that dirhams and dinars are specified in agency contracts; therefore, restricting the purchase to a specific debt makes it a transfer of debt from someone other than the debtor (tamlik al-dayn min ghayr man alayhi al-dayn), which is invalid. In contrast, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the agency is valid and binding on the principal, similar to the case where the item is specified; the loss is on the principal if the agent took possession. The commentary elaborates Abu Hanifa's proof: since dirhams and dinars are specified, if they are destroyed or the debt is forgiven, the agency terminates. The authorization amounts to an invalid transfer of debt. Alternatively, it is an order to spend what one does not own before possession, which is also invalid. Thus, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that such agency is invalid, and the agent bears the loss until the principal takes possession.

Key Terms
غَيْر عَيْن (ghayr ‘ayn): A non-specific item; an item not specified by type or price, leading to uncertainty (jahalah) in the agency.
تَمْلِيك الدَّيْن مِن غَيْر مَنْ عَلَيْهِ الدَّيْن (tamlik al-dayn min ghayr man ‘alayhi al-dayn): Transfer of a debt from someone other than the debtor; a prohibited transaction in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the item is unknown, causing gharar in the sale.
B) Because dirhams and dinars become specified in agency, leading to an invalid transfer of debt.
C) Because the agent does not own the debt.
D) Because the principal is not present.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi holds that agency to buy an unspecified item with a debt owed by the agent is invalid; the agent is liable until the principal takes possession.

Specificity of Dirhams in Agency
Explains the intra-Hanafi dispute on ta'ayyun of currency in wakalah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on ta'ayyun of dirhams in agency, distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the mufta bihi position to determine liability when currency is lost.

Core Idea
Dirhams do not become specific in agency after delivery according to the majority; loss does not void the agency.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: do dirhams and dinars become individually identified (ta'ayyun) in an agency contract? The answer determines who bears the loss if the currency is destroyed before the agent completes the purchase. Abu Hanifa held that they do become specific, voiding the agency upon loss; the Sahibayn and later majority held they do not, so the principal remains liable. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze contractual obligations and the role of custom in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the precise ruling of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding whether dirhams and dinars become specific (ta'ayyun) in agency contracts.
Abu Hanifa says they become specific; the Sahibayn say they do not.
2
Understand the core issue
Recognise that the dispute is about the effect of loss of the currency before delivery. For Abu Hanifa, loss voids the agency; for the Sahibayn, it does not.
The practical consequence (thamarah) is liability for the agent's purchase.
3
Follow the Sahibayn's proof
Analyse the analogy (qiyas) from ordinary sales and from the command to give a debt to whomever the debtor chooses — both indicate non-specificity.
In sale, dirhams are not specific; agency is a means to sale, so similar rule applies.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's response
Examine the counter-analogy from charity (giving debt to the poor) and the distinction that specifying the seller makes the agency valid and binding.
The charity analogy shows that designation makes the recipient known, like specifying a seller.
5
Determine the tarjih
Note that the majority of later scholars (عامتهم) adopt the Sahibayn's view that dirhams do not become specific after delivery, and this is the mufta bihi.
Two benefits: agency continues as long as dirhams exist in custom, and agent cannot claim reimbursement from principal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندهما: إذا قَبَضَ المأمورُ يصير ملكاً للآمر ؛ لأنَّ الدَّراهم والدنانير لم تتعين فلم يتقيَّدِ التوكيلُ بالدَّين، فصحت الوكالة، فيكون للأمر. وجوابه : ما مر أنها تتعيَّن في الوكالات ، فإنَّه إذا قيَّد الوكالة بها عيناً كانت أو ديناً ، فهلكت أو سقط الدِّينُ تبطل الوكالة. يملك ما في ذمة المديون حقيقة قبل القبض ، فكان أمرُ ربِّ الدين بالدفع إلى البائع أمراً فيما لا يملكه فكان باطلاً. وهذا كما إذا قال : أعطه ما لي عليك من شئت، فإنّه باطل ؛ لأنه أمر بصرف ما لا يملكه إلا بالقبض إلى من يختاره المديون بنفسه، بخلاف ما إذا عين الموكَّلُ البائع، فإنَّ التوكيل صحيح لازم للآمر ؛ لأنه يصير البائع أولاً وكيلاً عنه في القبض ثم يتملكه، وهذا ليس تمليك من غير من عليه، وليس أمرا بصرف ما لم يقبض، كما لو وهب دينه على غيره، ووكل الموهوب له لقبضه. كذا في شروح «الهداية»(۱). [1]قوله : وعندهما ...الخ ؛ تحريره : إنّ الدراهم والدنانير لا تتعيَّن في المعاوضات ، سواء كانت تلك المعاوضات ديناً أو عيناً ؛ ولهذا لو اشترى شيئاً بدراهم على المشتري ثم تصادقا أن لا دين لا يبطل الشراء، ويجب عليه مثله ، فإذا لم تتعين صار الإطلاق والتقييد به سواء. كما في غير الدين، حتى إذا وكله بأن يشتري له عبداً ولم يُعين الألف، ولا البائع ولا المبيع، جاز التوكيل، فكذا هذا ، فصار كما لو قال : تصدق بما لي عليك من الدين (۲) على المساكين فإنّه يجوز [٢] قوله : ما مر...الخ ؛ والجواب عن قياسهما على الأمر بالتصدق أنه جعل المال تعالى، وهو معلوم فصار كتعيين البائع. [٣] قوله : تتعين في الوكالات ؛ قال في «الكفاية» (۳) نقلاً عن «الذخيرة»: قال محمد ه في «الزيادات»: رجل قال لغيره اشترِ لي بهذه الألف [من] الدراهم جارية، وأراه الدراهم، ولم يسلّمها إلى الوكيل، حتى سرقت الدراهم، ثم اشترى الوكيل جارية بألف درهم، لزم الموكل. ثم قال : الأصل: إنَّ الدراهم والدنانير لا تتعيَّنان في الوكالات قبل التسليم بلا خلاف ؛ لأنَّ الوكالات وسيلة إلى الشراء، فيعتبر بنفس الشراء، والدراهم والدنانير لا تتعينان فيه قبل التسليم، فكذا فيما هو وسيلة إليه ، فأما بعد التسليم هل تتعين؟ اختلف المشايخ فيه : فبعضهم قالوا : تتعيَّن حتى تبطل الوكالة بهلاكها ؛ لأنَّ يد الوكيل يدُ أمانة، والدراهم والدنانير تتعيَّنان في الأمانات، وعامتهم على أنها لا تتعيَّن. وفائدة النقد والتسليم على قول عامة المشايخ اثنتان : إحداهما : إنه يتوقف بقاء الوكالة ببقاء الدراهم المنقودة، فإنَّ العرف الظاهر فيما بين النَّاسِ أَنَّ الموكَّل إذا دفع الدَّراهم إلى الوكيل يريد شراءه حال قيام الدراهم في يد الوكيل. والثانية : قطع رجوع الوكيل على الموكّل فيما وجب للوكيل عليه، وهذا لأنَّ شراء الوكيل يوجب دينين ، ديناً للبائع على الوكيل، ودينا للوكيل على الموكل. انتهى.
And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): When the agent takes possession, it becomes ownership for the principal, because dirhams and dinars are not specific, so the agency is not tied to the debt, thus the agency is valid, so it belongs to the principal. The answer: It was previously stated that they do become specific in agencies, for when he restricts the agency to them, whether they are specific or a debt, then if they perish or the debt falls, the agency becomes void. The debtor actually owns what is in his liability before possession, so the creditor's command to pay to the seller is a command regarding what he does not own, so it is void. This is like when he says: Give what I have against you to whomever you wish, it is void, because it is a command to spend what he does not own except through possession, to whomever the debtor chooses himself, unlike when the principal specifies the seller, then the agency is valid and binding on the principal, because the seller first becomes an agent for him in taking possession then acquires ownership, and this is not transferring ownership from someone not obligated, nor is it a command to spend what has not been taken, like when he gifts his debt to another and the donee appoints an agent to collect it. Thus in the commentaries of al-Hidayah. [1] His statement: 'And according to the two... etc.'; its explanation: Dirhams and dinars are not specified in commutative contracts, whether those contracts involve a debt or a specific item; hence if one buys something for dirhams on credit from the buyer, then they agree that there is no debt, the sale is not invalidated, and the equivalent must be paid. So when they are not specified, the absolute and the restricted become the same. As in non-debt cases, even if he appoints him to buy a slave without specifying the thousand, nor the seller, nor the item, the agency is valid. So this is like when he says: Give charity from the debt I have against you to the poor, it is valid. [2] His statement: 'What was previously stated... etc.'; and the answer to their analogy with the command to give charity is that he designated the money for Allah, and it is known, so it becomes like specifying the seller. [3] His statement: 'They become specific in agencies'; said in al-Kifayah quoting al-Dhakhirah: Muhammad said in al-Ziyadat: A man said to another: Buy me a slave girl with these [من] dirhams, and he showed him the dirhams, but did not hand them over to the agent, until the dirhams were stolen, then the agent bought a slave girl for a thousand dirhams, it is binding on the principal. Then he said: The principle: Dirhams and dinars do not become specific in agencies before delivery without dispute, because agencies are a means to purchase, so they are considered like the purchase itself, and dirhams and dinars are not specified in the purchase before delivery, so similarly in what is a means to it. As for after delivery, do they become specific? The scholars differed: Some said they become specific, so the agency becomes void by their destruction, because the agent's hand is a trust hand, and dirhams and dinars become specific in trusts. But the majority hold that they do not become specific. The benefit of payment and delivery according to the majority of scholars are two: One: The continuation of the agency depends on the continued existence of the paid dirhams, for the apparent custom among people is that when the principal gives the dirhams to the agent, he intends his purchase as long as the dirhams remain in the agent's hand. The second: Cutting off the agent's recourse against the principal for what is owed to the agent, because the agent's purchase creates two debts: a debt from the seller to the agent, and a debt from the agent to the principal. End.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether dirhams and dinars become specific (ta'ayyun) in agency contracts. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that they do not become specific before delivery, so if the agent takes possession, the purchased item becomes the principal's property. Abu Hanifa's position is that they do become specific in agencies, so if the specified currency is lost, the agency becomes void. The commentary explains the reasoning: in commutative contracts, dirhams are not specific, but agencies are different because they are a means to purchase. A key analogy is the command to give charity: if the principal says 'give my debt to the poor', it is valid because the charity recipient is known. The answer to the Sahibayn's analogy is that designating for Allah makes the recipient known, like specifying the seller. The text then quotes al-Kifayah and al-Dhakhirah, citing a case from Muhammad's al-Ziyadat: if an agent buys a slave after the specified dirhams were stolen, the principal is still liable. The principle affirmed is that before delivery, there is no dispute on non-specificity; after delivery, the majority hold they remain non-specific, with two practical benefits: (1) agency continues as long as the dirhams exist in common custom, and (2) it prevents the agent from claiming reimbursement from the principal for the debt created.

Key Terms
تتعين (tata'ayyan): Becomes specific or determined; here, whether dirhams and dinars become individually identified in a contract so that their loss affects the contract.
وكالة (wakālah): Agency; a contract where one person appoints another to perform a legal act on his behalf.
عامتهم (āmmatuhum): The majority of them; refers to the majority of later Hanafi scholars who adopt the Sahibayn's view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of later Hanafi scholars (عامتهم), do dirhams and dinars become specific in agency contracts after delivery?
A) Yes, they become specific and the agency is void if they are lost.
B) No, they do not become specific; the agency continues and the principal bears the loss.
C) It depends on whether the agency is for sale or purchase.
D) They become specific only if the principal explicitly states so.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the mufta bihi is that dirhams do not become specific in agency contracts after delivery; thus, loss of the currency does not void the agency.

Slave as Agent: Self-Purchase and the Effect of Wording
Ruling on a slave acting as agent to buy himself from his master.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a slave acting as agent to buy himself, distinguish between the two scenarios based on the slave's wording, and explain the legal reasoning behind the different outcomes.

Core Idea
A slave's words dictate the legal nature of the transaction: agency or self-emancipation.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced case in agency law. A principal instructs a slave to buy himself from his master. The slave's response determines whether the transaction is a sale for the principal or an emancipation contract for the slave. We will walk through the two scenarios and the objection raised, clarifying the legal principles at work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Scenarios
Recognize the two possible wordings by the slave when addressing his master: mentioning the principal's name or not.
The key phrase is 'li-fulān' (to so-and-so).
2
State the Ruling for Each
If the slave says 'Sell me to so-and-so', the sale is valid on behalf of the principal. If he simply says 'Sell me', the slave is emancipated upon payment.
The second outcome is considered a contract of kitābah (emancipation for payment).
3
Recognize the Objection
Understand the natural objection: In standard agency, when an agent purchases a specific item without naming the principal, the purchase still goes to the principal. Why is this case different?
The objector assumes the same rule should apply here.
4
Understand the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi position distinguishes based on the nature of the act. Here, the slave is performing an act of a different type – emancipation for payment ('itq ʿalā māl) – not a purchase. For such acts, if not attributed to the principal, it is attributed to the agent (the slave).
This is an application of the principle that the classification of the transaction matters.
5
Apply the Distinction
Be able to articulate that the key is whether the agent's act is of the same type as the principal's instruction. If it is a different type (e.g., emancipation vs. purchase), the default attribution changes.
This reasoning prevents an unwanted transfer of ownership to the principal when the slave clearly intends self-purchase.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبشراء نفس المأمورِ من سيِّدِهِ إن قال : بعني نفسي لفلان، فباع يقع عن الآمر، فإن لم يقل : لفلان عُيّق على المولى ) : أي إذا قال رجل لعبد : اشتر لي نفسك من مولاك ، فالعبد إن قال : بعني نفسي لفلان ، فباع يقع عن الآمر ، وإن لم يقل لفلان عُيّق على المولى. فإن قيل : الوكيلُ بشراء شيء معيَّن إذا اشتراه من غير أن يضيف إلى الآمرِ يَقعُ عن الآمر. قلنا : الوكيل قد أتى بتصرف من جنس آخر، وهو العتق على مال، وفي مثل هذا يقع عن الوكيل
And regarding the agent purchasing himself from his master: if he says 'sell me myself to so-and-so', then the sale is valid on behalf of the principal; but if he does not say 'to so-and-so', the slave is emancipated on the master. That is: when a man says to a slave: Buy yourself from your master for me, then the slave if he says to his master: Sell me myself to so-and-so, and he sells, it is valid on behalf of the principal; but if he does not say 'to so-and-so', the slave is emancipated on the master. If it is said: The agent for purchasing a specific thing, when he purchases it without attributing it to the principal, it is valid on behalf of the principal. We say: The agent has performed an act of a different type, which is emancipation for payment, and in such a case, it is valid on behalf of the agent.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on a specific case in agency: a principal instructs a slave to buy himself from his master. The slave, as agent, then addresses his master. Two scenarios: (1) If the slave says 'Sell me to so-and-so' (mentioning the principal's name), the sale is executed on behalf of the principal, and the principal owns the slave. (2) If the slave simply says 'Sell me' without specifying the buyer, the transaction is considered a contract of kitabah (emancipation for a payment), and the slave becomes free upon payment to the master, and the act is attributed to the slave himself, not the principal. The passage then answers an objection: Normally, if an agent buys a specific item without mentioning the principal, the purchase is still for the principal. Why isn't this case similar? The answer: Here the agent (the slave) is performing a different type of transaction—not a purchase but emancipation for payment (ʿitq ʿalā māl). In such a transaction, if the agent does not attribute it to the principal, it is considered done on behalf of the agent (the slave) himself, not the principal.

Key Terms
عتق على مال (ʿitq ʿalā māl): Emancipation for a payment; a contract where a slave gains freedom in exchange for paying his master a sum of money.
وكيل (wakīl): Agent; here, the slave acting on behalf of the principal to purchase himself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave says to his master 'sell me' without mentioning the principal's name, what is the legal outcome according to this passage?
A) The slave becomes free on payment, and the act is attributed to the principal.
B) The slave becomes free on payment, and the act is attributed to the slave himself.
C) The sale is void because the principal was not named.
D) The principal owns the slave regardless.

Key Takeaway
The legal effect of a slave's self-purchase as agent depends on whether he names the principal; if he does not, it becomes an emancipation contract for the slave.

Agency for a Slave to Purchase Himself: Agent as Messenger or Principal?
Ruling on agent's wording when buying a slave from his master

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who bears the price when an agent buys a slave from his master on the slave's behalf, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad's two narrations.

Core Idea
An agent buying a slave for himself is a messenger; the slave owes the price, not the agent.

In Hanafi fiqh, agency (wakalah) is a contract where one person appoints another to perform a legal act. A unique scenario arises when a slave appoints an agent to purchase him from his master. Since a slave cannot own himself, the transaction is legally treated as a manumission for a price. The key question is: who bears the price if the agent does not specify that he is buying for the slave? This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya' examines the ruling and an intra-Hanafi disagreement from Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two possible wordings
A slave gives an agent money and says: 'Buy me from my master.' The agent can either specify 'I bought him for himself' (the slave) or remain silent/unclear. Each leads to a different legal outcome.
The wording is crucial because it determines whether the transaction is a manumission or a sale.
2
Understand the legal consequence of each wording
If the agent says 'for himself', the slave is freed and owes the price (manumission for a consideration). If the agent does not specify, the purchase is for the agent himself: he becomes the owner of the slave and owes the price to the master, while the master keeps the slave's money as his earnings.
The master's receipt of the slave's money is valid in both cases because it is the slave's earnings.
3
Analyze the rationale: why buying oneself is metaphorical manumission
A slave cannot own himself, so a true sale is impossible. Therefore, the transaction is treated as a manumission for a price: the slave 'buys' his freedom. The agent, when acting for the slave, is merely a messenger (safir) conveying the slave's acceptance of manumission.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) comparing the case to regular manumission.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute: two narrations from Imam Muhammad
Imam Muhammad narrates two positions: (1) in 'Bab al-Wakalah bil-Itq', the slave owes the price and the agent is not liable; (2) in 'Wakalah al-Ma'dhun' and 'al-Jami' al-Kabir', the agent is liable by analogy to a regular agency (where the agent becomes principal).
The difference arises from whether the slave's incapacity to own himself changes the nature of agency.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position and its basis
The relied-upon position is the first: the agent is a messenger, and the slave owes the price. The author of 'Umda al-Ri'aya' declares it correct because the agent is only a conduit and the wording of sale is metaphorical for manumission.
This tarjih follows the principle that where the literal meaning is impossible, a metaphorical meaning is adopted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Book of Agency / Chapter of Agency for Selling and Buying. And regarding the purchase of the commander (slave) himself from his master for one thousand that he paid: if he (the agent) said to his master: 'I bought him for himself', and he sold him, then he (the slave) is freed by that, and the price is upon the slave; if he did not say 'for himself', then (the slave) belongs to the agent, and the price is upon him (the agent), and the thousand (dirhams) belong to the master. That is, a slave said to a man: 'Buy me myself from my master for one thousand', and paid it to him. The agent said: 'I bought him for himself', and he (the master) sold him, then it is a manumission for a consideration. And if he did not say 'for himself', then the purchase is from the agent, so the price is upon the buyer, and this thousand belongs to the master because it is his slave's earnings. If you say: the slave here is interdicted, and if the agent is interdicted, rights do not revert to him. We say: the interdiction is removed here by the contract that he conducted accompanied by the master's permission. Then if the purchase is for the commander (slave), the slave's acceptance is necessary because it is a sale; it is not concluded except by offer and acceptance. And if it is for the slave, the master's saying 'I sold' suffices, and the slave's acceptance is not needed after his saying 'Buy me myself' because it is a manumission, so the master acts alone in it, based on the principle that one person can handle both sides of the contract in manumission like marriage, but cannot handle both sides in a sale. Thus in al-Tabyin and others. Explanation: If a slave appoints a man to buy himself from his master for one thousand, and pays the thousand to him, and the agent says to the master at the time of purchase: 'I bought your slave for himself', and he (the master) sold him on this basis, then that slave is freed, and his wala' (clientage) is for his master. And if he (the agent) said: 'I bought him', and did not clarify that he bought him for himself (the slave), then the slave becomes the property of the agent, who is the buyer, and the thousand that he took from the slave and paid to the master belongs to the master in both cases gratuitously. The principle of this: the sale of a slave by himself is a manumission for a consideration, and the slave's purchase of himself is an acceptance of manumission for a price; because considering it a sale in reality is not possible, either because the slave is not capable of ownership, or because it is impossible for him to own himself, so it is treated as a metaphor for manumission due to the existence of removal of ownership in it like a sale. So if the agent buys him for the slave, he becomes a manumitter, so wala' is obligatory upon him; and the agent in acceptance is a messenger and a speaker on his behalf, so rights do not revert to him. And if the agent is absolute and does not qualify with 'for himself', the contract takes effect for the agent, because the apparent meaning of this is a sale, so one does not deviate from it to manumission without the master's knowledge, and perhaps he is not pleased with it because of the resulting obligation of his clientage and the responsibility for his crimes, so the purchase is not for the slave, unlike the agent of someone other than the slave, where the purchase enters into the ownership of the principal without him clarifying to the master that he is buying for his principal, because the ruling of the contract in that case does not differ between it being for him or for his principal, as all is a sale, and the agent is a principal in both cases, so rights attach to him in both cases, so clarification is not needed. And here, one (case) is a manumission that results in wala', and rights attach to the agent; the other is a sale, and its rulings differ from manumission, so his consent to one does not indicate consent to the other, so clarification is necessary. So if he does not clarify, ownership is established for the agent, and the thousand is for the master because it is his slave's earnings, and upon the buyer (agent) or upon the slave if he is freed, a thousand like it or the substitute for the manumission, because the payment has been voided due to the master's entitlement to what he paid by another aspect, namely that it is his slave's earnings, so it was his property before the purchase and before the manumission, so his property cannot be valid as a substitute for his property. Then, if he does not clarify, the master recovers from the agent, because he is the contractor and owner of the slave, so rights revert to him. And if he clarified that he buys for the slave, Muhammad mentioned in 'Chapter of Agency for Manumission' in the 'Book of Agency' that the manumission occurs and the money is upon the slave, not the agent. And he mentioned in 'Agency of the Permitted Slave and the Mukatib' from the 'Book of Agency', and in 'Agency' of al-Jami' al-Kabir that the slave is freed and the money is upon the agent, because his appointment to purchase the slave for the slave is like his appointment to purchase for another, so the agent is demanded for its substitute. And the correct is the first, because the agent of the slave in manumission is a messenger and a speaker on his behalf, and the wording of sale becomes a metaphor for manumission due to the impossibility of considering the meaning of sale in reality; and for this, it is not free from being attributed to the slave who commands it, so his rights do not attach to the agent, so the commander (slave) is demanded, just as if the command to sell the slave himself from the slave was the master, where the claim for the substitute is against the master, not the agent. Thus in
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on a scenario where a slave appoints an agent to buy him from his master. The text explains that if the agent explicitly says 'I bought him for himself' (meaning for the slave), the transaction is considered a manumission for a price, and the slave is freed with the price owed by him. If the agent does not specify, the purchase is for the agent himself, making him the owner of the slave and liable for the price, while the master keeps the slave's money as his earnings. The reasoning distinguishes between a sale and a manumission: a slave cannot own himself, so buying himself is a metaphorical manumission. The agent's role as a mere messenger (safir) is crucial; if he clarifies the intent, the slave is freed and owes the price; if not, the agent owns the slave. The passage also presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf from Imam Muhammad: one narration states the agent is not liable (the relied-upon position), while another makes the agent liable, analogizing to a regular agency. The author prefers the first, as the agent is only a messenger and the wording is metaphorical for manumission. This ruling emphasizes the importance of clear wording in agency contracts where the nature of the transaction (sale vs. manumission) changes the outcome.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): Agent appointed to perform a transaction (buying/selling) on behalf of another.
الآمر (al-āmir): The one who commands the agent; here, the slave who orders his own purchase.
إعتاق (iʿtāq): Manumission; freeing a slave. In this context, a sale treated as manumission because the slave buys himself.
سفير (safīr): A messenger or intermediary who merely conveys the offer/acceptance without being a principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a slave appoints an agent to buy him from his master, what happens if the agent says 'I bought him' without specifying 'for himself'?
A) The slave is freed and owes the price.
B) The agent becomes the owner of the slave and owes the price.
C) The master keeps both the slave and the price.
D) The contract is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In agency for a slave's self-purchase, the agent's wording determines whether it is a manumission (slave owes price) or a sale for the agent (agent owes price).

Slave's Agent Buying for Manumission: Who Bears the Price?
Ruling on price liability when slave's agent buys him

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi opinions on who bears the price when a slave's agent buys him for manumission, and apply the preferred view (mufta bihi) to a given scenario.

Core Idea
The slave's agent is a mere messenger; the price falls on the slave, not the agent.

When a slave appoints an agent to buy him (resulting in automatic manumission), the financial liability may differ depending on whether the agent explicitly states his intent. The Hanafi school has two narrations from Imam Muhammad on this point. This lesson walks through the reasoning and identifies the relied-upon position (al-sahih) that the price is on the slave, not the agent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the default rule
Identify the general rule: if an agent is appointed to buy something and does not specify 'for myself', the contract falls on the agent. This is because the default in sales is that the agent acts for himself.
This default preserves the master's rights and avoids imputing manumission without consent.
2
Distinguish the slave's agent from a free agent
For a free person, buying without specification still benefits the principal because the agent is always a principal in liability. For the slave, however, the two outcomes (sale vs manumission) have different legal consequences, so specification is necessary.
With a free agent, both possibilities are sale; with a slave, one is sale and the other is manumission with wala'.
3
Analyze the two scenarios
Case 1: Agent does not specify: the slave is not freed, the agent owns the slave, and the master takes the price as the slave's earning. Case 2: Agent specifies 'for the slave': the slave is freed, and there is a dispute on who bears the price.
The dispute is only in the second case.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad is reported to have two views: (a) the price is on the slave (from 'Bab al-Wakala bil-'Itq'), and (b) the price is on the agent (from 'Wakalat al-Ma'dhun' and al-Jami' al-Kabir).
These are both attributed to the same imam, so it's an intra-Hanafi transmission difference.
5
Apply the tarjih
The correct view (al-sahih) is the first: the agent is a mere messenger (safir), so liability falls on the slave (the commander). The master's recourse is against the slave, not the agent.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The wording: And if the agent is absolute and does not restrict by saying 'for himself,' the contract falls for the agent, because the apparent meaning is for sale, so one does not deviate from it to manumission without the master's knowledge, and perhaps he does not consent to it due to what it entails of his guardianship and responsibility for his crimes. Thus the purchase is not for the slave, unlike the agent who is not the slave, where it enters the ownership of the principal by purchase without him making known to the master that he is buying it for his principal, because the ruling of the contract does not differ between being for himself or for his principal, since all is sale, and the agent is a principal in both cases, so that rights attach to him in both cases, so there is no need for clarification. And here, one is manumission resulting in wala', and rights attach to the agent, and the other is sale, and its rulings are contrary to manumission, so his consent to one does not indicate consent to the other, so clarification is necessary. Thus if he does not clarify, ownership is established for the agent, and the thousand (price) is for the master because it is his slave's earning, and it is upon the purchaser or upon the slave if he is freed a similar thousand, or the substitute for manumission, because the payment has been voided due to the master's entitlement to what he paid by another avenue, i.e., it was his slave's earning, so it was his property before the purchase and before manumission, so his property cannot serve as a substitute for his property. Then if he does not clarify, the master has recourse against the agent because he is the contractor and owner of the slave, so rights revert to him. And if he clarifies that he is buying for the slave, then Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned in 'Chapter of Agency for Manumission' in the 'Book of Agency': Indeed, the manumission occurs, and the money is upon the slave, not the agent. And he mentioned in 'Agency of the Authorized and the Mukatab' from the 'Book of Agency', and in 'Agency' of 'al-Jami' al-Kabir': Indeed, the slave is freed and the money is upon the agent because his agency to buy the slave for the slave is like his agency to buy him for another, so the agent is demanded for its substitute. And the correct is the first, because the agent of the slave in manumission is a messenger and interpreter, and the word of sale is metaphorical for manumission due to the impossibility of considering the meaning of sale literally; therefore, it cannot dispense with its addition to the slave who commands it, so his rights do not attach to the agent, so the commander is demanded, just as when the command to sell the slave himself is from the master, where the demand for the substitute is upon the master, not the agent. Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of a slave appointing an agent to buy him (resulting in manumission). The discussion opens with a general principle: if a person appoints an agent to buy something and the agent does not specify 'for myself', the contract is for the agent himself, because the default in sales is that the agent acts for his own benefit. The reason is that the master may not consent to manumission due to the loss of his rights of guardianship and liability for the slave's crimes. This contrasts with an ordinary agent (not a slave), where the purchase goes to the principal even without specification, because in both cases it is a sale and the agent is the principal for liability purposes. In the slave's case, however, one outcome (manumission) changes the nature of the transaction, so specification is needed. If the agent does not clarify, the purchased slave belongs to the agent, and the price belongs to the master (as it is the slave's earning). If the agent does clarify that he buys for the slave, then the slave is freed. There is a dispute: Imam Muhammad says the price is on the slave; another view (attributed to him in al-Jami' al-Kabir) says the price is on the agent. The correct view (al-sahih) is the first: the agent is merely a messenger, so the financial obligation falls on the slave (the commander).

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): An agent appointed to perform a legal act on behalf of another.
سفير (safīr): A messenger or intermediary who merely conveys the decision of the principal.
عتق (‘itq): Manumission; freeing a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The agent, because he is the direct buyer.
B) The slave, because the agent is a messenger.
C) The master, because the slave cannot own property.
D) The seller bears the loss.

Key Takeaway
The slave's agent in manumission is a mere messenger; the price falls on the slave, not the agent.

The Basic Rule: Trustee vs Denier in Agency Disputes
Ruling on credibility in agency disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for who is believed in a dispute between an agent and principal regarding a purchase, and apply it to the two primary scenarios of paid versus unpaid price.

Core Idea
The agent is believed as trustee if price was paid; otherwise the principal is believed as denier.

When an agent and principal disagree over whether a purchase was made for the principal, the resolution depends on who holds the money. The Hanafi school applies the principle that a trustee (amin) is believed with his oath, while a denier (munkir) is believed when faced with a claim. This lesson unpacks the foundational ruling from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Determine the two conflicting claims: agent says 'I bought it for you', principal says 'You bought it for yourself'. Also note the slave has died, so the loss is at stake.
The death of the slave triggers the need to assign liability.
2
Check who paid the price
The key factual difference is whether the principal had already given the price to the agent (دفع الثمن) or not. This determines who is considered the trustee.
If price paid, agent holds the money as trustee (amin) for the principal.
3
Apply the rule for trustee (amin)
If the principal paid the price, the agent is a trustee; his statement that he bought for the principal is accepted with his oath, because trustees are believed in matters of their trust.
Hanafi maxim: القول للأمين مع اليمين.
4
Apply the rule for denier (munkir)
If the principal did not pay the price, the agent is claiming the price from the principal; the principal denies. The denier's statement is accepted (القول للمنكر).
This is because the burden of proof is on the claimant.
5
Conclude the hukm
In the first case, the agent is believed and bears no liability; the principal bears the loss. In the second case, the principal is believed, so the agent must pay the price or compensate for the slave.
The ruling is about the credibility of statements, not about the actual ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : اشتريت عبداً للآمر فمات، وقال الآمر : بل لنفسك صدق الوكيل إن كان دَفَعَ الأمرُ الثَّمن ، وإلا فالأمر (فإن قال : اشتريت عبداً للأمر، فمات، وقال الآمر : بل لنفسك صدق الوكيل إن كان دَفَعَ الأمرُ الثَّمن ، وإلا فالآمر ) : أي أمر رجلاً بشراء عبد بألف، فقال الوكيل : قد فعلت ومات العبد عندي، وقال الآمر : اشتريت لنفسك، فإن دَفَعَ الأمرُ الثَّمَنَ فالقولُ للوكيل، وإن لم يدفع، فالقول للآمر (1) «التبيين»، وغيره. [١] قوله : فإن قال ... الخ ؛ يعني إن قال الوكيل لمن وكله بشراء عبد: اشتريت لك عبداً فمات ذلك العبد عندي، وقال الموكَّلُ : لا بل اشتريته لنفسك، فالقول للوكيل المأمور إن دفع الآمر الثمن ؛ لأنه أمين، فالقول للأمين مع اليمين، وإن لم يدفع الآمر الثمن إلى الوكيل، فالقول للموكل ؛ لأنّ الوكيل يدَّعي الثمن على الموكل وهو ينكر، فالقول للمنكر. واعلم أنَّ المصنف له قد أجمل هاهنا إجمالاً غير لائق ، وكان عليه أن يفصل، فإنَّ في هذه المسألة ثمانية أوجه، والحكم مختلف، فإنَّ الوكيل : إما أن يكون مأموراً بشراء عبدٍ بعينه، أو بغير عينه. وعلى الوجهين : إما أن يكون الثمن منقوداً، أو غير منقود. وعلى كل وجه : إما أن يكون العبد حيَّاً حين أخبر الوكيل بالشراء، أو ميتاً. فإن كان الوكيل مأموراً بشراء عبدٍ بعينه، فإن أخبر بشرائه، والعبد حق قائم ، فالقول للمأمور إجماعاً سواء كان الثمن منقوداً أو غير منقود ؛ لأنه أخبر عن أمر يملك استئنافه يجعل الشراء للموكل، فإنَّ الوكيل بشراء شيء بعينه لا يملك شراءه لنفسه بمثل ذلك الثمن في حال غيبته والمخبر به في التحقيق والثبوتِ يستغني عن الإشهاد، وهذا كما إذا قال المطلقته : راجعتُك وهي في العدة، وكذبَتْهُ، فإنّ القول له. وإن كان العبد ميتاً حين أخبر ، فقال : هلك عندي بعد الشراء، وأنكر الموكل، فإن كان الثمن غير منقود فالقولُ للموكل الآمر ؛ لأنه أخبر عمَّا لا يملك
"If he (the agent) said: I bought a slave for the commander (principal), and it (the slave) died, and the commander said: Rather for yourself, the agent is believed if the commander paid the price, otherwise the commander (is believed)." Meaning: he commanded a man to buy a slave for a thousand, then the agent said: I have done so and the slave died with me, and the commander said: You bought it for yourself. If the commander paid the price, the statement is the agent's; if he did not pay, the statement is the commander's. (From al-Tabyin, etc.) His saying: 'then he said... etc.' means: if the agent said to the one who appointed him to buy a slave: I bought a slave for you and that slave died with me, and the principal said: No, rather you bought it for yourself, then the statement is the agent's if the principal paid the price, because he is a trustee, so the trustee's statement together with an oath is accepted. If the principal did not pay the price to the agent, then the statement is the principal's because the agent claims the price from the principal while he denies it, and the statement of the denier is accepted. Know that the author has summarized here improperly, and it was upon him to detail, for in this issue there are eight aspects, and the ruling differs. The agent: either he is commanded to buy a specific slave, or not specific. In both cases: either the price is paid (in cash) or not paid. In every case: either the slave is alive when the agent reports the purchase, or dead. If the agent is commanded to buy a specific slave, and he reports his purchase while the slave is still existing, then the statement is the agent's unanimously, whether the price is paid or not, because he reports about a matter that he can independently initiate (i.e., he could make the purchase for the principal). Indeed, the agent for buying a specific thing cannot buy it for himself for such a price in his absence, and the reported matter in reality and establishment dispenses with witnesses. This is like when a man says to his divorced wife: I have taken you back while she is in her waiting period, and she denies it, then his statement is accepted. And if the slave was dead when he reported, and he said: it perished with me after purchase, and the principal denied, then if the price was not paid, the statement is the principal's because he reports about what he does not have the power to...

Commentary
This passage deals with a disputed claim between an agent and principal regarding an agency to buy a slave. The basic rule: if the principal paid the price in advance, the agent is believed (with his oath) because he is a trustee; if the principal did not pay, the principal is believed because the agent is claiming money from him. The commentary then expands: the text was too concise; there are eight scenarios based on: (1) whether the agent was ordered to buy a specific slave or not, (2) whether the price was paid or not, (3) whether the slave was alive or dead at the time of reporting. For a specific slave, if the slave is alive, the agent is believed in all cases; if dead, then the ruling differs. The analogy is given of a man claiming to have revoked his divorce. The passage ends mid-sentence on the page; it continues on the next page.

Key Terms
أمين (amīn): A trustee; one who is entrusted with property; in this context, the agent who received the price is a trustee and his word is accepted.
القول للوكيل (al-qawlu lil-wakīl): The statement (or legal credibility) is given to the agent; i.e., his claim is accepted over the principal's.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the credibility in agency disputes hinges on whether the principal paid the price: the agent is believed as trustee if paid, otherwise the principal is believed as denier.

Eight Scenarios: Detailed Expansion of Agency Disputes
Expansion of the basic ruling into eight case-specific rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the eight scenarios of dispute between agent and principal based on specificity of the object, payment of price, and status of the slave, and derive the ruling for each.

Core Idea
The ruling changes depending on three binary variables: specific/non-specific slave, price paid/not paid, slave alive/dead.

The text criticizes the author for being too concise, noting that there are actually eight distinct cases. These arise from three independent variables: whether the agent was ordered to buy a specific slave or not, whether the price was paid or not, and whether the slave was alive or dead at the time of reporting. This lesson unpacks each scenario and its ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three binary variables
The cases depend on (1) specific vs non-specific slave, (2) price paid (manqud) vs not paid (ghayr manqud), (3) slave alive (hayy) vs dead (mayyit) at the time of agent's report.
This gives 2 x 2 x 2 = 8 cases.
2
Analyze the specific slave, alive sub-case
If the agent was ordered to buy a specific slave and reports the purchase while the slave is alive, the agent is believed unanimously (ijma'an) regardless of payment, because he could have bought it for the principal and has full control.
The text mentions an analogy with divorce revocation.
3
Analyze the specific slave, dead sub-case
If the slave is dead and the price was not paid, the principal is believed because the agent reports about something he did not have the power to do (i.e., he could have bought it for himself). If the price was paid, the agent is believed as trustee.
The text cuts off here; the full ruling can be inferred from the basic principle.
4
Generalize to non-specific slave cases
The text only partially covers specific slave; for non-specific, the ruling likely follows the basic rule: agent believed if price paid (trustee), else principal believed (denier). The living/dead status may affect the assessment of credibility.
The commentary implies the eight cases are needed because the ruling varies.
5
Apply the divorce revocation analogy
The text compares to a man telling his divorced wife 'I have taken you back' while she denies; the husband is believed because he can unilaterally revoke. Similarly, for a specific slave, the agent can unilaterally make the purchase for the principal, so he is believed.
This analogy reinforces the agent's credibility when the slave is alive and specific.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : اشتريت عبداً للآمر فمات، وقال الآمر : بل لنفسك صدق الوكيل إن كان دَفَعَ الأمرُ الثَّمن ، وإلا فالأمر (فإن قال : اشتريت عبداً للأمر، فمات، وقال الآمر : بل لنفسك صدق الوكيل إن كان دَفَعَ الأمرُ الثَّمن ، وإلا فالآمر ) : أي أمر رجلاً بشراء عبد بألف، فقال الوكيل : قد فعلت ومات العبد عندي، وقال الآمر : اشتريت لنفسك، فإن دَفَعَ الأمرُ الثَّمَنَ فالقولُ للوكيل، وإن لم يدفع، فالقول للآمر (1) «التبيين»، وغيره. [١] قوله : فإن قال ... الخ ؛ يعني إن قال الوكيل لمن وكله بشراء عبد: اشتريت لك عبداً فمات ذلك العبد عندي، وقال الموكَّلُ : لا بل اشتريته لنفسك، فالقول للوكيل المأمور إن دفع الآمر الثمن ؛ لأنه أمين، فالقول للأمين مع اليمين، وإن لم يدفع الآمر الثمن إلى الوكيل، فالقول للموكل ؛ لأنّ الوكيل يدَّعي الثمن على الموكل وهو ينكر، فالقول للمنكر. واعلم أنَّ المصنف له قد أجمل هاهنا إجمالاً غير لائق ، وكان عليه أن يفصل، فإنَّ في هذه المسألة ثمانية أوجه، والحكم مختلف، فإنَّ الوكيل : إما أن يكون مأموراً بشراء عبدٍ بعينه، أو بغير عينه. وعلى الوجهين : إما أن يكون الثمن منقوداً، أو غير منقود. وعلى كل وجه : إما أن يكون العبد حيَّاً حين أخبر الوكيل بالشراء، أو ميتاً. فإن كان الوكيل مأموراً بشراء عبدٍ بعينه، فإن أخبر بشرائه، والعبد حق قائم ، فالقول للمأمور إجماعاً سواء كان الثمن منقوداً أو غير منقود ؛ لأنه أخبر عن أمر يملك استئنافه يجعل الشراء للموكل، فإنَّ الوكيل بشراء شيء بعينه لا يملك شراءه لنفسه بمثل ذلك الثمن في حال غيبته والمخبر به في التحقيق والثبوتِ يستغني عن الإشهاد، وهذا كما إذا قال المطلقته : راجعتُك وهي في العدة، وكذبَتْهُ، فإنّ القول له. وإن كان العبد ميتاً حين أخبر ، فقال : هلك عندي بعد الشراء، وأنكر الموكل، فإن كان الثمن غير منقود فالقولُ للموكل الآمر ؛ لأنه أخبر عمَّا لا يملك
"If he (the agent) said: I bought a slave for the commander (principal), and it (the slave) died, and the commander said: Rather for yourself, the agent is believed if the commander paid the price, otherwise the commander (is believed)." Meaning: he commanded a man to buy a slave for a thousand, then the agent said: I have done so and the slave died with me, and the commander said: You bought it for yourself. If the commander paid the price, the statement is the agent's; if he did not pay, the statement is the commander's. (From al-Tabyin, etc.) His saying: 'then he said... etc.' means: if the agent said to the one who appointed him to buy a slave: I bought a slave for you and that slave died with me, and the principal said: No, rather you bought it for yourself, then the statement is the agent's if the principal paid the price, because he is a trustee, so the trustee's statement together with an oath is accepted. If the principal did not pay the price to the agent, then the statement is the principal's because the agent claims the price from the principal while he denies it, and the statement of the denier is accepted. Know that the author has summarized here improperly, and it was upon him to detail, for in this issue there are eight aspects, and the ruling differs. The agent: either he is commanded to buy a specific slave, or not specific. In both cases: either the price is paid (in cash) or not paid. In every case: either the slave is alive when the agent reports the purchase, or dead. If the agent is commanded to buy a specific slave, and he reports his purchase while the slave is still existing, then the statement is the agent's unanimously, whether the price is paid or not, because he reports about a matter that he can independently initiate (i.e., he could make the purchase for the principal). Indeed, the agent for buying a specific thing cannot buy it for himself for such a price in his absence, and the reported matter in reality and establishment dispenses with witnesses. This is like when a man says to his divorced wife: I have taken you back while she is in her waiting period, and she denies it, then his statement is accepted. And if the slave was dead when he reported, and he said: it perished with me after purchase, and the principal denied, then if the price was not paid, the statement is the principal's because he reports about what he does not have the power to...
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage deals with a disputed claim between an agent and principal regarding an agency to buy a slave. The basic rule: if the principal paid the price in advance, the agent is believed (with his oath) because he is a trustee; if the principal did not pay, the principal is believed because the agent is claiming money from him. The commentary then expands: the text was too concise; there are eight scenarios based on: (1) whether the agent was ordered to buy a specific slave or not, (2) whether the price was paid or not, (3) whether the slave was alive or dead at the time of reporting. For a specific slave, if the slave is alive, the agent is believed in all cases; if dead, then the ruling differs. The analogy is given of a man claiming to have revoked his divorce. The passage ends mid-sentence on the page; it continues on the next page.

Key Terms
إجماع (ijmāʿ): Consensus; here, unanimous agreement among scholars that the agent is believed in the case of a specific alive slave.
منقود (manqūd): Paid in cash; the price was given to the agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which case is the agent believed even if the principal did not pay the price?
A) When the slave is non-specific and alive.
B) When the slave is specific and alive.
C) When the slave is specific and dead.
D) When the slave is non-specific and dead.

Key Takeaway
For a specific slave alive at the time of the agent's report, the agent is believed by consensus even without payment; other cases follow the payment-based rule.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
