The Dispute Between Agent and Principal Over Purchase of a Slave: Intra-Hanafi Analysis
Intra-Hanafi dispute over qawl in agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules of qawl (whose statement is accepted) to the eight scenarios of agency dispute and distinguish the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
When the agent can initiate the contract anew, his statement is accepted—unless suspicion taints him.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya expands a brief rule in Al-Wiqaya into eight detailed scenarios. The core question: when agent and principal disagree over who the slave was bought for, whose word is taken? The answer hinges on two variables: trust (the agent as amin) and the ability to recoup (ist'inaf). Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) diverge on one key scenario, revealing how suspicion (tuhma) and initiative interact in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic rule
When the price is paid, the agent is believed as a trustee. When not paid, the principal (denier) is believed unless the agent can initiate the contract anew.
This is the practical starting point.
2
Map the eight scenarios
Recognize the three binary variables: specified/unspecified slave, price paid/unpaid, slave alive/dead at report. Combine them to form eight possibilities.
Each combination potentially has a different ruling.
3
Isolate the disputed scenario
The only scenario where Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ: unspecified slave, alive, price unpaid. This is the 'place of suspicion' (mawdi' al-tuhma).
Both agree on all other seven scenarios.
4
Compare the two Hanafi positions
Imam Abu Hanifa rules for the principal due to suspicion; the Sahibayn rule for the agent because the agent can initiate the contract anew and is a trustee.
The difference lies in how much weight is given to suspicion.
5
Trace the proof structure
The Sahibayn analogize to the specified slave case (qiyas) where the agent is always believed; Imam distinguishes by the presence of suspicion, which is absent in the specified case.
Understanding the analogy clarifies the reasoning.

If he said: I bought a slave for the principal and it died, and the principal said: Rather for yourself, the agent is believed if the principal paid the price, otherwise the principal (is believed). (If he said: I bought a slave for the principal, and it died, and the principal said: Rather for yourself, the agent is believed if the principal paid the price, otherwise the principal): meaning he ordered a man to buy a slave for one thousand, and the agent said: I have done (so) and the slave died with me, and the principal said: You bought it for yourself. If the principal paid the price, the statement is the agent's; if he did not pay, the statement is the principal's. (1) Al-Tabyin (4:268-269). [1] His statement: “If he said... etc.”; meaning if the agent said to the one who appointed him to buy a slave: I bought for you a slave and that slave died with me, and the principal said: No, rather you bought it for yourself, the statement is that of the agent commanded if the principal paid the price, because he is a trustee, so the statement of the trustee is accepted with an oath. If the principal did not pay the price to the agent, the statement is that of the principal, because the agent claims the price from the principal and he denies it, so the statement of the denier is accepted. Know that the author has summarized here in an unsuitable manner, and he should have detailed, for in this issue there are eight aspects, and the ruling differs. For the agent: either he is commanded to buy a specific slave or an unspecified one. In both cases: either the price is paid or unpaid. In every case: either the slave was alive when the agent informed of the purchase or dead. If the agent was commanded to buy a specific slave, and he informed of its purchase while the slave was still existing, the statement is the agent's by consensus, whether the price was paid or not, because he informed of a matter that he can initiate anew making the purchase for the principal, for the agent buying a specific thing cannot buy it for himself with the same price in his absence, and the informant in reality and certainty dispenses with witnesses. This is like when a man says to his divorced wife: I have taken you back while she is in her waiting period, and she denies it, then his statement is accepted. If the slave was dead when he informed and he said: It perished with me after purchase, and the principal denied it, then if the price was unpaid, the statement is the principal's, because he informed of something he does not have the power to initiate. The Cream of the End for the Support of Care. [1] And he gave the reason in Al-Hidayah for when the principal did not pay the price: that the agent informed of a matter he cannot initiate anew, and for when the price was paid: that the agent is a trustee seeking to discharge the trust. Because the dead is not a locus for initiating a contract, and his aim is to claim the price from the principal, who denies it, so the statement is his. If the price was paid, then the statement of the commanded agent is accepted with his oath, because the price was a trust in his hand, and he claimed to have discharged the trust in the manner he was ordered, so his statement is accepted. If the slave was unspecified and alive, and the agent said: I bought it for you, and the principal said: No, you bought it for yourself, then if the price was paid, the statement is the agent's, because he is informing of something he can initiate anew; if unpaid, the statement is the principal's according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and according to the two companions: the statement is the agent's because he informed of something he can initiate anew, so it is valid, as in the specific case. And for him (Imam) it is a place of suspicion, that he bought it for himself, and if he sees the bargain is unfavorable, he imposes it on the principal. Unlike the case where the price was paid, because he is a trustee regarding it, so his statement about discharging the trust is accepted, and implicitly the slave belongs to the principal as a subordinate, and many things are established implicitly and subordinately even if not established directly. And unlike the case where the slave is specified, because he cannot buy it for himself as previously. The summary: if the price was paid, the statement is the agent's absolutely; if unpaid, then if the agent cannot initiate the contract (because the slave is dead), the statement is the principal's; if he can initiate, then if it is not a place of suspicion, the statement is the agent's by agreement; if it is a place of suspicion, the statement is also the agent's according to the two companions, and according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) it is the principal's. Mentioned by al-Zayla'i and al-Ayni and others. (1) In Tabyin al-Haqa'iq (4:265-266). (2) Ramz al-Haqa'iq (2:123). (3) Al-Hidayah (3:142).
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a dispute between an agent (wakil) and principal (amir/muwakkil) over a purchase. The text, from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, expands on the matn of Al-Wiqaya. The issue: an agent claims he bought a slave for the principal, and the slave died; the principal denies and says the agent bought it for himself. The general rule: if the principal had paid the price to the agent, the agent's statement is believed (with an oath) because he is a trustee (amin) and claims to have discharged the trust. If the principal did not pay, the principal's statement is believed because the agent is claiming the price from the principal, who denies it; so the statement of the denier is accepted. The commentator then notes eight scenarios based on whether the slave was specified or unspecified, whether the price was paid or not, and whether the slave was alive or dead when the agent informed. The key principle is whether the agent 'can initiate the contract anew' (yamliku al-ist'inaf). If the agent can still make the purchase for himself (e.g., the slave is alive and not specified), there is suspicion (tuhma) that he might be acting in his own interest. The positions: Imam Abu Hanifa considers the place of suspicion and rules in favor of the principal when the price is unpaid and the slave is unspecified and alive. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) rule in favor of the agent in that case because the agent could initiate the contract for the principal. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position according to later Hanafi scholars is that of the two companions (the Sahibayn) in many cases, but the text does not explicitly state the final tarjih. The passage also analogizes to the case of a husband taking back his divorced wife, where he is believed because he can initiate the action.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakil): an agent appointed to perform a task (here, buying a slave) on behalf of the principal
آمر / موكل (amir / muwakkil): the principal who appoints the agent; the person who gives the order to buy
ثمن منقود (thaman manqud): the price that has been paid (delivered) by the principal to the agent
موضع التهمة (mawdi' al-tuhma): a situation where the agent is suspected of acting in his own interest rather than the principal's

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which scenario is the agent always believed by consensus regardless of price paid or not?
A) Specific slave, alive at report
B) Unspecified slave, alive at report
C) Unspecified slave, dead at report
D) Specific slave, dead at report

Key Takeaway
The acceptance of the agent's statement depends on whether he is a trustee (price paid) or a claimant (price unpaid), and when suspicion arises, the principal's statement gains weight unless the agent can initiate the contract anew.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Dispute Between Agent and Principal: Who Is Believed When Buying a Slave?
Analysis of dispute rulings with conditions of payment and slave specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whose statement is accepted in an agent-principal dispute over slave purchase, and apply the conditions of payment and slave specification to determine the outcome.

Core Idea
Whose statement is accepted depends on payment and specification; suspicion (tuhma) shifts credibility.

When an agent claims he bought a slave for his principal and the principal denies, the court must decide who to believe. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling from Al-Hidayah, which varies based on whether the price was paid (manqud) or unpaid, and whether the slave was specified (mu'ayyan) or generic. We also examine an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples regarding the generic, unpaid case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
The agent says 'I bought it for you,' the principal says 'You bought it for yourself.' The question: whose statement is accepted in court?
This is a judicial credibility ruling, not a hukm taklifi.
2
Examine the first factor: payment status
If the price was paid (manqud), the agent is a trustee (amin) and his statement is accepted with oath. If unpaid, the situation is more complex.
Payment eliminates suspicion because the agent claims to have discharged a trust.
3
Examine the second factor: specification of the slave
If the slave is specified (mu'ayyan), the agent cannot buy it for himself, so no suspicion arises. If generic (ghayr mu'ayyan), the agent could have bought for himself, creating tuhma.
Specification removes the possibility of self-dealing.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
When the price is unpaid and the slave is generic and alive, Abu Hanifa says the principal's statement is accepted (due to tuhma), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the agent's statement is accepted (because the agent reports on an act he can initiate).
This difference arises from weighing suspicion vs. the agent's ability to perform the act.
5
Synthesize the summary (al-hasil)
If price paid, agent's statement always accepted. If unpaid and slave dead, principal's statement accepted. If unpaid and slave alive: if no suspicion (e.g., specified), agent's statement by agreement; if suspicion (generic), Abu Hanifa favors principal, Sahibayn favor agent.
The commentator adds that the underlying principle is that the denier's statement is accepted in debt claims.

وعلل في «الهداية» فيما إذا لم يدفع الآمرُ الثَّمَنَ : بأن الوكيل أخبر بأمرٍ لا يملك استئنافه ، وفيما إذا دفعَ الثَّمن بأن الوكيل أمين يريدُ الخروج عن عهدة الأمانة استئنافه ؛ لأنَّ الميت ليس بمحل لإنشاء العقد فيه، وغرضه أن يدعي على الموكل بالثمن، وهو منكر، فيكون القولُ له، وإن كان الثمن منقوداً، فالقول للوكيل المأمور مع يمينه ؛ لأنَّ الثمن كان أمانة في يده، وقد ادَّعى الخروج عن عهدة الأمانة من الوجه الذي أمره به، فيكون القولُ له. وإن كان العبد بغير عينه، فإن كان حيّاً، فقال المأمور: اشتريته لك، وقال الآمر : لا بل اشتريته لنفسك، فإن كان الثمن منقوداً ، فالقولُ للمأمور ؛ لأنه مخبرٌ عمَّا يملك استئنافه، وإن لم يكن منقوداً فالقولُ للآمر عند الإمام، وعندهما : يكون القــولُ للمأمور ؛ لأنه أخبر عما يملك استئنافه، فصح، كما في المعين، وله أنه موضع تهمة ، بأنه اشتراه لنفسه، فإذا رأى الصفقة خاسرة ألزمها الأمر. بخلاف ما إذا كان الثمن منقوداً ؛ لأنه أمين فيه، فيقبل قوله في الخروج عن العهدة، وفي ضمنه يكون العبد للآمر تبعاً، وكم من شيء يثبتُ ضِمْناً وتَبَعاً، وإن لم يثبت قصداً، وبخلاف ما إذا كان العبد معيّناً ؛ لأنه لا يملك أن يشتريه لنفسه كما تقدّم. والحاصل إنَّ الثمن إن كان منقوداً فالقول للمأمور مطلقاً، وإن كان غير منقودٍ فإن كان الوكيل لا يملك الإنشاء بأن يكون العبد ميتاً فالقول للأمر، وإن كان يملك الإنشاء، فإن كان في غير موضع التهمة فالقول للمأمور اتفاقاً، وإن كان في موضع التهمة فالقولُ أيضاً للمأمور عندهما، وعنده للأمر، ذكره الزَّيْلَعِيُّ والعَيْنِي وغيرهما. وله الرجوع بالثَّمن على الآمرِ دَفَعَهُ إلى بائعِهِ أو لا ، وله حبس المبيع من آمره لقبض ثمنه ، وإن لم يدفع أقولُ : كل واحد من التعليلين شامل للصورتين، فلا يتم به الفرق، بل لا بد من انضمام أمر آخر وهو أن يكون الوكيل فيما إذا لم يدفع الثَّمَنَ يدعي الثَّمَنَ على الأمر، وهو ينكره، فالقولُ للمنكر، وفيما إذا دَفَعَ الثَّمَنَ يدَّعي الأمرُ الثَّمَنَ على المأمور وهو يُنْكِرُه ، فالقول للمنكر.
And he explained in Al-Hidayah regarding when the principal did not pay the price: that the agent reported about a matter he cannot initiate [for himself], and regarding when he paid the price: that the agent is a trustee who wants to exit from the trust obligation. [For the first case] because the dead [slave] is not a subject for initiating the contract, and his purpose is to claim the price from the principal, and he denies, so the statement is his. And if the price was paid in cash, the statement is of the agent with his oath, because the price was a trust in his hand, and he claimed to have exited from the trust in the manner he was ordered, so the statement is his. And if the slave is unspecified, and he is alive, and the agent said: I bought it for you, and the principal said: No, rather you bought it for yourself, then if the price was paid in cash, the statement is of the agent; because he reports about what he can initiate. And if it was not paid in cash, the statement is of the principal according to Imam [Abu Hanifa], and according to them [the two disciples], the statement is of the agent; because he reports about what he can initiate, so it is valid, as in the case of the specified [slave]. And he [Abu Hanifa] has [the reasoning] that it is a place of suspicion, because he bought it for himself, so when he sees the deal is bad, he imposes it on the principal. Unlike when the price was paid in cash, because he is a trustee in it, so his statement is accepted regarding exiting the trust, and implicitly the slave belongs to the principal as a consequence, and many things are established implicitly as a consequence even if not established explicitly. And unlike when the slave is specified, because he cannot buy it for himself as previously stated. The summary: if the price was paid in cash, the statement is of the agent absolutely. If it was not paid in cash, then if the agent cannot initiate [the purchase] because the slave is dead, the statement is of the principal. If he can initiate, then if it is not a place of suspicion, the statement is of the agent by agreement. If it is a place of suspicion, the statement is also of the agent according to them [the two disciples], and according to him [Abu Hanifa] it is of the principal. Al-Zayla'i and Al-Ayni and others mentioned this. And he [the agent] has the right to reclaim the price from the principal whether he paid it to his seller or not, and he has the right to withhold the merchandise from his principal for receipt of his price, even if he did not pay. I say: each of the two explanations covers both cases, so the distinction is not complete; rather, another matter must be added: that the agent, when he did not pay the price, claims the debt from the principal, and he denies it, so the statement is that of the denier; and when he paid the price, the principal claims the price from the agent, and he denies it, so the statement is that of the denier.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on disputes between an agent (wakil) appointed to buy a slave and the principal (amir). It comes from Al-Hidayah and is expanded upon by the commentator. The central issue: who is believed when the agent claims he bought for the principal, and the principal claims he bought for himself? The ruling depends on two conditions: (1) whether the price was actually paid (manqud) or not, and (2) whether the slave is specific (mu'ayyan) or generic (ghayr mu'ayyan). The author first discusses the case where the slave was specific but has died. If the price was not paid, the agent's statement is not accepted because he is reporting on a matter he cannot initiate (the dead slave cannot be purchased anew). If the price was paid, the agent is considered a trustee, and his statement is accepted with an oath because he claims to have discharged the trust. Then the discussion moves to a generic, living slave. If the price was paid, the agent's statement is accepted because he reports on something he can initiate (he could buy another slave). If the price was not paid, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa says the principal's statement is accepted because the agent is in a position of suspicion—he might have bought for himself and then attribute a bad deal to the principal. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) say the agent's statement is accepted because he reports on what he can initiate, just as in the specified case. The author then summarizes: if the price is paid, the agent's statement is always accepted. If not paid, it depends on whether the agent can initiate and whether there is suspicion. Finally, the commentator adds his own analysis: the two explanations (agent cannot initiate vs. agent is a trustee) each cover both cases, so the real distinction is that when the price is not paid, the agent claims a debt from the principal (who denies), so the denier's statement is accepted; and when paid, the principal claims from the agent (who denies), so again the denier prevails. This clarifies the rulings.

Key Terms
استئناف (isti'naf): Initiating a contract; here, the agent's ability to commence a new purchase on his own behalf.
منقود (manqud): The price that has been paid in cash by the agent to the seller.
عين (ayn): Specified or determined; here, a specific slave (mu'ayyan) as opposed to a generic (ghayr mu'ayyan) one.
تهمة (tuhma): Suspicion; the possibility that the agent may act dishonestly for personal gain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the summary (al-hasil) in the passage, when is the agent's statement accepted absolutely?
A) When the price is unpaid and the slave is generic.
B) When the price is paid in cash (manqud).
C) When the slave is dead.
D) When the principal denies.

Key Takeaway
In disputes over agent-principal purchases, credibility hinges on payment status and specification, with an intra-Hanafi disagreement when both suspicion and agent's ability to initiate coexist.

Agent's Right to Reclaim Price and Withhold Merchandise
Explanation of agent's right based on constructive exchange

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the basis of the agent's right to demand the price and withhold the merchandise using the concept of constructive exchange (mubadala hukmiyya).

Core Idea
The agent becomes a seller by legal fiction, entitling him to the price and a lien on the goods.

In Hanafi agency law, a purchase by an agent is not a simple two-party transaction. Through the concept of 'mubadala hukmiyya' (constructive exchange), the agent is legally treated as a seller to his principal. This gives the agent powerful rights: he can demand the price from the principal even before paying the original seller, and he can withhold the merchandise until paid. This lesson unpacks that legal fiction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Agent's Rights
From the passage, list the two specific rights the agent has: reclaiming the price from the principal and withholding the merchandise.
2
Understand the Legal Fiction
The passage states these rights are based on a 'constructive exchange' (mubadala hukmiyya) between agent and principal. This fiction treats the agent as a seller to the principal.
This fiction is unique to Hanafi fiqh in agency.
3
Apply the Fiction to Price Demand
Even if the agent hasn't paid the original seller, he can demand the price from the principal because in the fiction he sold the goods to the principal and is entitled to the price.
The timing of payment to the seller is irrelevant.
4
Analyze the Lien (Habs)
The agent can withhold the merchandise until the principal pays, just as a seller has a lien until payment. This is a direct consequence of the constructive exchange.
This right exists even if the agent hasn't paid the seller.
5
Distinguish the Trustee Explanation
The commentator points out that the 'trustee seeking to exit trust' explanation only applies when the agent has the price in hand; if not paid, the constructive exchange is the sole basis.
Understand why the trustee explanation is limited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله الرجوع بالثَّمن على الآمرِ دَفَعَهُ إلى بائعه أو لا ) : أي للوكيل بالشراء الرجوع بالثَّمن على الآمر إذا فعل ما أمره به سواء دفع الوكيلُ الثَّمنَ إلى بائعه، أو لم يدفعه، جعلوا هذه المسألة مبنية على أنه يجري بين الوكيل والموكل مبادلة حكميّة ، فيصير الوكيل بائعاً من موكله ، فله مطالبةُ الثَّمن ، وإِن لم يَدْفَعْ إلى بائعه. وله حبس المبيع من آمره لقبض ثمنه ، وإن لم يدفع) : بناء على ما ذكرنا من المبادلة الحكمية الآمر، فإن وقع إليه الألف، فالقولُ قولُ المأمور ؛ لأنَّ في الوجه الأوّل أخبرَ عمَّا لا يملك استئنافه، وهو الرجوع بالثمن على الآمر ، وهو ينكر، والـقـول للمنكر، وفي الوجه الثاني هو أمين يريد الخروج عن عهدة الأمانة، فيقبل قوله. انتهى. 11 ] قوله : شامل للصورتين ؛ لا يخفى عليك أنَّ التعليل الثاني لا يشمل صورة عدم دفع الثمن ؛ لأنه لا ثمن في يد الوكيل حتى يكون أميناً. [٢] قوله : أي للوكيل ...الخ ؛ يعني أنَّ الوكيل له طلب الثمن من موكله إذا اشترى وقبض المبيع، سواء دفع الوكيل الثمن إلى بائعه أو لا. (۳) قوله : مبنية على أنه...الخ ؛ ولهذا لو اختلفا في الثمن يتحالفان ، ويرد الموكل على الوكيل بالعيب، فيصير الوكيل بائعاً من موكله حكماً، فيطلب الثمن من موكله ، سواء دفعه إلى بائعه أو لا. (٤) قوله : وله حبس المبيع ... الخ ؛ أي للوكيل بالشراء حبس [ما] اشتراه من موكله ؛ لأنه يقبض ثمن المبيع، وإن لم يدفع الثمن إلى بائعه لما عرفت من أن المبادلة الحكمية تجري بينهما.
And he has the right to reclaim the price from the principal, whether he paid it to his seller or not: that is, for the agent to reclaim the price from the principal if he did what he was ordered, whether the agent paid the price to his seller or did not pay it. They based this issue on the fact that a constructive exchange occurs between the agent and the principal, so the agent becomes a seller to his principal, and therefore he can demand the price even if he did not pay it to his seller. And he has the right to withhold the merchandise from his principal for receipt of his price, even if he did not pay: based on what we mentioned of the constructive exchange; the principal, if the thousand [dirhams] came to him, the statement is of the agent; because in the first case he reported about what he cannot initiate, and he has the right to reclaim the price from the principal, and he denies, so the statement is of the denier; and in the second case, he is a trustee seeking to exit the trust obligation, so his statement is accepted. End. [11] His statement: covering both cases; it is not hidden from you that the second explanation does not cover the case of non-payment of the price, because there is no price in the agent's hand for him to be a trustee. [2] His statement: that is, for the agent... etc.; meaning that the agent has the right to demand the price from his principal when he buys and takes possession of the merchandise, whether the agent paid the price to his seller or not. (3) His statement: based on that... etc.; and for this reason, if they differ on the price, they both take an oath, and the principal returns the defective merchandise to the agent, so the agent becomes a seller to his principal by legal fiction, and he demands the price from his principal, whether he paid it to his seller or not. (4) His statement: and he has the right to withhold the merchandise... etc.; that is, for the agent to withhold what he bought from his principal in order to receive the price of the merchandise, even if he did not pay the price to his seller, because you know that the constructive exchange occurs between them.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage explains the legal consequences of the agent's purchase using the concept of 'mubadala hukmiyya' (constructive exchange). The commentators clarify that when an agent buys an item as instructed, a legal fiction treats the transaction as if the agent first buys from the seller and then sells to the principal. This gives the agent the right to demand the price from his principal immediately, even if he hasn't paid the original seller. Furthermore, the agent can withhold the merchandise until the principal pays him. The basis is that the agent is a trustee of the price, but once he buys, the trust shifts to the merchandise. The passage also addresses a subtle criticism: the initial explanation that the agent is a trustee seeking to exit the trust applies only when he has the price in hand; when he hasn't paid, there is no price to be a trustee over. Therefore, the commentator adds that the distinction must be based on who is the claimant and who is the denier. The passage also mentions that if the principal and agent dispute the price, they both take oaths, and the principal can return the item for defects, reinforcing the seller-buyer relationship between them.

Key Terms
مبادلة حكمية (mubadala hukmiyya): A legal fiction by which the agent is treated as a seller to the principal, allowing him to demand the price and withhold the goods.
حبس المبيع (habs al-mabee'): The right of the agent to retain the purchased item until the principal pays the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The agency contract itself.
B) A legal fiction of constructive exchange (mubadala hukmiyya) between agent and principal.
C) The principal's permission.
D) The seller's consent.

Key Takeaway
The agent's right to price and lien on goods stems from the legal fiction of constructive exchange, not actual payment.

Destruction of Purchased Item in Agent's Hand: Liability Before and After Detention
Ruling on destruction of purchased item in agent's hand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the destruction of a purchased item in the agent's hand before and after detention, and explain the three intra-Hanafi positions on the nature of the agent's guarantee.

Core Idea
After detention, the agent's liability mirrors a seller, not a pledgee—the price drops, freeing the principal.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah addresses a critical scenario in agency sales: what happens if the purchased item is destroyed after the agent detains it to secure the price? The answer hinges on whether the detention transforms the agent's possession from that of a deputy to that of a de facto seller. The text presents three views within the Hanafi school and defends the majority position using the concept of a virtual exchange (mubadalah hukmiyyah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Scenarios
Note that the ruling distinguishes between destruction before detention (principal bears loss, price remains due) and after detention (price drops, principal freed). Understand the basic rule as stated in the matn.
2
Distinguish the Three Hanafi Positions on Guarantee
After detention, three views: (1) Abu Hanifa/Muhammad: seller guarantee (price drops). (2) Abu Yusuf: pledge guarantee (liable for lesser of value and price). (3) Zufar: no right to detain, full value guarantee.
Abu Yusuf's position is intermediate.
3
Analyze Zufar's Objection and the Hanafi Response
Zufar argues the agent is a deputy, so no right to detain; detention makes him a transgressor. The response posits a virtual exchange (mubadalah hukmiyyah) between agent and principal, giving the agent a seller-like right.
Key evidence: return for defect, mutual imprecation, and undivided half share.
4
Evaluate the Proof for Virtual Exchange
The analogies (رد بالعيب, تحالف) show the relationship is sale-like. Also, detention can be for the agent's own right, not just the principal's.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi and Its Practical Consequence
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: price drops. Thus, after detention, the principal pays nothing and the agent bears the loss.
This is the ruling in Al-Wiqayah and the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن هلك في يده قبل حبسه منه هلك على الآمر ولم يسقط ثمنه، وبعد حبسه منه سقط، فإنه إذا حبسه عن الآمر لقبض الثمن، فهلك في يد الوكيل يكون مضموناً على الوكيل، ثم اختلف فيه : فعند أبي يوسف يضمن ضمان الرهن، وعند محمد وهو قول أبي حنيفة يضمن ضمان المبيع، فما ذكر في «المتن» من سقوط الثمن إشارة إلى هذا المذهب، وعند زفر يضمن ضمان الغصب إذ عنده ليس له حق الحبس. فإن كان الثمن مساوياً للقيمة فلا اختلاف وإن كان الثمن عشرة، والقيمة خمسة عشر، فعند زفر يضمن خمسة عشر، وعند الباقين يضمن عشرة، وإن كان بالعكس فعند زفر يضمن عشرة، فيطالب الخمسة من الموكل. وقال زفر: ليس له الحبس؛ لأن الوكيل نائب عن موكله، فتقوم يده مقام يد الموكل، ويكون قبضه قبض الموكل، ولا يحبس المبيع بعد القبض؛ لأن المبيع أمانة في يده، وليس للأمين حبس الأمانة بدين له على صاحبها. قلنا: إن الموكل قد ملك المبيع بعقد باشره الوكيل، ببدل استوجبه عليه، وهذا معنى البيع فيحبسه به كما لو باعه إياه حقيقة، وقد تقدم أن بينهما مبادلة حكمية؛ ولهذا يرده الموكل عن الوكيل بعيب، ويجري التحالف بينهما عند الاختلاف في الثمن، وهذا من خصائص البيع، فكذا هذا، ولا نسلم أن قبضه قبض الموكل، بل قبضه يجوز أن يكون لإحياء حق نفسه، ويجوز أن يكون بتتميم مقصود الموكل. فيتبين في الآخرة بحبسه أن القبض كان لحقه، وبعدم الحبس كان للموكل، وقبل ذلك الأمر موقوف، فلا يحكم عليه بشيء؛ ولأن هذا القبض لا يمكن التحرز عنه، إذ لا يقدر على القبض على وجه لا يصير الموكل به قابضاً، وما لا يمكن التحرز عنه يكون عفواً، فلا يسقط به حقه من غير رضاه، وفي سقوطه ضرر عليه. هكذا قالوا. قوله: فإن هلك في يده.... الخ؛ يعني أن المشترى إن هلك في يد الوكيل قبل أن يحبسه من موكله يهلك على مال الموكل لا الوكيل، ولا يسقط ثمن المبيع عن الموكل، فيرجع الوكيل عليه؛ لأن يده كيد الموكل، فإذا لم يحبس يصير الموكل قابضاً بيده. وإن هلك المشترى في يد الوكيل بعد حبسه إياه سقط الثمن عند الطرفين؛ لأنه بمنزلة البائع منه، وكان حبسه لاستيفاء الثمن، فيسقط بهلاكه، وعند أبي يوسف، هو كالرهن فيكون مضموناً بالأقل من قيمته ومن الدين؛ لأنه مضمون بالحبس للاستيفاء بعد أن لم يكن مضموناً به وهذا هو معنى الرهن بخلاف المبيع؛ لأن البيع ينفسخ بهلاكه، وهاهنا لا ينفسخ أصل العقد. قلنا: إن بينهما مبادلة حكمية، كما بيناه سابقاً، فيكون معتبراً بالمبادلة، وهو البيع، ولا نسلم أن العقد لا ينفسخ بل ينفسخ بينهما، وإن لم ينفسخ في حق البائع كما لو رضي الوكيل بالعيب ولم يرض به الموكل ينفسخ العقد بينهما، وإن لم ينفسخ في حق البائع، والدليل على أنه ليس كالرهن أنه يثبت في النصف الشائع، والحبس بحكم الرهن لا يثبت فيه. وعند أبي يوسف له يهلك بالأقل من قيمته ومن الثمن، حتى لو كان الثمن أكثر من قيمته رجع الوكيل بذلك الفضل على موكله، وعند زفر له يضمن جميع قيمته؛ لأنه ليس له أن يحبسه عنده، فبالحبس يكون متعدياً كالمودع الذي يمنع الوديعة عن صاحبها، وإذا تأملت فيما قررناه سابقاً ظهر لك الجواب عما استدل به زفر. وكذا عند أبي يوسف له؛ لأن الرهن يضمن بأقل من قيمته ومن الدين، وعند محمد له يكون مضمونا بالثمن، وهو خمسة عشر.
If it perishes in his hand before he detains it from him, it perishes on the orderer and its price does not drop, and after his detention from him, it drops. Because if he detains it from the orderer to receive the price, and it perishes in the agent's hand, it becomes guaranteed upon the agent. Then there is disagreement: according to Abu Yusuf, he guarantees the guarantee of a pledge; according to Muhammad, which is the saying of Abu Hanifa, he guarantees the guarantee of the sold item. What is mentioned in the text regarding the dropping of the price indicates this school. According to Zufar, he guarantees the guarantee of usurpation, since with him he does not have the right of detention. If the price equals the value, there is no disagreement. If the price is ten and the value is fifteen, according to Zufar he guarantees fifteen, and according to the rest he guarantees ten. If vice versa, according to Zufar he guarantees ten, and he demands the five from the principal. Zufar said: He does not have the right of detention, because the agent is a deputy for his principal, so his hand stands in place of the principal's hand, and his taking possession is the principal's taking possession, and he does not detain the sold item after possession, because the sold item is a trust in his hand, and a trustee does not detain the trust for a debt owed to him by its owner. We replied: The principal indeed owned the sold item by a contract that the agent himself contracted, in exchange for a consideration that became obligatory upon him. This is the meaning of sale, so he detains it thereby as if he had actually sold it to him. It has preceded that between them there is a virtual exchange; for this reason the principal returns it to the agent due to a defect, and mutual imprecation occurs between them when differing over the price, and this is from the characteristics of sale. So likewise this. And we do not concede that his possession is the principal's possession. Rather, his possession is permissible to be for reviving his own right, and permissible to be for completing the principal's purpose. In the end, by his detention it becomes clear that the possession was for his right, and by lack of detention it was for the principal, and before that the matter is suspended, so nothing is judged upon him. And because this possession cannot be avoided, since he is not able to take possession in a manner that does not make the principal a possessor through it, and what cannot be avoided is overlooked, so his right does not drop by it without his consent, and in its dropping there is harm upon him. Thus they said. His saying: 'If it perishes in his hand...' etc. Means that the purchased item, if it perishes in the agent's hand before he detains it from his principal, perishes on the principal's wealth, not the agent, and the price of the sold item does not drop from the principal, so the agent recourse upon him; because his hand is like the principal's hand, so if he does not detain, the principal becomes a possessor through his hand. And if the purchased item perishes in the agent's hand after his detention of it, the price drops according to both parties; because he is in the position of the seller of it, and his detention was to collect the price, so it drops by its perishing. According to Abu Yusuf, it is like a pledge, so it is guaranteed by the lesser of its value and the debt; because it is guaranteed by detention for collection after it was not guaranteed by it, and this is the meaning of a pledge, unlike the sold item; because the sale is annulled by its perishing, and here the original contract is not annulled. We replied: Between them there is a virtual exchange, as we clarified earlier, so it is considered as an exchange, which is the sale, and we do not concede that the contract is not annulled; rather it is annulled between them, even if it is not annulled vis-à-vis the seller. Just as if the agent accepts the defect and the principal does not accept it, the contract is annulled between them, even if not annulled vis-à-vis the seller. And the evidence that it is not like a pledge is that it is established in an undivided half, and detention by virtue of a pledge is not established in it. According to Abu Yusuf, it perishes by the lesser of its value and the price, so if the price is more than its value, the agent recourse for that excess upon his principal. According to Zufar, he guarantees its entire value; because he does not have the right to detain it with him, so by detention he becomes a transgressor like the depositary who withholds the deposit from its owner. And if you reflect on what we have established earlier, the answer to what Zufar argued becomes clear to you. And likewise according to Abu Yusuf, because the pledge is guaranteed by the lesser of its value and the debt, and according to Muhammad it is guaranteed by the price, which is fifteen.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah regarding the fate of the purchased item (المشترى) when it is destroyed in the hands of the agent (الوكيل) after the sale. The agent has the right to detain the item to secure payment of the price from the principal (الموكل). The passage distinguishes two scenarios: (1) if the item perishes before the agent detains it, the loss falls on the principal and the price remains due; (2) if it perishes after detention, the price drops (i.e., the principal is freed from paying). However, there is an intra-madhab dispute on the nature of the detention. According to Abu Yusuf, the agent's detention makes him like a pledgee (مرتهن), so the item is guaranteed by the lesser of its value or the debt. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the detention gives the agent a status akin to a seller (بائع), so the sale is effectively annulled and the price drops. According to Zufar, the agent has no right to detain at all, so detention is a transgression (تعدي) and he is liable for the full value. The text defends the position that the agent's right to detain is analogous to a sale (مبادلة حكمية) and thus the price drops. The commentary also addresses Zufar's objections and provides counterarguments, emphasizing that the agent's possession is not purely for the principal but also for his own right to collect the price.

Key Terms
حبس (habs): Detention of the purchased item by the agent to secure payment of the price from the principal.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee; here it refers to the agent's financial responsibility for the item if it is destroyed.
مبادلة حكمية (mubadalah hukmiyyah): A virtual exchange or legal exchange that is deemed to have occurred between the agent and principal, even though no actual sale took place between them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, what happens to the price if the purchased item is destroyed in the agent's hand after he detains it from the principal?
A) The price is reduced to the value of the item
B) The price drops completely and the principal does not pay
C) The agent must pay the full price to the principal
D) The principal pays the price and the agent is free

Key Takeaway
After the agent detains the purchased item, its destruction frees the principal from the price, based on the virtual exchange between agent and principal.

Agent's Addition to Specific vs Absolute Price: Intention or Payment?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent's purchase with absolute price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding ownership when an agent buys with an absolute price without intention, and apply the respective rules.

Core Idea
When an agent buys with an absolute price and no intention, Imam Muhammad gives it to him; Abu Yusuf follows the money.

This passage deals with a common agency scenario: an agent is told to buy something with a specified amount of money. But what if the agent does not specify whom he is buying for? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on how to resolve this when there is no clear evidence of intention. This lesson examines the positions of Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, their reasoning, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two price scenarios
Distinguish between a specific price (e.g., 'this gold coin') and an absolute price (e.g., 'this money' without further detail). The ruling differs based on this distinction.
Specific price: purchase belongs to the owner of that price. Absolute price: further analysis needed.
2
Analyze absolute price sub-scenarios
For an absolute price that is prompt, there are three possibilities: (1) both agree on whose intention it was, (2) they differ on intention, (3) both agree no intention was present. Focus on scenario (3) where the dispute arises.
Scenarios (1) and (2) are resolved by agreement or payment respectively.
3
State Imam Muhammad's position and analogy
Imam Muhammad holds that the purchase belongs to the agent (contracting party) because transactions are attributed to the doer. He analogizes to a proxy for Hajj who fails to intend on behalf of the principal; the Hajj counts as his own.
Key difference: intention is a condition in worship but not in transactions.
4
State Abu Yusuf's position and evidence
Abu Yusuf holds that payment (naqd) determines ownership: the purchase goes to the one whose money was used. Since the absolute price leaves the transaction ambiguous, payment resolves the ambiguity.
Payment indicates the agent's real intent, overriding the absence of explicit intention.
5
Understand the underlying legal principle
The disagreement stems from whether intention or outward action (payment) should be decisive in contracts (mu'amalat). Imam Muhammad prioritizes the natural attribution of contracts to the agent; Abu Yusuf prioritizes the evidence of payment as a stronger indicator.
Both views are valid within the Hanafi school; the mufta bihi depends on context and later scholars' preferences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أضافه إلى ثمن معيَّن كان الشراء لصاحب ذلك الثمن؛ لأنَّ الظاهر أنه يضيف الشراء إلى مالِ مَن يشتريه له، وهذا لأنَّ الثمن وإن كان لا يتعيَّن، لكن فيه شبهة التعين من حيث سلامة المبيع به وتعين قدره ووصفه؛ ولهذا لا يطيب له الربح إذا اشترى بالدراهم المغصوبة ودينه يمنعه من أن يشتريه لنفسه بإضافة العقد إلى مال غيره؛ لأنَّ ذلك مستنكر شرعاً وعادةً فلا يرتكبه. وقد جرى العرف فيما إذا اشترى لنفسه أن يضيف العقد إلى ماله، وهو ليس بمستنكر شرعاً وعرفاً، فيكون المشترى لمالك ذلك الثمن، وإن نوى خلاف ذلك جرياً على مقتضى العرف والشرع، ثمَّ إذا نقد من مال الموكل فيما اشتراه لنفسه يجب عليه الضمان. وإن أضافه إلى ثمن مطلق، فلا يخلو: إما أن يكون حالاً أو مؤجّلاً. فإن كان حالا فلا يخلو إمّا أن يتصادقا على وجود النية لأحدهما، أو على عدمها، أو يختلفا فيه. فإن كان الثمن حالاً واتَّفقا على وجودِ النيَّة لأحدهما كان من نوى له؛ لأنَّ الثابت باتفاقهما كالثابت عياناً، واتفاقهما حجّة عليهما، ولا يلتفت إلى النقد، ولا عبرة به في هذه الحالة؛ لأنَّ له أن يشتري لنفسه وللموكل، فإذا عينه بنية فقد تعيَّن، ويكون بالنقد من مال الموكل غاصباً فيما إذا نواه لنفسه. وإن اختلفا في النية يُحَكِّمُ النقد بالإجماع؛ لأنَّ دلالته على التعيين مثل دلالته إضافة الشراء إليه؛ لأنَّ الظاهر أن يفعل ما يجوز له شرعاً، أو يجري على عوائده. وإن اتَّفقا على أنه لم تحضره النيّة، فعند محمّد الله هو ا للعاقد؛ لأنّ ما يطلقه الإنسان من التصرفات يكون لنفسه، فصار كالمأمور بالحج إذا أطلق ولم ينو أنـه للمحجوج عنه، وعند أبي يوسف يُحَكِّمُ النقد؛ لأنَّ المطلق يحتمل، فبقي موقوفاً، فمن أي المالين نقد فقد عيَّنَ المحتمل به، فصار كحالة التكاذب. بخلاف المأمور بالحج، فإنّ الحج عبادة، وهو لا يتأدَّى إلا بالنية، فكان مأموراً بأن ينوي الحج عن المحجوج عنه، فإذا لم يفعل كان مخالفاً، وأما المعاملات فالنية ليست بشرط فيها، فلا يصير بتركها مخالفاً، فيبقى الحكم موقوفاً على النقد. هكذا ذكروا). وأما قول الإمام ففي «المنح): قولُ الإمام فيما ذكره العراقيون مع محمد ه، وغيرهم ذكروه مع الثاني. انتهى.
If he adds it to a specific price, the purchase is for the owner of that price; because the apparent is that he adds the purchase to the wealth of the one for whom he is buying, and this because the price, although not physically specified, has a semblance of specification in terms of the safety of the sold item and the specification of its quantity and description; therefore profit is not permissible for him if he buys with usurped dirhams, and his religion prevents him from buying for himself by adding the contract to the wealth of another; because that is objectionable legally and customarily, so he would not do it. And custom has established that when he buys for himself, he adds the contract to his own wealth, and that is not objectionable legally and customarily, so the purchased item belongs to the owner of that price, even if he intended otherwise, proceeding according to custom and law. Then, if he pays from the wealth of the principal when he bought for himself, he is liable for guarantee. And if he adds it to an absolute price, it is not free from: either it is prompt or deferred. If it is prompt, then it is either that they both affirm the existence of intention for one of them, or they affirm its absence, or they differ on it. If the price is prompt and they agree on the existence of intention for one of them, it is for the one he intended; because what is established by their mutual agreement is like something established by eyewitness, and their agreement is a proof against them, and payment is not considered, and no attention is paid to it in this case; because he is allowed to buy for himself and for the principal, so if he specifies by intention then it is specified, and if he pays from the wealth of the principal while intending it for himself, he is a usurper. And if they differ on the intention, payment determines by consensus; because its indication of specification is like its indication of adding the purchase to him; because the apparent is that he does what is legally permissible for him, or follows his customs. And if they agree that no intention was present, then according to Muhammad—may Allah have mercy on him—it is for the contracting party; because whatever a person undertakes of transactions is for himself, so it becomes like one ordered to perform Hajj when he undertakes it without intending it for the one on whose behalf he is performing Hajj. And according to Abu Yusuf, payment is determinative; because what is absolute is subject to probability, so it remains suspended, and from whichever of the two funds he pays, he has thereby specified the probable, so it becomes like the situation of mutual denial. Unlike the one ordered to perform Hajj, because Hajj is an act of worship and is not valid except with intention, so he was ordered to intend the Hajj on behalf of the one on whose behalf he is performing, so if he did not do so, he would be disobedient. As for transactions, intention is not a condition in them, so by omitting it he does not become disobedient, so the ruling remains suspended until payment. This is what they mentioned. As for the saying of the Imam, in 'Al-Minah': the saying of the Imam according to what the Iraqis mentioned is with Muhammad, and others mentioned it with the second. End.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an agent (wakil) who adds the purchase to a specific or absolute price (thaman). It begins with the ruling: if the agent adds the purchase to a specific price (e.g., 'I buy this for the owner of this gold coin'), the purchase belongs to that owner. The reason is that the price, though not physically specified, has a semblance of specification in terms of quantity and description. The custom (urf) supports this: ordinarily, if buying for himself, he adds it to his own wealth; if for another, to that other's. If he buys for himself but pays from the principal's wealth, he must guarantee. Next, the passage deals with an absolute price (e.g., 'I buy with this money' without specifying). The price may be prompt or deferred. For prompt: if both agree on whose intention it was, it goes to that person. If they differ, payment (naqd) determines by consensus, because payment indicates whom he intended to buy for. If they agree no intention was present, there are two positions: Imam Muhammad says it is for the agent himself, because transactions are attributed to the one who performs them unless proven otherwise (analogy to hajj by proxy where intention is required). Abu Yusuf says payment determines, because the absolute price leaves the transaction suspended; payment specifies it. The difference arises because hajj is worship requiring intention, while transactions (mu'amalat) do not. The end notes that some attribute Imam Abu Hanifa's view to Muhammad's position, others to Abu Yusuf's.

Key Terms
ثمن معين (thaman mu'ayyan): A specific price, such as a particular coin or item, identified at the time of contract.
ثمن مطلق (thaman mutlaq): An absolute or unspecified price, e.g., 'this money' without further identification.
نية (niyyah): Intention of the agent at the time of contract regarding whether the purchase is for himself or the principal.
نقد (naqd): The actual payment or disbursement of money, used as evidence to resolve disputes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The principal, because the agent acted on his behalf
B) The agent himself, because transactions are attributed to the contracting party
C) It is determined by payment (naqd)
D) The item is invalid and must be returned

Key Takeaway
When an agent buys with an absolute price and no intention is proven, Imam Muhammad gives the purchase to the agent; Abu Yusuf follows the money.

Invalidation of Sarf and Salam by Agent's Departure
Ruling with proof: agent's departure invalidates Sarf/Salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule regarding whose departure invalidates Sarf and Salam, and explain why the agent's departure matters while the principal's does not.

Core Idea
In Sarf and Salam, only the agent's departure before qabd invalidates the contract – because the agent is the contracting party, not the principal.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding agency in the special contracts of Sarf (currency exchange) and Salam (forward sale). The matn states that these contracts are invalidated by the departure of the agent (wakil) before taking possession (qabd), but not by the departure of the principal (muwakkil). We will explore the legal reasoning behind this distinction, including the role of the contracting party and the comparison with a messenger (rasul).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a Sarf or Salam contract conducted through an agent. The ruling: if the agent departs before qabd, the contract becomes batil (invalid). If the principal departs, it remains valid.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas based on the definition of the contracting party. The agent is the aqid, so his qabd is required, analogous to any other contracting party.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The analogy connects the requirement of qabd in the same session for Sarf and Salam to the fact that the agent is the contracting party. Since qabd must be performed by the aqid, the agent's departure before qabd violates the condition.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some later scholars (e.g., Khawaharzadah) held that if the principal is present in the session, the agent's departure does not invalidate. The commentator rejects this, affirming the agent's role is absolute.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the agent's departure invalidates regardless of the principal's presence, as stated in Al-Hidayah and other authoritative texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة باب الوكالة بالبيع والشراء
ويبطل الصرف والسلم بمفارقة الوكيل دون آمره
[١] قوله : ويبطل...الخ ؛ يعني يعتبر في السلم والصرف مفارقة الوكيل لا الموكل، فيبطل عقدهما بمفارقة الوكيل صاحبه قبل القبض؛ لوجود الافتراق من غير قبض، ولا يبطل بمفارقة الموكل، إذ القبض للعاقد، وهو ليس بعاقد، وما قيل: من أنه إذا حضر الموكل مجلس العقد لا يعتبر مفارقة الوكيل ضعيف؛ لكون الوكيل أصلاً في الحقوق في البيع مطلقاً. كما في «البحر».
والتفصيل: إن المستحق في الصرف والسلم قبض العاقد، والعاقد هو الوكيل، فيشترط قبضه، وإن كان لا يتعلق به الحقوق؛ كالصبي والعبد المحجور عليه؛ لأن قبضه وتسليمه صحيح، وإن لم يتوجه عليه المطالبة، ففي حكم صحة التقابض هو كوكيل تعلق به حقوق العقد، فإذا قبض الوكيل ثم العقد؛ لوجود شرطه، وإن فارقه قبل القبض بطل شرطه، وإن فارقه الموكل قبل القبض لا يبطل؛ لأنه ليس بعاقد.
بخلاف الرسول فيهما؛ لأن الرسالة حصلت في العقد لا في القبض، وكلام الرسول ينتقل إلى المرسل فيكون العاقد هو المرسل، فيكون قبض الرسول قبض غير العاقد فلا يجوز.
وقال في «النهاية»: هذا إذا كان الموكل غائباً من مجلس العقد، وأما إذا كان حاضراً في مجلس العقد يصير كأن الموكل صارف بنفسه، فلا تعتبر مفارقة الوكيل، وعزاه إلى خُوَاهَرْ زَادَه، وهذا مشكل، فإن الوكيل أصيل في باب البيع حضر الموكل في مجلس العقد أو لم يحضر. كذا في «التبيين».
[٢] قوله : بمفارقة الوكيل؛ قيد بالوكيل؛ لأن الرسول فيهما لا يعتبر مفارقته؛
Book of Agency, Chapter of Agency for Sale and Purchase: Sarf and Salam are invalidated by the departure of the agent, not his principal. [1] His statement: 'and it becomes invalid...' etc., means that in Salam and Sarf, the departure of the agent is considered, not the principal. So their contract becomes invalid by the agent departing from his companion before qabd, because there is separation without qabd. It does not become invalid by the principal's departure, because qabd is for the contracting party, and he is not the contracting party. What has been said: that if the principal is present in the contract session, the agent's departure is not considered, is weak, because the agent is the original in rights in sale absolutely, as in Al-Bahr. The detail: The right in Sarf and Salam is the qabd of the contracting party, and the contracting party is the agent, so his qabd is required, even if rights are not attached to him, like a minor or a prohibited slave, because his qabd and delivery are valid, even if demand is not made against him. So in the ruling of valid exchange, he is like an agent with rights of contract attached. So if the agent takes qabd, then the contract is valid due to existence of its condition; if he separates before qabd, its condition is invalidated; if the principal separates before qabd, it is not invalidated because he is not the contracting party. Unlike the messenger in both, because the messengership occurs in the contract, not in qabd, and the messenger's speech transfers to the sender, so the contracting party is the sender, so the messenger's qabd is qabd of a non-contracting party, so it is not valid. And it is said in Al-Nihayah: This is if the principal is absent from the contract session; but if he is present in the contract session, it becomes as if the principal is exchanging himself, so the agent's departure is not considered, and he attributes it to Khawaharzadah. This is problematic, for the agent is an original in the chapter of sale whether the principal is present in the contract session or not. So in Al-Tabyin. [2] His statement: 'by the agent's departure' he restricted to the agent, because the messenger in both is not considered regarding his departure.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling from the matn that Sarf (currency exchange) and Salam (forward sale) are invalidated by the departure of the agent (wakil) before qabd (taking possession), not by the departure of the principal (muwakkil). The commentator explains that the key factor is that the agent is the contracting party (aqid) in the sale, so his qabd is required. The principal is not the contracting party, so his departure does not affect the contract. A hypothetical is raised and rejected: if the principal is present in the contract session, some say the agent's departure is irrelevant; but the commentator labels this weak because the agent remains the original party in the sale regardless of the principal's presence. A detailed comparison with a messenger (rasul) is given: the messenger's speech transfers to the sender (the principal), so the messenger is not the contracting party; therefore, his qabd is not valid. The passage also references an opinion from Al-Nihayah regarding the principal's presence, but the commentator rejects it, affirming the agent's role is unchanged.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakil): An agent who is the contracting party in the sale; his qabd is required.
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints the agent; not the contracting party, so his departure does not affect the contract.
رسول (rasul): A messenger who conveys speech; his speech transfers to the sender, so he is not the contracting party, and his qabd is invalid.
صرف (sarf): Currency exchange; requires qabd before separation.
سلم (salam): Forward sale; requires qabd before separation.
قبض (qabd): Taking physical possession; required for validity in Sarf and Salam.
مفارقة (mufaraqa): Separation from the session; if by the agent before qabd, the contract is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if an agent for a Sarf contract departs before qabd, the contract is:
A) Valid if the principal is present
B) Invalid
C) Valid regardless
D) Valid if the principal also departs

Key Takeaway
In Sarf and Salam, only the departure of the agent (the contracting party) before qabd invalidates the contract; the principal's departure has no effect.

Denial of Order in Agency Sale: The Binding Effect of Iqrar
Illustrating the ruling when a buyer denies having ordered the sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer denies the principal's order after acknowledging agency, and explain how iqrar and ta'ati affect the sale.

Core Idea
A buyer's admission of agency binds him; only the principal's denial can undo it.

Imagine telling a shopkeeper, 'Sell this to Ahmed,' then later saying Ahmed never asked you. In Hanafi fiqh, your initial statement is an admission (iqrar) that Ahmed authorized the purchase, so you cannot later contradict yourself. But what if Ahmed himself says he didn't authorize it? This lesson unpacks that scenario, exploring the binding nature of iqrar, the exception when the principal denies, and how voluntary delivery can still create a valid sale by ta'ati (mutual exchange).

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal dispute
The buyer initially says 'Sell this to Zayd' (implying agency) but later denies Zayd ordered it. The question is whether his denial is effective.
This is a classic case of iqrar (admission) followed by a contradictory claim.
2
Understand the Hanafi rule on iqrar
In Hanafi law, iqrar (admission) against one's own interest is binding and cannot be contradicted without a valid reason. The buyer's statement 'Sell for Zayd' is an admission of agency.
Iqrar is a strong proof; the buyer is bound by his own words.
3
Recognize the exception: principal's denial
If Zayd (the principal) confirms that he never ordered the purchase, the buyer's admission is retracted because agency requires the principal's mandate. The phrase 'fa in saddaqahu la ya'khudhuhu jabran' indicates the agent cannot forcibly deliver the item.
The exception protects the principal's rights and prevents unjust seizure.
4
Apply the concept of ta'ati
Even if the agency is voided, if the buyer voluntarily hands over the item to Zayd, it constitutes a valid sale by ta'ati (mutual action), as delivery with sale intention suffices for a contract in Hanafi school.
Ta'ati is valid for items of minor value and does not require verbal offer/acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : بعني هذا لزيد، فباعه، ثم أنكر الأمر أخذه زيد، فإن صدقه لا يأخذه جبراً.
قوله: لأن قوله ... الخ؛ حاصله: إن قول المشتري: بعني لزيد، إقرار منه بالوكالة، فلا يلتفت إلى إنكاره للتناقض.
وقيل: إن قوله: يعني لزيد إقرار بتوكيله؛ لأن هذا البيع إنما يكون لزيد إذا أمر زيد به، فلا يصدق في إنكاره أمره. فإن صدقه لا يأخذه جبراً؛ أي إن صدق زيد المشتري أنه لم يأمره لا يأخذه جبراً؛ لأن إقرار المشتري ارتد برده، وإنما قال جبراً؛ لأن المشتري إن سلمه إلى زيد يكون بيعاً بالتعاطي، فالتسليم على وجه البيع يكفي للتعاطي، وإن لم يوجد نقد الثمن.
If he said: Sell this to Zayd, and he sold it, then the buyer denied the order, Zayd takes it. If he (Zayd) affirms him, he does not take it by force. His statement: because his statement... etc.; the summary: The buyer's statement: 'Sell for Zayd' is an acknowledgement of agency, so his denial is not considered due to contradiction. And it is said: His statement meaning for Zayd is an acknowledgement of his agency; because this sale is only for Zayd if Zayd ordered it, so he is not believed in his denial of the order. 'If he affirms him, he does not take it by force': i.e., if Zayd affirms the buyer that he did not order him, he does not take it by force, because the buyer's acknowledgement is repudiated by his statement. He said 'by force' because if the buyer hands it over to Zayd, it becomes a sale by ta'ati (mutual action), and handing over with the intention of sale is sufficient for ta'ati, even if the price is not paid in cash.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents a case: Someone tells an agent (or seller) 'Sell this to Zayd', then after the sale, the buyer denies that Zayd ordered the purchase. The ruling: Zayd takes the item because the buyer's statement 'Sell for Zayd' is an admission that Zayd authorized the purchase, so he cannot later contradict himself. However, if Zayd himself confirms that he never ordered the purchase, then the agent cannot forcibly deliver the item to Zayd; the buyer's admission is retracted. The term 'jabran' (by force) is used because if the buyer voluntarily hands it over, it constitutes a valid sale by ta'ati (mutual exchange without formal offer/acceptance). The commentary clarifies that the buyer's initial statement is an acknowledgment (iqrar) of agency, which is binding on him, but if Zayd denies, the agency is disproved, and the buyer's acknowledgment is effectively withdrawn.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Admission by the buyer that he was acting as agent for Zayd; binding on him.
التعاطي (ta'ati): A sale by mutual action without verbal offer/acceptance; valid in Hanafi school.
وكالة (wakalah): Agency; the buyer's statement 'Sell for Zayd' implies agency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Zayd takes the item because of the buyer's admission
C) The agent must return the item
D) Zayd must pay the price

Key Takeaway
A buyer's admission of agency (iqrar) is binding and cannot be contradicted, but if the principal (Zayd) confirms the buyer's denial, the admission is voided, and the agent cannot forcibly deliver the item; however, voluntary delivery constitutes a valid sale by ta'ati.

Agency in Purchase: Agent Exceeds Instructions & Valid Salam Agency
Explaining the ruling when agent buys more than instructed and the picture of salam agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on an agent exceeding purchase instructions and describe the valid form of salam agency.

Core Idea
An agent exceeding instructions does not bind the principal for the excess, according to Abu Hanifa; salam agency requires the agent to buy, not sell.

This passage presents a common scenario: an agent buys more meat than instructed at no extra cost. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on whether the principal is bound for the excess. The passage also clarifies the valid structure of salam agency: the agent must buy on behalf of the principal, not sell.

Guided Path
1
Identify the instruction and agent's action
The principal instructs: buy one mann of meat for one dirham. The agent buys two mann for one dirham (market price is one mann per dirham).
Quantity doubled at same total cost.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
Principal is bound for only one mann at half a dirham (proportional price). The agent exceeded authority, so the excess is void.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling
Principal is bound for two mann for one dirham. The agent acted beneficially, so the principal must accept the gain.
This view is also held by al-Shafi'i.
4
Identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, principal pays half dirham and gets one mann. Under Sahibayn, principal pays full dirham and gets two mann.
5
Describe valid salam agency form
The agent must buy the commodity (e.g., wheat) by salam contract. It is not agency to sell by salam, as that would involve selling what is not owned.
The agent is the contracting party, but the contract binds the principal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن وكل بشراء من لحم بدرهم، فشرى منوين بدرهم مما يباع من بدرهم لزم موكله من بنصف درهم. هذا عند أبي حنيفة. وعندهما يلزمه منوان بدرهم.
صورة السلم: أن يوكل رجلاً بأن يشتري له كر بر بعقد السلم، وليس المراد التوكيل ببيع الكر بعقد السلم؛ لأن هذا لا يجوز إذ الوكيل يبيع طعاماً في ذمته على أن يكون الثمن لغيره، ولا نظير له في الشرع، وإنما يعتبر مفارقة الوكيل؛ لأن العاقد هو الوكيل.
[٢] قوله : ومَن وكل ...الخ؛ يعني من وكله بشراء من واحد من اللحم بدرهم واحد، فاشترى الوكيل منوين من اللحم بدرهم مما يباع منه من بدرهم، لزم الموكل من واحد بنصف درهم.

And whoever is appointed to buy one mann of meat for one dirham, and he buys two mann for one dirham of that which is sold at one dirham per mann, then his principal is bound for one mann for half a dirham. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And according to the two companions, he is bound for two mann for one dirham. The form of salam: that a man appoints an agent to buy for him a kurr of wheat by salam contract. The intention is not agency to sell the kurr by salam, because that is not permissible, since the agent would sell food in his own liability with the price for someone else, and there is no parallel in Shariah. Rather, the agent's departure is considered because the agent is the contracting party. [2] His statement: 'And whoever is appointed...' etc., meaning whoever appoints him to buy one mann of meat for one dirham, and the agent buys two mann for one dirham of that which is sold at one dirham per mann, then the principal is bound for one mann for half a dirham. [3] His statement: 'And according to the two companions he is bound...' etc.; and the three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i) say this, and it is mentioned in some copies of Mukhtasar...
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents a case of agency in purchase: The principal instructs the agent to buy one mann of meat for one dirham. The agent instead buys two mann of the same quality meat for one dirham (since the market price is one mann per dirham, he gets double the quantity). The ruling according to Imam Abu Hanifa: The principal is bound only for one mann at half a dirham (i.e., the agent exceeded his authority, but the excess is void, so the principal takes one mann at the market rate). According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the principal is bound for the full two mann for one dirham because the agent acted within the general scope. The passage then transitions to the 'form of salam' (surah al-salam), describing a valid salam agency: the principal appoints an agent to buy a specific commodity (kurr of wheat) by salam contract. It clarifies that this is not an agency to sell by salam (which would be invalid). The commentary reiterates the meat example and notes that the Sahibayn's view is also held by al-Shafi'i (the 'three').

Key Terms
من (mann): A unit of weight (approx. 2 raṭls); used for meat.
درهم (dirham): A silver coin; unit of currency.
سلم (salam): A forward sale where price is paid in advance for deferred delivery of specified goods.
صورة السلم (surat al-salam): The valid form of a salam contract in agency; agent buys, not sells, on credit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 2 mann for 1 dirham
B) 1 mann for 1 dirham
C) 1 mann for half a dirham
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
An agent's excess in quantity at no extra cost does not automatically bind the principal; Abu Hanifa's view limits liability to the instruction, while Sahibayn extend it for benefit.

Dispute over Price for Specified Item: Mutual Oath and Tasdiq
Ruling when agent is told to buy a specific item without naming price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when an agent is instructed to buy a specific item without naming a price, explain the mutual oath process, and identify when the agent's word is accepted with or without oath.

Core Idea
When agent and principal dispute the price of a specific item, mutual oath applies, rescinding the constructive sale.

In agency purchases, disputes often arise over the price paid. If the principal instructed the agent to buy a specific item without specifying a price, and they later disagree, the Hanafi school prescribes a mutual oath between them. This lesson unpacks that ruling, the role of the seller's confirmation, and a crucial distinction based on whether the agent had already received the price. It also clarifies a common confusion: is the agent's word accepted with or without an oath?

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The case: The principal says 'Buy this slave' without naming a price. The agent buys it, then they dispute the price. Start by recognizing that this is a mu'ayyan (specific) item, not a generic purchase, so the ruling differs from cases where the item is described generically.
The matn says 'وكذا في معين' — 'Similarly for a specific item.' It connects to the previous rule about generic items.
2
Apply the mutual oath ruling
The 'most apparent' view holds that they take mutual oath (tahalf). The agent swears he bought it for the claimed price, the principal swears it was less. This leads to rescission of the constructive sale between them.
Even if the seller confirms the agent's claim, mutual oath is still required between agent and principal.
3
Understand the trust vs. exchange distinction
If the agent had already received the price from the principal before buying, the trust aspect (amanah) takes precedence, so the agent's word is taken without oath? Actually, the text says if he received the price, the dispute considers violation and trust; if not, violation and exchange. The answer: when trust precedes exchange, the trust governs; otherwise, mutual oath.
This nuance explains why some sources say 'the agent is believed' – but only in cases where he has the money.
4
Clarify the tasdiq (belief) with or without oath
The commentator notes that al-Durr says 'the agent is believed' without mentioning oath, but later authorities (Ibn Nujaym, al-Ashbah) require an oath except for the special case of debt collection after the principal's death, where evidence is needed.
The mufta bihi (operative opinion) is that the agent must swear, except in that one exception.
5
Determine the practical consequence
If mutual oath occurs, the constructive sale is rescinded, and the item remains with the agent, who must pay the price he claimed. If the agent's word is taken without oath (in the exceptional case), he is believed without swearing.
This shows the importance of knowing when oath is required for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And similarly regarding a specific item for which he did not name a price, and he bought it and they differed over its price, and if the seller believed the agent, in the most apparent view they take mutual oath. Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of price disputes in agency. It presents a second scenario: the agent is told to buy a specific slave (mu'ayyan) without specifying the price. If they later dispute the price, they take mutual oath (tahalf). This is the 'most apparent' view. The commentary explains that if the seller confirms the agent's claim, the effect is still mutual oath between agent and principal, leading to rescission of the sale, and the slave remains with the agent. An objection is raised: why treat the case differently when the agent has already received the price? The answer distinguishes priority: when the agent has the money, the trust aspect (amanah) comes before the exchange, so his word is preferred; if not, the exchange aspect governs and leads to mutual oath. The final note discusses whether the agent's word is accepted with or without oath. The commentator clarifies that the general ruling 'agent is believed' means without oath, but contrary opinions exist; the relied-upon view requires an oath except in certain cases like debt collection after the principal's death, where evidence is needed.

Key Terms
تصديق بغير حلف (tasdiq bi-ghayr hilf): Acceptance of the agent's claim without requiring an oath.
بائع (ba'i'): The seller who sold the item to the agent; his confirmation of the price affects the mutual oath.
مبادلة حكمية (mubadalah hukmiyyah): Juristic exchange where the agent and principal are treated as buyer and seller in constructive sale.

A) The agent is believed without oath
B) They take mutual oath
C) The principal is believed
D) The sale is void automatically

Key Takeaway
For an agent buying a specific item without a named price, mutual oath is required; the agent's word is generally accepted with his oath, not without.

Seller After Receiving Price: A Stranger to the Agency
Commentary expanding a ruling on the seller's credibility in agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the credibility of a seller who has received the price in an agency sale and explain the two scenarios that render him a stranger to the parties.

Core Idea
Once the seller takes the price, he becomes an outsider to the agency—his word is not accepted against the agent or principal.

This passage from the commentary clarifies a key rule in the law of agency for sale. It explains that after receiving the price, the seller is a legal stranger to both the agent and the principal, and therefore his testimony regarding the agency is rejected. Understanding this rule helps distinguish who has standing to make claims in agency disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Recognize that the passage discusses the credibility of the seller after the sale has been completed in an agency transaction.
The seller is a third party to the agency contract.
2
Distinguish the two possibilities
The commentator breaks down two cases: (1) the seller has received the price, (2) the seller has not received the price. In both, he is a stranger to at least one party.
After receipt, he is ajnabi to both; before receipt, he is ajnabi to the principal only.
3
Apply the ruling
Conclude that in either case, the seller's testimony is not accepted (la yusaddaq) regarding the agency.
The ruling protects the agency from external interference.
4
Reflect on the rationale
Understand that the seller has no legal interest in the agency once the price is paid, making him an outsider.
This preserves the integrity of the wakalah contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنَّ البائع بعد استيفاء الثَّمَنِ أَجنبي عنهما ، وأيضاً هو أجنبي عن الموكل فلا يصدق عليه.
ومات سنة (۳۳۳) ثلاث وثلاثين وثلاثمئة ، وقيل سنة (۳۳۲) اثنتين وثلاثين وثلاثمائة ، وإن شئت زيادة التفصيل فارجع إلى «أعلام الأخيار» و«الطبقات) للكفوي وغيرهما من التواريخ المبسوطة. [١) قوله : لأن البائع ... الخ ؛ حاصله : إنَّ البائع لا يخلو : إما أن يستوفي الثمن أو لا ، فعلى الأول: هو أجنبي عن الوكيل والموكل، وعلى الثاني : فهو أجنبي عن الموكل فلا مدخل له بينهما، فلا يصدق عليه.
(۱) «كتائب أعلام الأخيار للكفوي(ق۸۱).
Because the seller after receiving the price is a stranger to both of them, and also he is a stranger to the principal, so he is not believed regarding it. And he died in the year 333 AH (three hundred and thirty-three), and it is said in the year 332 AH (three hundred and thirty-two). And if you wish more detail, refer to 'A'lam al-Akhyar' and 'al-Tabaqat' by al-Kafawi and other detailed histories. [1] His statement: 'because the seller... etc.'; its summary: The seller is not free from either receiving the price or not. In the first case, he is a stranger to the agent and the principal. In the second, he is a stranger to the principal, so he has no connection between them, therefore he is not believed against them. (1) 'Kata'ib A'lam al-Akhyar' by al-Kafawi (p.81).
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a ruling from the chapter of agency for sale and purchase. It explains the legal status of the seller after he has received the full price. The ruling states that once the seller has taken the price, he becomes a stranger (ajnabi) to both the agent (wakil) and the principal (muwakkil). Consequently, his word is not accepted (la yusaddaq) regarding the agency transaction—for example, if he claims that he did not receive the price or that the agent acted without authority. The commentary then breaks down two scenarios: (1) if the seller has received the price, he is a stranger to both parties; (2) if he has not received the price, he is still a stranger to the principal. In neither case does he have standing to give testimony or make a claim that affects the agency relationship. The passage also contains a biographical note about the death of a scholar (likely the author of the original text) in 333 AH (or 332 AH) and recommends consulting 'A'lam al-Akhyar' and 'al-Tabaqat' by al-Kafawi for further details. The footnote at the end references 'Kata'ib A'lam al-Akhyar'. This note is an aside and does not affect the legal ruling.

Key Terms
بائع (ba'i'): The seller in a sale transaction; the party who transfers ownership of the sold item in exchange for the price.
ثمن (thaman): The price paid in a sale; the consideration received by the seller.
أجنبي (ajnabi): A stranger or outsider; someone who has no legal connection or interest in the transaction.
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent (wakil) to act on his behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
After receiving the price, the seller becomes a legal stranger to the agency; his word carries no weight in disputes between the agent and principal.
