Agent's Purchase with Overpayment: Defining Minor vs Excessive
Determining when an agent's purchase with overpayment binds the principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi definition of minor and excessive overpayment (ghabn yasir vs fahish) to determine whether an agent's purchase binds the principal.

Core Idea
An agent's overpayment binds the principal if it falls within appraisers' estimates—otherwise the agent bears the loss.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya expands on the rule that an agent's purchase is bound by the conditions of agency. The central issue is overpayment (ghabn): how much deviation from market value is tolerated? The Hanafi school distinguishes between minor and excessive overpayment, and the binding effect depends on whether the item has a known market price. The commentator presents multiple views and provides the relied-upon criterion from Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agency type
Determine if the agency is for a specific or non-specific item, as the ruling differs.
For a specific item, there is disagreement; for non-specific, the agent bears minor overpayment in all cases.
2
Distinguish minor vs excessive overpayment
Understand the definition from Muhammad in al-Nawadir: the boundary is the appraisers' range.
Whatever falls within the estimates of appraisers is minor; what lies outside is excessive.
3
Apply the two-tier rule
For items without known value, minor overpayment binds principal; excessive does not. For items with known value, any increase does not bind.
Shaykh al-Islam qualifies the definition for known-value commodities like bread and meat.
4
Resolve intra-Hanafi disagreements
Note differing views on specific-item agency and Khwaharzada's isolated vs combined overpayment. The mufta bihi is based on appraisers' estimates.
Soundest view: appraisers' range defines minor; all other views are valid ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقيد شراء الوكيل به : في «الذخيرة»: إن كانت الوكالة بشراء شيء بعينه ، قيل : بمثل القيمة، وبزيادة يتغابنُ النَّاس فيها : وهي ما يقوم به مقوم بمثل القيمة ، وبزيادة يتغابنُ النَّاس فيها : وهي ما يقومُ به مقوّم ) يتحملُ من الوكيل الغبنُ اليسير دون الفاحش، وقيل: لا يتحمل منه اليسير أيضاً، وفي الوكالة بشراء شيءٍ لا بعينه لا فرق بين ما إذا كان الثمن مسمَّى أو غير مسمى، فإنّه يتحمل منه الغبن اليسير على كل حال، على ما ذكر في «الزيادات».
وذكر محمد الله في بعض الكتب : إنّ الثمن إذا كان مسمى لا يتحملُ منه الغبن اليسير، وقال شيخ الإسلام خُواهَرْ زَادَه : إِنَّ الغبنَ اليسير إنما يعطى إذا كان بانفراده ، أما إذا كان فاحشاً فبمقدار اليسير منه لا يكون عفواً منه. كذا في «الفصول العمادية».
قوله : بمثل القيمة...الخ ؛ فلا يلزم الموكل ما شرى وكيله بزيادة على القيمة لا يتغابن فيها، وهي الزيادة الفاحشة.
قوله : وهي ما يقوم به مقوم ؛ قال في كمال الدراية : في «الذخيرة» قال شيخ الإسلام في «جامعه»: تكلموا في الحد الفاصل بين اليسير والفاحش، والصحيحُ عن محمد في «النوادر»: إنَّ كلَّ ما يدخلُ تحت تقويم المقومين فهو يسير، وما لا فهو فاحش.
قال شيخ الإسلام : وهذا التحديد فيما لم يكن له قيمة معلومة في البلد ، كالعبيد والدواب، فأما ما له قيمة معلومة كالخبز واللحم ، فإنَّ الوكيل إذا زادَ لا ينفذ على الموكل وإن كانت الزيادة كالفلس ونحوه ؛ لأن ما يدخل تحت تقويم المقومين هو فيما يحتاج فيه إلى تقويمهم، وهذا لا يحتاج. انتهى. وقال في «جامع المضمرات»: رجلٌ اشترى بعشرة دراهم فامتنع الموكل من أخذه منه ، لما يرى فيه من الغبن ، فعرض المبيع على المقومين الذين لهم معرفة في تقويم المبيع، فقال بعضهم : يساوي تسعة وقال بعضهم : يساوي عشرة. فهذا داخل تحت تقويم المقومين، فيلزم الموكل، وإن لم يقومه أحد منهم بعشرة فهذا ما لا يدخل تحت تقويم المقومين، ولا يتغابنُ النَّاسُ في مثله، فيلزم الوكيل ؛ لأنه لا يملك الشراء بالغبن الفاحش. انتهى بقدر الضرورة.
And the agent's purchase is bound by it: In 'al-Dhakhira': If the agency is to purchase a specific thing, it is said: at like the value, and with an increase where people overlook such difference: that is what an appraiser appraises at like the value, and with an increase where people overlook such: that is what an appraiser appraises. He bears from the agent the minor overpayment but not the excessive; and it is said: he does not bear even the minor. And in agency to purchase a non-specific thing, there is no difference between whether the price is named or not, for he bears the minor overpayment in any case, as mentioned in 'al-Ziyadat'. And Muhammad Allah mentioned in some books: if the price is named, he does not bear even the minor overpayment. And Shaykh al-Islam Khwaharzada said: the minor overpayment is excused only if it is isolated, but if it is excessive, the minor portion of it is not excused. This is in 'al-Fusul al-Imadiyya'. His saying: at like the value...etc.; so the principal is not bound by what his agent bought at an increase over the value that people do not overlook, which is the excessive increase. His saying: that is what an appraiser appraises; said in 'Kamal al-Diraya': In 'al-Dhakhira', Shaykh al-Islam said in his 'Jami': They discussed the boundary between minor and excessive. The sound view from Muhammad in 'al-Nawadir' is that everything that falls under the appraisal of appraisers is minor, and what does not is excessive. Shaykh al-Islam said: This definition applies to what does not have a known value in the town, such as slaves and animals. As for what has a known value like bread and meat, if the agent increases, it does not bind the principal even if the increase is like a fils or similar, because what falls under the appraisal of appraisers is in what needs their appraisal, but this does not need. End quote. And said in 'Jami' al-Mudmarat': A man bought for ten dirhams, and the principal refused to take it from him, seeing overpayment in it. He presented the commodity to appraisers who have knowledge in appraising the commodity. Some said: it is worth nine; some said: it is worth ten. This falls under the appraisal of appraisers, so it binds the principal. If none of them appraised it at ten, then it does not fall under the appraisal of appraisers, and people do not overlook such, so it binds the agent, because he does not have the authority to purchase with excessive overpayment. End quote as needed.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the phrase 'And the agent's purchase is bound by it' from the base text. It discusses the effect of overpayment (ghabn) on the validity of an agent's purchase. The commentator starts by citing al-Dhakhira, which distinguishes two types of agency: (1) agency to purchase a specific thing, and (2) agency to purchase a non-specific thing. For a specific thing, there are two opinions: one says the agent bears minor overpayment (ghabn yasir) but not excessive; another says he does not bear even minor. For a non-specific thing, the agent bears minor overpayment regardless of whether the price was specified or not, according to al-Ziyadat. Then he mentions a third opinion from Muhammad (presumably Imam Muhammad) that if the price is named, even minor overpayment is not borne. Then he quotes Khwaharzada who distinguishes: minor overpayment is excused only if it occurs alone (i.e., the total purchase price is only slightly over market); but if the overall purchase price is excessively high, the minor portion of that excess is not excused. The commentator then explains the statement 'at like the value': the principal is not bound by an increase that is excessive (fahish). He then provides the definition of minor vs excessive from Muhammad in al-Nawadir: whatever falls within the range of appraisers' estimates is minor; what lies outside is excessive. However, Shaykh al-Islam qualifies this: this definition applies only to items without a fixed market price (e.g., slaves, animals). For items with a known price (e.g., bread, meat), any increase, even a small amount, does not bind the principal. Finally, he quotes Jami' al-Mudmarat with an example: if appraisers differ between 9 and 10 dirhams for a 10-dirham purchase, it falls within their appraisal and binds the principal; if none appraise it at 10, it is excessive and binds the agent.

Key Terms
الغبن (al-ghabn): The difference between the price paid and the market value; overpayment.
الغبن اليسير (al-ghabn al-yasīr): Overpayment that falls within the range of appraisers' estimates; considered tolerated.
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghabn al-fāḥish): Overpayment that lies outside the range of appraisers' estimates; not tolerated.
المقومون (al-muqawwimūn): Appraisers or valuers who estimate the market price of a commodity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the sound view from Muhammad in al-Nawadir, what is the boundary between minor and excessive overpayment?
A) Any amount less than 1/10 of the value.
B) Whatever falls within the range of appraisers' estimates is minor; what does not is excessive.
C) Any amount that the principal does not accept.
D) Overpayment of less than one dirham.

Key Takeaway
An agent's purchase with overpayment binds the principal only if the extra amount is within the range of appraisers' estimates; otherwise the agent bears the loss.

Defining Trifling vs. Excessive Ghubn in Agency Purchase
Defines the boundary between tolerable and excessive price deception in agency purchases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of trifling ghubn and apply it to determine whether the principal is bound by the agent's purchase.

Core Idea
Trifling ghubn is whatever falls within the range of valuators' assessments; excessive ghubn binds the agent, not the principal.

In agency purchases, the agent may sometimes pay a price above market value. How much disparity is tolerable before the principal can reject the transaction? The Hanafi school defines the boundary between trifling (yasir) and excessive (fahish) ghubn by reference to expert valuators and, in some cases, customary percentages. This lesson unpacks these definitions and their practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The agent is authorized to buy, but the principal is not bound by an excessive price. The question is: what counts as excessive?
2
Understand the primary definition
Imam Muhammad (in al-Nawadir) defines trifling ghubn as whatever falls within the range of valuations by multiple expert valuators. If no valuator supports the price, it is excessive.
This is the relied-upon view.
3
Note the exception for known-value goods
Shaykh al-Islam Khwaharzada restricts this to items without a known market price (e.g., slaves, animals). For staple goods (bread, meat), even a tiny increase binds the principal only if valuators are needed; otherwise, it does not.
4
Apply the example from the text
If valuators estimate a purchase at between 9 and 10 dirhams, and the agent paid 10, it is trifling and binds the principal. If no valuator values it at 10, it is excessive and the agent bears the loss.
5
Know the percentage guidelines
As a secondary rule, some Hanafi authorities set percentages: 10% for goods, 11% for animals, 12% for real estate, reflecting transaction frequency and experience.
Percentages are not the primary test but practical benchmarks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[١] قوله : بمثل القيمة...الخ ؛ فلا يلزم الموكل ما شرى وكيله بزيادة على القيمة لا يتغابن فيها، وهي الزيادة الفاحشة. [٢] قوله : وهي ما يقوم به مقوم ؛ قال في كمال الدراية»(۱) : في «الذخيرة» قال شيخ الإسلام في «جامعه»: تكلموا في الحد الفاصل بين اليسير والفاحش، والصحيحُ محمد » في «النوادر»: إنَّ كلَّ ما يدخلُ تحت تقويم المقومين فهو يسير، وما لا فهو فاحش. قال شيخ الإسلام : وهذا التحديد فيما لم يكن له قيمة معلومة في البلد كالعبيد والدواب، فأما ما له قيمة معلومة كالخبز واللحم فإنَّ الوكيل إذا زادَ لا ينفذ على الموكل وإن كانت الزيادة كالفلس ونحوه ؛ لأن ما يدخل تحت تقويم المقومين هو فيما يحتاج فيه إلى تقويمهم، وهذا لا يحتاج. انتهى. وقال في «جامع المضمرات»: رجلٌ اشترى بعشرة دراهم فامتنع الموكل من أخذه منه ، لما يرى فيه من الغبن ، فعرض المبيع على المقومين الذين لهم معرفة في تقويم المبيع، فقال بعضهم : يساوي تسعة وقال بعضهم : يساوي عشرة. فهذا داخل تحت تقويم المقومين، فيلزم الموكل، وإن لم يقومه أحد منهم بعشرة فهذا ما لا يدخل تحت تقويم المقومين، ولا يتغابنُ النَّاسُ في مثله، فيلزم الوكيل ؛ لأنه لا يملك الشراء بالغبن الفاحش. انتهى بقدر الضرورة. وقال البرجندي : والحاصل أنَّ مثل القيمة هو ما قوم به أكثر المقومين، وما قوم به أقلهم ويكون زائداً على ما قوم به الأكثر فهو ما يتغابن به الناس، فإن كان زائداً بحيث لم يقوم به أحد، فهو مما لا يتغابنُ الناس. ففي العبارة مساهلة، والمقصود ما ذكرناه. انتهى. وقال في «الهداية) : والذي لا يتغابن الناس فيه ما لا يدخل تحت تقويم المتقومين وقيل في العروض ده ينم وفي الحيوانات يازده، وفي العقارات د دوازده ؛ لأنَّ التصرف يكثر وجوده في الأوّل، ويقل في الأخير، ويتوسط في الأوسط، وكثرة الغبن ؛ لقلة التصرف. انتهى. فإنَّ الغبن يزيدُ (بقلة التجربة، وينقص من كثرتها وقلتها، وكثرتها بقلة وقوع التجارات وكثره وفي القسم الأول كثير، وفي الأخير قليل، وفي الأوسط متوسط ، فإذا كان الغبن إلى هذا المبلغ كان يسيراً ، فلزم الأمر ، وإن زاد على ذلك لزم الوكيل. كذا في «البناية»
At the like of the value, and with an increase concerning which people overlook each other: it is that which a valuator evaluates at like value, and with an increase concerning which people overlook each other: it is that which a valuator evaluates. The agent bears trifling deception but not excessive; and it is said: he does not bear even trifling. And in agency to buy an unspecified thing, there is no difference between whether the price is specified or unspecified, for he bears trifling deception in all cases, as mentioned in al-Ziyadat. And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned in some books: if the price is specified, he does not bear trifling deception. And Shaykh al-Islam Khwaharzada said: Trifling deception is only given if it is by itself; but if it is excessive, then to the extent of the trifling part of it is not excused. Thus in al-Fusul al-Imadiyya. [1] His saying: 'At the like of the value...' etc.: So the principal is not bound by what his agent bought with an increase over the value concerning which people do not overlook each other, and that is the excessive increase. [2] His saying: 'And it is that which a valuator evaluates': He said in Kamal al-Diraya: In al-Dhakhira, Shaykh al-Islam said in his Jami': They discussed the dividing line between trifling and excessive. The correct view is that of Muhammad in al-Nawadir: Whatever falls under the valuation of the valuators is trifling, and what does not is excessive. Shaykh al-Islam said: This definition applies to what does not have a known value in the town, like slaves and beasts. As for what has a known value, like bread and meat, if the agent increases, it is not binding on the principal even if the increase is a fils or similar, because what falls under the valuation of the valuators is what requires their valuation, and this does not require it. End quote. And he said in Jami' al-Mudmarat: A man bought for ten dirhams, and the principal refused to take it from him due to the deception he saw in it. So he presented the sold item to the valuators who have knowledge of valuing sold items. Some said: it is worth nine, and some said: it is worth ten. This falls under the valuation of the valuators, so it binds the principal. And if none of them valued it at ten, then this does not fall under the valuation of the valuators, and people do not overlook each other in its like, so the agent is liable, because he does not have the right to buy with excessive deception. End quote to the extent necessary. And al-Birjandi said: The conclusion is that the like of the value is what most valuators value it at, and what the fewest value it at, and what is in excess of what the majority valued is what people overlook each other in. If it is excessive to the extent that no one valued it, then it is something people do not overlook each other in. So there is leniency in the expression, and the intended meaning is what we mentioned. End quote. And he said in al-Hidaya: What people do not overlook each other in is what does not fall under the valuation of the valuators. And it is said: for goods, ten percent; for animals, eleven percent; for real estate, twelve percent; because transactions occur frequently in the first, rarely in the last, and moderately in the middle, and the frequency of deception is due to the rarity of transactions. End quote. So deception increases with lack of experience, and decreases with its frequency or lack, and its frequency is due to the scarcity or frequency of transactions. In the first category it is much, in the last it is little, and in the middle it is moderate. If deception reaches this amount it is trifling, so the matter binds; if it exceeds that, the agent is liable. Thus in al-Binaya.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a definition and analysis of the boundary between trifling (yasir) and excessive (fahish) ghubn in agency purchases. The commentary opens by clarifying that the agent is only authorized to bind the principal for a trifling increase over the market value, not an excessive one. The author then discusses varying opinions: some say the agent does not bear even trifling when the price is specified, while others maintain he does in all cases. The core definition, attributed to Imam Muhammad in al-Nawadir, is that trifling ghubn is whatever falls within the range of valuations by multiple expert valuators; if no valuator supports that price, it is excessive. Shaykh al-Islam Khwaharzada restricts this to items without a known market price; for staple goods like bread and meat, even a minimal increase does not bind the principal because valuators are not needed. The passage also illustrates an example: if valuators differ (e.g., nine vs. ten dirhams for a ten-dirham purchase), the purchase binds the principal because it falls within their range. Later, al-Birjandi refines the definition: the like value is what most valuators assign, and an increase up to what the minority assigns is tolerable; beyond that is excessive. Finally, al-Hidayah and al-Binaya provide percentage-based guidelines: 10% for goods, 11% for animals, 12% for real estate, reflecting the frequency of transactions and associated experience. The overall logic is that the agent's authority is limited to trifling disparities, and exceeding that makes the agent personally liable.

Key Terms
الغبن (al-ghubn): Deception or disparity between the price paid and the actual market value, classified as trifling (yasir) or excessive (fahish).
تقويم المقومين (taqwim al-muqawwimeen): Valuation by expert valuators; the range of their estimates determines if the ghubn is trifling.
اليسير (al-yasir): Trifling, tolerable deception within the range of valuators' assessments.
الفاحش (al-fahish): Excessive deception beyond the range of valuators' estimates, not binding on the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, what constitutes trifling ghubn (الغبن اليسير)?
A) Any increase up to 10% of the market price.
B) Any increase that falls within the range of valuations by expert valuators.
C) Any increase that is less than the agent's personal profit.
D) Any increase that the principal accepts after the fact.

Key Takeaway
Trifling ghubn is defined by the range of expert valuations, and the agent's purchase within that range binds the principal; excessive ghubn outside that range makes the agent personally liable.

Ruling on Buying Half of Commissioned Item Pending Purchase of Remainder
Explains suspension of partial purchase and intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an agent's partial purchase of a commissioned item and explain the intra-madhhab disagreement with Imam Zufar.

Core Idea
An agent's purchase of part of a commissioned item is suspended until the remainder is bought.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in agency (wakalah) where an agent buys only half of what he was commissioned to purchase. The Hanafi school holds that the purchase is suspended (tawaquf) and only becomes binding if the agent completes the purchase of the remaining half. We will explore the reasoning behind this ruling and the dissenting view of Imam Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
The three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree that the partial purchase is suspended until the remainder is bought. This is recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah.
The word 'by agreement' (بالاتفاق) in the text refers to the consensus of the three.
2
Understand the rationale
Buying part may be a means (wasila) to full compliance when the item is jointly owned and cannot be bought all at once.
The commentary gives the example of property owned by a group where buying a share is possible.
3
Examine Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that the first half was a violation because the agent did not complete the commission, so it belongs to the agent. The second half is bought to avoid co-ownership, thus also for himself.
Al-Birjandi in Sharh al-Niqaya records Zufar's view.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The majority view considers that the agent's intent is revealed by subsequent completion. If he buys the rest, the initial purchase was a means, not a violation.
The text also mentions a distinction between sale and purchase: in purchase there is suspicion (tuhmah) that the agent acted for himself.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The suspension view is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) because it is the majority view and forms part of Zahir al-Riwayah.
Zufar's view is a valid ikhtilaf within the school but is not the operative fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتَوَقفَ شراء نصف ما وكل بشرائه على شراء الباقي) هذا بالاتفاق"، والفرق" لأبي حنيفة له بين البيع والشراء أن في الشراء تهمة وهي أنه اشترى لنفسه ، ثُمَّ نَدِمَ فيلقيه على الموكل [1] قوله : وتوقف...الخ ؛ فإنّ شرى الباقي لزم النصف، وإن لم يشتره لم يلزمه ؛ لأن شراء البعض قد يقع وسيلة إلى الامتثال بأن يكون المشترى ملكاً لجماعة، ولا يتفق شراؤه جملة، ويتفق شراء حصة، فإن اشترى الوكيل قبل أن يرد الموكل شراءه البعض ظهر أنه وسيلة، فنصفه على الموكل. [٢] قوله : هذا بالاتفاق ؛ بين أئمتنا وهو عند الأئمة الثلاثةِ أيضاً، وأمّا عند زفر فيصير مشتريها كله لنفسه، إذ شراء النصفِ الأَوَّلِ وقعَ له بسبب المخالفة، وكذا النصف الثاني دفعاً لضرر الشركة ذكره البِرْجَنْدِيُّ في «شرح النقاية». And the purchase of half of what he was commissioned to buy is suspended upon the purchase of the remainder. This is by agreement. And the distinction for Abu Hanifa between sale and purchase is that in purchase there is suspicion: that he bought for himself, then regretted and throws it onto the principal. [1] His saying: 'And is suspended...' etc.: For if he buys the remainder, the half is binding; if he does not buy it, it is not binding, because buying part may occur as a means to compliance, such as when the purchased item is owned by a group and buying it all at once is not possible while buying a share is possible. If the agent bought before the principal repudiates his purchase of the part, it becomes clear that it was a means, so half is on the principal. [2] His saying: 'This is by agreement' among our imams, and it is also with the three imams. As for Zufar, he becomes the owner of all of it for himself, because the purchase of the first half occurred due to violation, and similarly the second half to avoid the harm of partnership. Al-Birjandi mentioned it in Sharh al-Niqaya. [3] His saying: 'And the distinction...' etc.: Means the distinction for Abu Hanifa between sale and purchase, where not...
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when an agent commissioned to buy a certain item buys only half of it. The main ruling is that the purchase of that half is suspended (tawaquf) pending the purchase of the remainder. If the agent later buys the rest, the half becomes binding on the principal; otherwise it does not. This is agreed upon by the three Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) and also by the founders of the other three schools. However, Zufar (a Hanafi dissenter) holds that the agent becomes owner of the entire item himself. His reasoning: the first half was a violation because the agent did not complete the commission, so it is his own property; the second half he buys to avoid co-ownership, so it also becomes his. The commentary explains the rationale for the majority view: buying part may be a means to fulfil the commission when the item is owned jointly and cannot be bought all at once. If the agent buys part and then the rest, it shows the initial partial purchase was intended as a step towards full compliance, so it binds the principal. Abu Hanifa further distinguishes between sale and purchase: in a sale, the agent's partial sale would not be suspended, but in a purchase there is a suspicion that the agent might have bought for himself and then changed his mind. The passage cuts off mid-sentence, but the key point is that partial purchase under commission is considered a means to an end, and thus contingent on completion.

Key Terms
التوقف (al-tawaquf): Suspension of the legal effect of a partial purchase until the remaining part is bought.
الوسيلة (al-waseelah): A means to an end; here, buying part of the commission is considered a means to full compliance.
التهمة (al-tuhmah): Suspicion; in purchase, there is suspicion that the agent acted for himself and later changed his mind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The purchase is void immediately because it violates the commission.
B) The purchase is binding on the principal from the start.
C) The purchase is suspended until the agent buys the remainder; if he does, it binds the principal.
D) The purchase is for the agent himself, and he must return the item.

Key Takeaway
An agent's partial purchase under wakalah is suspended and becomes binding only upon completion of the remainder, per the three imams.

Purchase of Half by Agent: Conditional on Completing the Whole
Explaining a ruling on agency for purchase of a partial share

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an agent's partial purchase of an authorized item and explain why suspicion (tuhma) distinguishes purchase from sale, and identify the threshold of tolerable price disparity (ghubn) that determines agent liability.

Core Idea
An agent's partial purchase binds the principal only if the whole is bought; suspicion in purchase prevents the agent from offloading a regretted deal.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya addresses a common agency scenario: an agent authorized to purchase an entire item buys only half. The Hanafi school holds that the half-purchase is conditional on subsequently buying the remainder, concluding that the suspicion (tuhma) inherent in purchase—that the agent may be acting for himself—necessitates this condition. In contrast, agency for sale is treated more leniently. The passage also defines what constitutes a tolerable price disparity (ghubn) that does not invalidate the transaction, establishing thresholds for different types of property.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its condition
Identify that if an agent authorized to buy a whole buys only half, the half-purchase is binding on the principal only if the remainder is also purchased; otherwise the agent is liable.
The condition is key: the principal is not bound until the remainder is purchased.
2
Understand the distinction between sale and purchase
Note that in sale, a partial sale is permissible without condition because there is no suspicion. In purchase, suspicion (tuhma) exists that the agent may have bought for himself and later regretted.
The text explicitly states: 'the distinction for Abu Hanifa between sale and purchase is that in purchase there is suspicion'.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Zufar holds that the agent becomes owner of the entire item because the first half was a violation and the second half was to avoid partnership harm.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the role of tuhma as the proof
Tuhma is a rational argument (dalil aqli) that justifies the condition; it protects the principal from the agent's potential fraud.
The condition prevents the agent from offloading a regretted purchase onto the principal.
5
Connect to ghubn thresholds
Understand that if the price disparity exceeds what people normally overlook, the agent is liable; the thresholds vary by property type (commodities, animals, real estate) based on frequency of transactions.
The passage from al-Hidayah provides examples: 1/10 for commodities, 1/12 for animals, etc.

Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on a ruling from the matn of al-Wiqaya regarding agency for purchase. The author explains that if an agent is authorized to purchase an item and only purchases half of it, that half-purchase is conditional upon also purchasing the remaining half. This ruling is agreed upon among the Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), although Zufar differs by holding that the entire purchase is for the agent himself due to violation of instructions. The reason for the distinction between sale and purchase (where in sale, selling half is permissible without such condition) is the element of suspicion (tuhma) in purchase: the agent may have bought for himself and then regretted it, so he attributes it to the principal. The commentary then delves into the concept of 'what people overlook' (ma la yataghābanu al-nās) regarding price disparity (ghubn). It cites al-Hidayah and al-Bina'ya to define the threshold of tolerable deficiency, mentioning different thresholds for commodities, animals, and real estate based on frequency of transactions. The central idea is that the agent's purchase of a part is not automatically binding on the principal unless it serves as a means to purchase the whole, and the test for binding is whether the price discrepancy is within tolerable limits.

Key Terms
وكالة (wakālah): Agency; a contract by which one person authorizes another to perform a legal act on his behalf.
تهمة (tuhma): Suspicion or presumption that the agent is acting for his own benefit rather than the principal's.
غبن (ghubn): Disparity or deficiency in price between the actual value and the agreed price; an excessive disparity that parties do not normally overlook.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason for distinguishing between sale and purchase in the ruling on partial execution?
A) Sale requires more expertise than purchase.
B) Purchase involves suspicion (tuhma) that the agent might be acting for himself.

D) Purchase has a higher risk of financial loss.

Key Takeaway
In agency for purchase, an agent's partial purchase binds the principal only as a means to complete the whole purchase, and price disparity beyond tolerable limits shifts liability to the agent.

Return of Defective Item to Agent: Evidence and Defect Type
Explaining when an agent can accept return of a defective item and pass it to the principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which an agent can return a defective item to the principal and identify which proof methods allow return depending on the defect type.

Core Idea
An agent's acknowledgment of a possible defect binds him personally, not the principal.

This passage from Hanafi fiqh of agency (wakala) and defects (ʿayb) clarifies the agent's duties when a sold item is returned due to a defect. The ruling distinguishes between defects that are impossible to have occurred after the sale and those that are possible, and also between different types of proof (bayyina, nukul, iqrar). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly assigning liability in such transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
The act is the return of a sold item to the agent by the purchaser due to a defect. The agent then wishes to pass this return to the principal. The ruling governs when the agent is allowed to do so.
Agent = wakil, principal = amir.
2
Distinguish defect types
Two categories: (1) Defects that cannot occur (e.g., extra finger) or cannot occur in the relevant period; (2) Defects that can occur after the sale.
This distinction determines whether the agent's own acknowledgment suffices.
3
Apply the rule for each defect type and proof method
For defects that cannot occur: agent returns to principal regardless of proof (bayyina, nukul, or iqrar). For defects that can occur: agent returns only if proof is bayyina or nukul; if agent himself acknowledges (iqrar), he cannot pass the return and is personally liable.
The rationale is suspicion of collusion or fault when the agent acknowledges a possible defect.
4
Understand the need for proofs even for impossible defects
The judge may need proof to ascertain the sale date or the defect's nature, especially if experts (women, doctors) are involved.
If the judge witnessed the sale and the defect is apparent, no proof is needed.
5
Note the sale vs. purchase agency asymmetry
For a purchase agent, statements are not readily accepted due to suspicion (tuhma); for a sale agent, statements are accepted. This is illustrated by the different rules on accepting the agent's claim about loss.
This is a separate but related principle in the passage.

And if a sold item is returned to the agent due to a defect that can occur or cannot occur like it, by evidence, or refusal to swear, or acknowledgement, he returns it to the principal, except an agent who acknowledges a defect that can occur like it, and he is liable for that. That is: the agent sold, then the item was returned to him due to a defect. If the defect is of a type that cannot occur, like an extra finger, or cannot occur in that period, he returns it to the principal whether the return to the agent was by evidence, refusal to swear, or acknowledgement. If the defect is of a type that can occur, then if the return to him was by evidence or refusal to swear, he returns it to the principal; but if it was by acknowledgement, he does not return it to the principal. The interpretation of requiring evidence, refusal to swear, or acknowledgement in the case of a defect that cannot occur is that the judge may know that this defect cannot occur within a month, but he is uncertain about the date of sale, so he needs one of these proofs; or the defect is known only by women or doctors, and the statement of a woman or doctor is proof for directing litigation but not for the return; so these proofs are needed for the return; but if the judge directly witnessed the sale and the defect was apparent, he does not need any of these. The sale of the agent is restricted, and his purchase is restricted; because suspicion is strong in the agent for purchase, that he might buy for himself, and then when he finds the price high, he attributes it to the principal, unlike sale. For this reason, if the agent for purchase says: I bought, took possession, and it perished in my hand, so bring the price, his statement is not accepted. But if the agent for sale says: I sold, took possession of the price, and it perished, his statement is accepted, as stated by al-Shumunni and others. [1] His saying: For if it is... etc.; its explanation: This defect is either not occurring, like an extra finger or extra tooth, or it is occurring but its like does not occur in that period, or its like does occur. If it is not occurring, the judge returns it without any proof from evidence, refusal to swear, or acknowledgement; and similarly if
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage deals with the scenario where a sold item is returned to an agent (wakil) due to a defect, and the agent then wishes to pass the return to his principal (amir). The ruling differentiates based on the nature of the defect and the means of proof. If the defect is of a type that cannot occur (e.g., an extra finger) or cannot occur in the short time since the sale, the agent may return it to the principal regardless of whether the proof is by evidence (bayyina), refusal to swear (nukul), or acknowledgement (iqrar). However, if the defect is of a type that can occur, then the agent may return it only if the proof is by evidence or refusal to swear; if the agent himself acknowledges the defect, he cannot pass the return to the principal, because his acknowledgement raises suspicion that he may be colluding or at fault. The commentary explains that the requirement for evidence, nukul, or iqrar in defects that cannot occur is due to the judge's possible uncertainty about the date of sale, or because only experts (women doctors) can detect the defect. The passage also reiterates the distinction between sale and purchase agency: in purchase, the agent's statement about the transaction is not readily accepted due to tuhma (suspicion), whereas in sale, his statement is accepted. This is illustrated by the rule that if the purchasing agent claims he bought and the item perished, he must provide proof; but the selling agent's claim of sale and loss of price is accepted on his word.

Key Terms
عيب (ʿayb): A defect in the sold item that gives the purchaser the right to return it (khiyar al-ʿayb).
بينة (bayyina): Evidence; typically the testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females in property cases.
نكول (nukūl): Refusal to swear an oath when required by the court; interpreted as an admission of the opponent's claim.
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment or confession by a party of a right against himself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The agent must return it to the principal regardless.
B) The agent cannot return it to the principal; he is liable himself.
C) The agent may return it only if he has witnesses.
D) The agent may return it only if the principal agrees.

Key Takeaway
An agent can only return a defective item to the principal if the defect is impossible or proven by external evidence; own acknowledgment of possible defects makes the agent personally liable.

Agent's Return of Defective Goods: Proof Requirements
Explains evidence required for agent to return defective goods to principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when an agent's return of a defective item to the principal is valid based on the type of proof (bayyinah, nukul, iqrar) and the judge's knowledge, and apply the Hanafi distinction between absolute and limited proofs.

Core Idea
A confession (iqrar) binds only the confessor; the agent cannot pass it to the principal without a judge's ruling.

When a buyer returns a defective item through an agent, the legal effect depends on the proof used. The Hanafi school distinguishes absolute proofs (bayyinah, nukul) from limited proofs (iqrar). This passage from Sharh al-Wiqayah explains the conditions under which the judge may return the item to the agent, and when the agent must personally bear the loss or litigate against the principal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and its default ruling
The act is the agent (wakil) returning the defective item (mabi') to the principal (muwakkil). The default ruling is that such a return is valid only if supported by proper proof (hujjah). Determine when proof is needed and when it is not.
The judge's personal knowledge can substitute for proof.
2
Distinguish between absolute and limited proofs
Bayyinah (witnesses) and nukul (refusal to swear) are absolute proofs (hujjah mutlaqah) and bind the principal. Iqrar (confession) is a limited proof (hujjah qasirah) that binds only the confessor (the agent). Understand how this distinction affects the return.
Confession alone cannot transfer liability to the principal without a judge's ruling.
3
Analyze the defect type and judge's knowledge
If the defect is newly occurring (hadith) but not typical for that period, the judge can return without proof if he knows the defect pre-existed. For typical defects or when the date is unknown, bayyinah or nukul is required. Iqrar alone is insufficient for return to the principal.
The judge's personal knowledge ('ilm) is a decisive factor.
4
Trace the effect of a judge's ruling on confession
When the buyer returns the item to the agent by confession with a judge's ruling, the judge coerces the agent, making the return a rescission (faskh) that binds both agent and principal. The agent can then litigate the principal. Without a judge, the return is an iqala (cancellation), a new sale that does not affect the principal.
The lack of mutual consent in a judge's ruling changes the nature of the transaction.
5
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may treat iqrar as binding on the principal in some cases, but the Hanafi school distinguishes based on the limited nature of confession. The practical result is that the agent may be stuck with the defective item unless he can produce absolute proof against the principal.
This distinction protects the principal from liability based solely on the agent's admission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(If the defect) was newly occurring, but something like it does not occur in such a period, the judge returns it without evidence or refusal to swear or confession, because he knows it was with the seller. The author only stipulated evidence, refusal to swear, or confession because the situation may be ambiguous to the judge, such as not knowing the date of sale, so he needs this proof to know that the date of sale is a month ago, so it becomes clear to the judge that this defect was in the seller's possession and he returns the sold item to him, or it was a defect known only to doctors and women. Their statement: 'A proof for directing litigation, not for return.' So if the judge witnessed the sale and the defect was apparent, he does not need any of these proofs. If the defect is something that occurs, the same ruling applies if there is evidence or refusal to swear, because evidence is absolute proof, and likewise refusal to swear is proof against him, so he returns it. Then in all these cases, the judge's return to the agent is a return to the principal. If the buyer returns it to the agent by confession with a judge's ruling, it is not a return to the principal, because confession is a limited proof and does not extend, unlike when the defect is not something that occurs. But the agent may litigate his principal and return it to him with evidence or refusal to swear. If the buyer returns it to the agent by his confession with his consent without a ruling, he cannot return it to the principal, because it is a cancellation (iqala), which is a new sale in relation to a third party, and the principal is their third. Unlike when he returns it to the agent by confession with a judge's ruling, because the return occurred by a ruling and he was coerced, so mutual consent was absent, which is a condition in financial transactions, so it is made a rescission for everyone. But the rescission is based on a limited proof, namely confession, so we act on both: from the perspective of return it is a rescission, and the agent may litigate the principal; from the perspective that it is based on a limited proof, it binds the agent unless he establishes a proof against the principal. If the defect is not new, or it is new but something like it occurs in that period, and he returns it to the agent by confession without a ruling, it binds the agent and he cannot litigate the principal in most narrations of al-Mabsut.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Wiqayah discusses the procedure for returning a defective item (mabi') when an agent (wakil) is involved. The key distinction is whether the defect existed before the sale (qadim) or arose after (hadith). The judge may return the item to the agent without evidence if he personally knows the defect was with the seller. However, when the date is uncertain, evidence (bayyinah), refusal to swear (nukul), or confession (iqrar) is required. The author explains that confession is a 'limited proof' (hujjah qasirah) that only binds the confessor, so if the buyer confesses to a defect and returns the item to the agent with a judge's ruling, the return binds the agent but not the principal. Yet the agent can then litigate the principal. If the return is by mutual consent without a judge (iqala), it is considered a new sale, so the agent cannot pass it to the principal. For defects that could have arisen after sale, evidence or refusal to swear is needed; confession alone is insufficient. The passage ends with a note from al-Mabsut that in most narrations, if the defect is not newly occurring and the agent accepts a return by confession without a judge, he cannot litigate the principal. This shows the Hanafi attention to the distinction between absolute and limited proofs and their effect on agency relationships.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakil): An agent authorized by the principal (muwakkil) to conduct a sale or purchase.
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints the agent (wakil).
عيب ('ayb): A defect in the sold item that existed before sale, entitling the buyer to return it.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females, establishing a fact.
نكول (nukul): The defendant's refusal to take an oath after the plaintiff demands it, treated as an admission in Hanafi law.
إقرار (iqrar): A confession or admission by a party, binding only on the confessor (hujjah qasirah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When the judge was not present at the sale
C) When the judge personally knows the defect existed before the sale
D) When the buyer confesses to the defect

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats confession as a limited proof that cannot by itself bind the principal; a judge's ruling is necessary to make the return effective against all parties.

When Principal and Agent Disagree: Cash or Credit?
Ruling on disputes about sale terms in agency and mudarabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for resolving disputes between a principal and agent about the terms of sale and distinguish it from the rule in mudarabah.

Core Idea
In agency, the principal is believed because the contract is built on restriction; in mudarabah, the mudarib is believed due to default generality.

Imagine you hire an agent to sell your car, and he sells it on credit, but you claim you instructed him to sell for cash. Who is believed? The Hanafi school provides a clear answer based on the nature of the agency contract. This lesson dissects that rule and contrasts it with the parallel rule in mudarabah (commenda), where the default is opposite.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
The agent sold on credit (nasī'ah). The principal claims he ordered cash (naqd); the agent claims he was given unrestricted authority (itlaq).
2
Apply the principle for agency disputes
Agency is built on restriction (taqyid); it is not valid without specifying type, genus, and price. Therefore, the principal's statement is taken with his oath.
The instruction comes from the principal, so he knows best what he ordered.
3
Contrast with mudarabah
In mudarabah, the default is generality (itlaq). Thus, if the mudarib claims he was allowed to sell on credit, his statement is accepted.
Mudarabah is inherently flexible; the mudarib is trusted with discretion.
4
Note the exception in mudarabah
If both parties agree that the mudarabah was restricted to a specific type, it reverts to agency rules, and the principal is believed.
Mutual agreement removes the default generality.

And in a narration of Kitab al-Buyu' from al-Asl, it binds the principal without litigation, because the return is determined, for they have done exactly what the judge would do if the matter were raised to him. For if they raised the matter to the judge regarding a defect that does not occur like it, he would return it to the agent, and would not burden him to establish proof for that, and that would be a return to the principal. The first view's reasoning is that the return was established by mutual consent, so it became like a new sale, and we do not concede that they did exactly what the judge would do; how can that be given the difference in authority? [1] His statement: 'If he sold...etc.'; meaning if the agent sold on credit for a term, and the principal said: 'I ordered you for cash,' and the agent said: 'No, I gave you generality and ordered you to sell without specifying cash,' the principal is believed with his oath, because agency is built upon restriction, so that it is not valid without specifying the type after the genus and the price. Do you not see that if he said: 'I appointed you to buy me an animal,' it is not valid; and if he said: 'I appointed you over my wealth,' he only has the right of preservation. So when it is built upon restriction and it is derived from the orderer's side, the statement is his. And if the mudarib sold on credit, and the capital owner said: 'I ordered you to sell it for cash,' and the mudarib said: 'No, I gave you generality,' the statement is the mudarib's, because the basis in mudarabah is generality and comprehensiveness, so his statement is considered with his oath. Unlike if the capital owner claimed mudarabah in one type and the mudarib in another type, where the statement is the capital owner's, because generality is removed by their mutual agreement, so it descends to pure agency (2). [2] His statement: 'And it is not valid...etc.'; meaning if a man appointed two agents, the action of one of them alone is not permissible.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Wiqayah contains two distinct rulings under the Chapter of Agency. The first ruling (قوله: فإن باع...) deals with a dispute between the principal (muwakkil) and agent (wakil) regarding the terms of sale. If the agent sold on credit (nasī'ah) and the principal claims he ordered cash (naqd), while the agent claims he was given unrestricted authority (itlaq), the principal is believed on oath. The reason is that agency is fundamentally based on restriction (taqyid); it is not valid unless the type, genus, and price are specified. Therefore, the principal's statement takes precedence. In contrast, in a mudarabah (commenda) contract, the mudarib is believed if he claims unrestricted authority, because the default in mudarabah is generality. However, if both parties agree that some restriction existed, the principal is believed because it reverts to pure agency. The second ruling (قوله: ولا يصح...) states that if a person appoints two agents jointly, neither can act alone in the matter of agency. A minority narration from Kitab al-Buyu' of al-Asl (the foundational text) holds that if both agents together return a defective item, it binds the principal without further litigation, akin to a judge's ruling. But the preferred view is that such a consensual return is like a new sale (iqala) and does not automatically bind the principal because there is a difference in authority between agents and a judge.

Key Terms
نسيئة (nasī'ah): A credit sale where payment is deferred to a specified future date.
نقد (naqd): Cash payment, immediate exchange of goods for money.
إطلاق (itlaq): Unrestricted authority; absence of specific conditions or terms.
تقييد (taqyid): Restriction; specifying conditions or terms in the agency contract.
وكيلان (wakilān): Two agents appointed jointly for the same task.
مضارب (mudarib): The managing partner in a mudarabah (commenda) contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute between a principal and an agent about whether the sale was for cash or credit, who is believed in Hanafi fiqh?
A) The agent, because he performed the sale
B) The principal, because agency is based on restriction
C) The buyer, as a third party
D) Both are equally believed and the matter goes to arbitration

Key Takeaway
In agency, the principal is believed in disputes over sale terms because the contract requires restriction; in mudarabah, the mudarib is believed due to default generality.

Two Agents, One Action: The Hanafi Rule of Joint Agency
Ruling on the prohibition of sole action by two joint agents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that two joint agents cannot act alone and evaluate the minority narration that says otherwise.

Core Idea
Two joint agents must act together; a sole action is invalid because agency is restrictive and requires specification.

When a person appoints two agents for the same task, can one of them validly act alone? The majority Hanafi view says no, but a minority narration from al-Asl suggests otherwise. This lesson clarifies the relied-upon position and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
Two agents appointed jointly cannot act alone; any sole action is invalid.
2
Understand the reason
Agency is built on restriction, and the principal's intent is to have both agents act together. Allowing one to act alone violates that intent.
3
Examine the minority narration
A narration from Kitab al-Buyu' of al-Asl states that if both agents together return a defective item, it binds the principal like a judicial ruling.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Analyze the majority response
The majority respond that the return was based on mutual consent, not judicial authority. It is a new sale (iqala), not an automatic binding.
Agents are not judges; their authority is limited.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that two agents cannot act alone; the minority narration is not operative in fatwa.

Whose Word is Taken: Disputes over Cash or Credit Sale in Wakalah and Mudarabah
Ruling on disputes over cash/credit sale terms in agency and mudarabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the Hanafi rule for whose word is taken in a dispute over cash or credit sale in wakalah and mudarabah, and explain the underlying reasoning based on the default nature of each contract.

Core Idea
In wakalah the principal's word prevails; in mudarabah the agent's word prevails — each follows the contract's default.

When an agent or mudarib sells goods on credit, a dispute may arise: the principal claims he ordered a cash sale, while the agent claims he was given absolute permission. How does the Hanafi school resolve this? The answer depends on the nature of the contract — agency is built on restriction, so the principal's word is taken; mudarabah is built on absolute permission, so the mudarib's word is taken. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contracts
The passage discusses two distinct legal relationships: wakalah (agency) and mudarabah (silent partnership). Note the different default natures: agency requires specification, mudarabah defaults to absolute permission.
2
State the dispute in each case
In both, the principal claims he ordered a cash sale; the agent/mudarib claims he was given absolute permission (itlaq). The dispute is about the terms of the sale: cash vs. credit.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule
In wakalah, believe the principal; in mudarabah, believe the mudarib. This is the mufta bihi position based on the nature of each contract.
4
Understand the reasoning
Agency is built on taqyeed (restriction); it is invalid without specifying details. Therefore, the principal's claim of restriction is consistent. Mudarabah defaults to itlaq (absolute), so the mudarib's claim is consistent.
The exception occurs when both agree on a specific type but differ on which type — then the principal's word prevails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن باعَ نساءً؛ فقال :آمره : أمرتُكَ بنقد، وقال الوكيل : أطلقت صدق الأمر وفي المضاربة المضارب، ( فإن باع" نساء؛ فقال :آمره : أمرتُكَ بنقد، وقال الوكيل : أطلقت، صدق الآمر، وفي المضارية المضارب)؛ لأنَّ الأمر يستفاد من الأمر، فالقول له، أما المضاربة فالظاهر فيها الإطلاق، فالقول للمضارب. [١]قوله : فإن باع ...الخ ؛ يعني إن باغ الوكيل بالبيع نساء إلى أجل ، وقال الموكل : أمرتك بالنقد، وقال الوكيل : لا ؛ بل أطلقت، وأمرتني بالبيع من غير تقييد بالنقدِ صُدِّقَ الموكَّلُ مع اليمين، فإنَّ مبنى الوكالة على التقييد حتى لا تصح بدون بيان النوع بعد الجنس والثمن، ألا ترى أنه لو قال : وَكُلْتُك أن تشتري لي دابة لا تصح ، ولو قال : وكلتك في مالي ليس له إلا الحفظ، فإذا كان مبناها على التقييد وهو يستفاد من جهة الأمر، كان القول قوله. وإن باع المضارب نساءً، فقال ربّ المال : أمرتُك أن تبيعه بالنقد، وقال المضارب لا بل أطلقت كان القول للمضارب ؛ لأنَّ الأصل في المضاربة الإطلاق والعموم، فيعتبر قولـه مـع اليمين، بخلاف ما إذا ادعى رب المال المضاربة في نوع ، والمضارب في نوع آخر، حيث يكون القولُ لرب المال ؛ لأنه سقط الإطلاق فيه بتصادقهما، فنزل إلى الوكالة المحضة (٢).
If he sold on credit, and the principal said: 'I ordered you for cash, and the agent said: 'I gave absolute permission', the principal is believed, and in mudarabah the mudarib is believed. (If he sold on credit, and the principal said: 'I ordered you for cash', and the agent said: 'I gave absolute permission', the principal is believed, and in mudarabah the mudarib is believed) because the command is derived from the commander, so his word is taken. As for mudarabah, the apparent is that it is absolute, so the word of the mudarib is taken. [1] His saying: 'For if he sold...' etc. means if the agent sold on credit to a term, and the principal said: 'I ordered you for cash', and the agent said: 'No, rather I gave absolute permission and ordered me to sell without restriction to cash', the principal is believed with an oath, because the foundation of agency is restriction, to the point that it is not valid without specifying the type after the genus and the price. Do you not see that if he said: 'I appointed you to buy a beast for me' it is not valid, and if he said: 'I appointed you over my wealth' he has nothing except preservation. So when its foundation is restriction, and it is derived from the commander, his word is taken. And if the mudarib sold on credit, and the owner of capital said: 'I ordered you to sell it for cash', and the mudarib said: 'No, rather I gave absolute permission', the word of the mudarib is taken, because the default in mudarabah is absolute and general, so his statement is considered with an oath, unlike if the owner claimed mudarabah in one type and the mudarib in another, where the word of the owner is taken because the absolute permission is removed due to their agreement, so it descends to pure agency (2).
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on a dispute between the principal and agent regarding the terms of sale. The text addresses two contexts: ordinary agency (wakalah) and investment partnership (mudarabah). In wakalah, when the principal claims he ordered a cash sale and the agent claims he gave absolute permission, the principal's word is taken. The reason is that agency is built upon restriction and specification — it is not valid without clearly stating the type of transaction after specifying the commodity and price. For example, saying 'I appoint you to buy a beast' is invalid because it is too vague. Since the agent receives authority from the principal, the principal's statement prevails. In mudarabah, however, the default is absolute generality (itlaq). Therefore, if the capital owner claims he restricted to cash and the mudarib claims absolute permission, the mudarib's word is taken. An exception occurs if both agree that the mudarabah was in a specific type but differ on which type, then the owner's word prevails because the absolute nature is lost and it becomes like a restricted agency.

Key Terms
نساء (nasi'ah): A sale with deferred payment, credit sale.
وكالة (wakalah): Agency; a contract where one person appoints another to act on his behalf.
مضاربة (mudarabah): A partnership where one provides capital and the other labor; profits shared according to agreement.
إطلاق (itlaq): Absolute or unrestricted permission, the default in mudarabah.
تقييد (taqyeed): Restriction; the default in wakalah requires specification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The agent's word
B) The principal's word
C) Both are equally believed
D) The buyer's word

Key Takeaway
The resolution of disputes over sale terms depends on the contract's default: in wakalah the principal prevails; in mudarabah the mudarib prevails.

Invalidity of Transactions by Slave, Mukatab, or Dhimmi for Muslim Minor's Property
Ruling with proof: incapacity for certain persons to manage a Muslim minor's property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling and identify the legal capacity required for guardianship over a Muslim minor's property.

Core Idea
A slave, mukatab, or non-Muslim cannot act as guardian over a Muslim minor's property; the Quran denies them capacity.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah explains a key Hanafi rule: certain persons (slaves, mukatabs, and non-Muslims) lack the legal capacity (wilayah) to handle a Muslim minor's property. The commentator provides Quranic evidence and also distinguishes the apostate, whose status is suspended. Understanding this rule is essential for property management in Islamic finance and family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Who is prohibited from transacting (selling or buying) for a Muslim minor's property? The text lists three persons: a slave, a mukatab, and a dhimmi (and by extension any non-Muslim).
The ruling also applies to a harbi, but the text mentions 'dhimmi' to exclude the apostate.
2
Understand the reasoning
Why do these persons lack capacity? A slave (including a mukatab) has no power over anything (Quran 16:75). A non-Muslim cannot have any authority over a believer (Quran 4:141).
The mukatab is still a slave until fully freed.
3
Analyze the Quranic proofs
Verse 16:75 provides a direct example that a slave lacks power, so he cannot exercise guardianship. Verse 4:141 establishes a permanent principle: non-Muslims cannot have 'sabil' (way/authority) over believers.
Both verses are qati al-thubut and qati al-dalala, forming clear proof.
4
Distinguish the apostate case
The apostate (murtadd) is different: his guardianship is suspended. If he returns to Islam, his past transactions are validated retroactively; if he dies on apostasy, they become void ab initio.
This suspension is because apostasy does not immediately cut off guardianship; the possibility of return exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يصح بيع عبد أو مكاتب أو ذمي في مال صغيره المسلم وشراؤه) : أي الشراء بماله، فالحاصل أن العبد والمكاتب لا ولاية لهما في مال ولده الصغير، والكافر لا ولاية له في مال صغيره المسلم. [١] قوله: ولا يصح ...الخ؛ يعني لا يجوز لعبد ولا مكاتب أن يتصرف في مال طفله ببيع أو شراء، ولا يجوز للكافر في حق طفله المسلم؛ لأن المكاتب عبد ما بقي عليه درهم، والعبد لا ولاية له، قال الله: ضَرَبَ اللَّهُ مَثَلًا عَبْدًا مَمْلُوكًا لَّا يَقْدِرُ عَلَى شَيْءٍ (النحل: ٧٥) والكافر لا ولاية له على المسلم، قال الله: وَلَن يَجْعَلَ اللَّهُ لِلْكَفِرِينَ عَلَى الْمُؤْمِنِينَ سَبِيلًا (النساء: ١٤١) وقيد بالذمي لا للاحتراز عن الحربي، فإنه في هذا الحكم مثل الذمي، بل عن المرتد، فإن ولايته على أولاده وأموالهم موقوفة، فإن أسلم جعل كأنه لم يزل مسلما، فينفذ تصرفه، وإن مات أو قتل على ردته تبطل لتقرر جهة انقطاع الولاية. كما صرحوا به. [٢] قوله: أي الشراء بماله؛ أشار إلى أن المراد بالشراء وهو الشراء بمال صغيره لا أن يشتري بمال نفسه، فإنه يجوز وينفذ عليه.
(And the sale or purchase by a slave, a mukatab, or a dhimmi in the property of his Muslim minor child is not valid) Meaning: the purchase from his property. The summary is that the slave and the mukatab have no guardianship over the property of their minor child, and the disbeliever has no guardianship over the property of his Muslim minor child. [1] His saying: 'And it is not valid etc.' means it is not permissible for a slave or a mukatab to dispose of his child's property by sale or purchase, nor for a disbeliever regarding his Muslim child, because the mukatab is a slave as long as a dirham remains due from him, and the slave has no guardianship. Allah said: 'Allah sets forth an example: a slave owned who has power over nothing' (an-Nahl 75). And the disbeliever has no guardianship over the Muslim, Allah said: 'And Allah will never give the disbelievers a way over the believers' (an-Nisa 141). It was restricted to dhimmi not to exclude the harbi, for he is like the dhimmi in this ruling, but rather to exclude the apostate, for his guardianship over his children and their properties is suspended. If he returns to Islam, it becomes as if he never left Islam, and his transaction is effective. If he dies or is killed on his apostasy, it becomes void because the basis of guardianship is cut off. As they have stated. [2] His saying: 'That is, the purchase from his property' indicates that what is meant by purchase is the purchase from the property of his minor child, not that he purchases with his own money, for that is permissible and effective upon him.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqayah regarding the invalidity of a sale or purchase made by a slave, a mukatab, or a dhimmi concerning the property of their Muslim minor child. The commentator explains that 'purchase' here means using the child's money, not the adult's own money (which would be valid). The legal reasoning: a slave lacks legal capacity and guardianship, supported by Quran 16:75. A mukatab (a slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom) is still a slave until he pays the last dirham, so he too has no guardianship. A non-Muslim (dhimmi or harbi) cannot have guardianship over a Muslim minor, based on Quran 4:141, which denies non-Muslims a way over believers. The text specifies 'dhimmi' not to exclude the harbi (who is the same in this ruling) but to exclude the apostate (murtadd): his guardianship over his children is suspended; if he returns to Islam, his past transactions are validated retroactively; if he dies or is killed on apostasy, they become void because his guardianship is permanently cut off.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract of kitabah with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
ولاية (wilayah): Legal guardianship or authority to manage a minor's property.
مرتد (murtadd): An apostate whose guardianship over Muslim children is suspended; its validity depends on whether he returns to Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a mukatab lack guardianship over his Muslim child's property?
A) Because he is a non-Muslim.
B) Because he is still a slave until the full kitabah price is paid.
C) Because he has not reached puberty.
D) Because he is absent.

Key Takeaway
A slave, mukatab, or non-Muslim cannot act as guardian over a Muslim minor's property; the Quran provides definitive proof.

intermediate
8 min
Invalidity of Slave, Mukatab, and Dhimmi Transactions in Minor's Property
Ruling with proof that slave and non-Muslim lack guardianship over minor Muslim's property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that transactions by a slave, mukatab, or dhimmi involving a Muslim minor's property are invalid, and identify the Quranic proofs establishing the prerequisites of freedom and Islam for guardianship (wilayah).

Core Idea
Guardianship over a Muslim minor's property requires freedom and Islam; without these, transactions are invalid.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, we examine a specific ruling about who can legally dispose of a Muslim minor's property. The text addresses scenarios where the guardian is a slave, a mukatab (a slave under contract for manumission), or a dhimmi (a non-Muslim protected citizen). It declares such transactions invalid and supports this by Quranic verses that establish the incapacity of slaves and the prohibition of non-Muslims having authority over Muslims. The commentary also clarifies an important exception: if the person buys with his own property, the transaction is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and transaction
Note the three types of individuals: a slave (abd), a mukatab (slave contracting freedom), and a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject). The transaction is buying or selling in the property of his minor Muslim child.
2
Understand the ruling
The matn clearly states that such transactions are not valid (لا يصح). The hukm is that the transaction is batil (void) because it lacks valid guardianship.
3
Recognize the legal reason: lack of wilayah
The commentary explains that slaves and mukatabs have no guardianship (wilayah) over their children's property. A mukatab is considered a slave as long as any dirham remains unpaid. A non-Muslim has no guardianship over a Muslim child.
Wilayah requires two conditions: freedom and Islam.
4
Examine the Quranic proofs
Two verses are cited: Surah An-Nahl 16:75 describes a slave as unable to do anything, proving incapacity. Surah An-Nisa 4:141 states that Allah will never give the disbelievers a way over the believers, proving that a non-Muslim cannot have authority over a Muslim.
The proofs are qat'i in transmission but zanni in interpretation, establishing the principle that these individuals lack the necessary legal capacity for guardianship.
5
Note the exception and clarification
The restriction to 'dhimmi' is not to exclude a harbi (who is the same) but to exclude an apostate (murtad), whose transactions are suspended pending his return to Islam. Additionally, the prohibition is specifically about transactions using the minor's property; if the slave, mukatab, or dhimmi buys with his own property, the transaction is valid.
This distinction is crucial: the source of funds matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يصح بيع عبد أو مكاتب أو ذمي في مال صغيره المسلم وشراؤه) : أي الشّراءُ بماله ) ، فالحاصل أنَّ العبد والمكاتب لا ولاية لهما في مال ولده الصغير، والكافر لا ولاية له في مال صغيره المسلم. [١] قوله : ولا يصح ...الخ ؛ يعني لا يجوز لعبد ولا مكاتب أن يتصرف في مال طفله ببيع أو شراء ، ولا يجوز للكافر في حق طفله المسلم ؛ لأنَّ المكاتب عبد ما بقي عليه درهم، والعبد لا ولاية له ، قال الله الله : ضَرَبَ اللَّهُ مَثَلًا عَبْدًا مَمْلُوكًا لَّا يَقْدِرُ عَلَى شَيْءٍ والكافر لا ولاية له على المسلم، قال الله : لم وَلَن يَجْعَلَ اللهُ لِلْكَفِرِينَ عَلَى الْمُؤْمِنِينَ سَبِيلًا ) (۲) ، وقيد بالذمي لا للاحتراز عن الحربي، فإنّه في هذا الحكم مثلُ الذمي، بل عن المرتد، فإنَّ ولايته على أولاده وأموالهم موقوفة، فإن أسلم جعل كأنه لم يزل مسلماً، فينفذ تصرفه، وإن مات أو قتل على ردَّتِهِ تبطل لتقرر جهة انقطاع الولاية. كما صرحوا به. [٢] قوله : أي الشراء بماله ؛ أشار إلى أن المراد بالشراء وهو الشراء بمال صغيره لا أن يشتري بمال نفسه، فإنّه يجوز وينفذ عليه. واجي
And it is not valid for a slave, a mukatib, or a dhimmi to sell or buy in the property of his minor Muslim child. That is, purchasing with his property. So the conclusion is that the slave and the mukatib have no guardianship over the property of their minor child, and the disbeliever has no guardianship over his minor Muslim child. [1] His statement: 'nor is it valid... etc.' means that it is not permissible for a slave or a mukatib to dispose of his child's property by selling or buying, nor is it permissible for a disbeliever regarding his Muslim child because the mukatib is a slave as long as a dirham remains upon him, and the slave has no guardianship. Allah said: 'Allah sets forth an example: a slave owned, unable to do anything.' (16:75) And the disbeliever has no guardianship over the Muslim. Allah said: 'And Allah will never give the disbelievers a way over the believers.' (4:141) And he restricted it to dhimmi not to exclude the harbi, for in this ruling he is like the dhimmi; rather to exclude the apostate, for his guardianship over his children and their property is suspended: if he embraces Islam, it is as if he never ceased being Muslim, so his transaction is effective; but if he dies or is killed upon his apostasy, it becomes void due to the establishment of the termination of guardianship, as they clarified. [2] His statement: 'i.e., purchasing with his property' indicates that what is meant by buying is buying with his minor's property, not buying with his own property, for that is valid and binding upon him.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Quran. The matn states that it is not valid for a slave, a mukatib (a slave contracted for manumission), or a dhimmi (a non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state) to engage in buying or selling in the property of his minor Muslim child. The commentary explains that the underlying reason is that these individuals lack guardianship (wilaya) over the minor's property. A slave has no legal authority because he himself is owned. A mukatib is considered a slave as long as any part of his freedom price remains unpaid. A non-Muslim has no authority over a Muslim, based on the Quranic verse 'Allah will never give the disbelievers a way over the believers' (4:141). The restriction to 'dhimmi' is not meant to exclude a harbi (enemy combatant), who is the same in this ruling, but rather to exclude an apostate (murtad), whose guardianship is suspended — if he returns to Islam, his transactions are validated retroactively; if he dies or is killed in apostasy, they are void. The commentary also clarifies that the prohibition is specifically about buying with the minor's property; if the slave, mukatib, or dhimmi buys with his own property, it is valid and binding on him. This is an important distinction: the ruling concerns transactions using the minor's wealth, not the guardian's own money. The overall lesson is that guardianship over a Muslim minor's property requires both legal capacity (freedom) and religious alignment (Islam).

Key Terms
ولاية (wilayah): Legal authority to manage the property of a minor, which requires legal capacity and Islam.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitaba) with his master, paying installments for his freedom. He remains a slave as long as any amount is unpaid and thus lacks guardianship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, why is a slave not allowed to buy or sell in the property of his minor child?
A) Because a slave has no wealth of his own.
B) Because a slave lacks legal authority (wilayah) due to being owned.
C) Because the minor is Muslim and the slave might be non-Muslim.
D) Because the transaction requires the child's consent.

Key Takeaway
A slave, mukatab, or non-Muslim cannot validly transact with a Muslim minor's property due to lack of guardianship; Islam and freedom are prerequisites for wilayah.
